FOR OMCIAL USE ONLY
DSFXMTIONS
Crude Birth Rate               Number of live births per year per 1,000
population.
Crude Death Rate               Number of deaths per year per 1,000.
Natural Increase Rate          Difference between crude birth and crude death
rates; usually expressed as a percentage.
Population Growth Rate         Rate of natural increase adjusted for (net)
migration, expressed as a percentage of the
total population in a given year.
Contraceptive Prevalence       The percentage of married women of reproductive
Rate                           age who are using a modern method of
contraception at any time.
Total Fertility Rate           The average number of live children that would
be born per women if she were to live to the end
of her childbearing years and bear children
according to a given set of age-specific
fertility rates. The Total Fertility Rate often
serves as an estimate of the average number of
children per family.
Net Reproduction Rate          The number of live-born daughters a cohort of
females would bear under a given fertility
schedule and a given set of survival
probabilities, from birth to the end of the
childbearing years.
Perinatal Mortality            Mortality related to the period between 28 weeks
gestation and one week postnatal.
Infant Mortality Rate          Annual number of deaths of infants under one
year per 1,000 live births during the same year.
Child Mortality Rate           Annual deaths of children 1-4 years of age per
1,000 children in the same age group.
Materna Mortality Rate         Number of maternal deaths per 1,000 births
attributable to pregnancy, childbearing, or
puerperal complications (i.e., within six weeks
following childbirth).
Morbidity                      Any departure, subjective or objective, fromt a
state of physiological or psychological well-
being. In this sense, sickness, illness, and
morbid condition are synonymous.
Morbidity Rate                 The frequency of disease and illness in a
population.

DEFINITIONS (continued)
Prevalence Rate                The number of persons having a particular
disease at a given point in time per population
at risk; usually expressed per 1,000 persons per
year.
Life Expectancy                Average number o! years expected to be lived by
children born in a given year if mortality rates
for each age/sex group remain the same in the
future.
Low Birth Weight (LBV)         Infants weight at birth less than 2,500 gr.  LBW
may be associated with either pre-term (less
than 37 weeks gestation) or full-term but small
for dates (38 weeks or more) of gestation.
Toxemia of Pregnancy           Group of metabolic disturbances occurring during
gestation or shortly after delivery,
characterized by the appearance of hypertension,
edema, and proteinuria (preeclampsia) and, in
severe case, convulsions and coma (eclampsia).
Puerperium                     The period after completion of the third stage
of labor until involution of the uterus is
complete, usually six weeks.
Puerperal sepsis               Infection of the reproductive organs caused by
septic childbirth conditions (also called
puerperal fever).
Risk                           A probability that an unfavorable outcome
related to morbidity or mortality will occur
within a stated period of time or age.
Dependency Ratio               Population 14 years or under and 65 years of age
or over as percentage of active population (aged
15 to 64 years). lndicates proportion of
population that needs economic support.

INDIA
CBILD SURVIVAL AND SAfl NOTERUHOOD PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMHARY
Borrovers                   India, acting by its President
Beneficiaries:             States and Union Territories of India
Amount:                     SDR 160.9 million (US$214.5 million equivalent)
Tt -s:                      Standard, with 35 years maturity
On-lending Terms:          Government of India (GOI) to the States and Union
Territories of India: In accordance with standard
arrangements for development assistance to States and
Union Territoriea.
Description:                The project would support enhancement and expansion of
COI's Maternal and Child Health (MCH) Program for the
1991-1995 period. It would cover the incremental
costs associated with the program. It would be na-
tional in scope, but with an emphasis on specific
districts where maternal and infant mortality rates
are higher than the national average. It would be co-
financed by UNICEF and take into account its experi-
ence in assisting the GOI in implementing the Univer-
sal Imunnization Program (UIP) during the Seventh
Five-Year Plan (1985-90). Its specific objectives
would be to enhance child survival, prevent maternal
mortality and morbidity, and increase the effective-
ness of service delivery. These objectives would be
achieved through supporting: (a) Child Survival Pro-
grams including: sustaining UIP as well as strengthen-
ing diarrhoea control programs, the control of acute
respiratory infections (ARI), prophylaxis against
blindness and eye lesions due to Vitamin A deficiency,
enhanced newborn care and the active promotion of
breast feeding. These interventions will be called
"UIP Plus;" (b) a Safe Motherhood Initiative which
would improve maternity care and preventive programs
for all women, giving enhanced emphasis to pregnant
women with high-risk factors, strengthening essential
obstetric care and capabilities to treat obstetric
complications, while concurrently promoting birth
spacing, timing, post partum counselling, and prophy-
laxis and control of nutritional anemia; and (c)
Institutional Systems DeveloRment which would include
improving and expanding training programs for family
welfare workers, expanding information, education and
conmunications dissemination, and strengthening exist-
ing center and state/district management information,
supervision, planning, procurement, logistics, and
maintenance systems.



-viii-
Benefits and Riskas        The project would have several immediate benefits.
First, in the absence of sustaining the immunization
program, another 250,000 children would die from
tetanus, 500,000 would die from measles and 170,000
would risk paralysis each year due to poliomyelitis.
Second, the project would assist in subtaining and
developing MCH services during a period when the scale
of publicly financed social services could be severely
and negatively impacted by GOI budgetary constraints.
Third, the project wo: ld contribute to a GOI review of
its current national MCH policies and to appropriate
adjustments in terms of content, targeting and deliv-
ery. Fourth, the project allows GOI the time and
opportunity to undertake vigorous trials at the dis-
trict level of the OUIP Plusw and Safe Motherhood
models which are being developed. It is estimated
that about 115 million lower-income women and about
190 million children in rural areas and urban slums
would benefit from this project. The long-term bene-
fit of the project is that it should promote a more
efficient and effective national family welfare pro-
gram which would lead to significant reductions of ma-
ternal, infant and young child morbidity, mortality,
and disability, thereby enhancing the quality of life
and of India's human capital.
There are some risks to the project. It will be
difficult, first, to ii.crease the number of activities
to be undertaken at the point of service delivery as
well as change the way health workers and supervisors
perform their tasks. However, ongoing IDA support to
the five Populaticn projects should alleviate this
risk by strengthening regular on-the-job training and
more appropriate supervision. In addition, GOI under
the proposed project would implement improved job
descriptions and work routines for staff and supervi-
sors. The large-scale cooperations between IDA and
UNICEF will also assist in reducing the constraints to
successful project implementation. UNICEF has exten-
sive contacts with the medical community in India and
a wide network of offices in the States. This should
assist in project monitoring, supervision and evalua-
tion.   There are also risks associated with the Safe
Motherhood Program. The project would include activ-
ities to increase awareness and action through Pancha-
yats, and Vonen's Groups including Mahilla Mandals and
Private Voluntary Organizations. Moreover, IEC pro-
grams would be designed to specifically address some
local customs which are dangerous to women's survival.
Also, local communities, through their representatives
and/or with the assistance of PVOs, would be mobilized
as an integral part of the planning for Safe Mother-
hood in that community.

DIA
CHILD SURVIVAL A*MD SAYN SOYNKUNOOD PROJECT
I. AMNILY WEZLARE IN INDA
A. Introduction
1.01      The proposed project is a critical part of IDA'" strategy of
supptorting human capital development and poverty alleviation in India. It
provides IDA with the opportunity to extend rapid but targeted asoistance to
the most vulnerable groups, about 115 million poor women and 190 million
children, who are at risk of falling through the social safety net during a
period of severe tudgetary constraints on publicly-financed social programs
(Annex 1). The special features of the project are that iL would: (a) assist
the GovArnment of India (GOI) to maintain coverage of vaccine-preventable
diseases at least to levels achieved at the end of the Seventh Five-Year Plan
pewiod (1989/90), as well as to expand the provision of servicss to previously
unserved areas and beneficiaries; (b) act as a coherent vehicl- to launch a
major initiative in Safe Motherhood to reduce maternal mortality and morbidi-
ty; (c) use the Universal Immunization Program (UIP) as an organizing frame-
work to deliver more efficiently, several critical public health interventions
including diarrhoea management, the control of acute respiratory infections
(ARI), Vitamin A supplementation, newborn care and breast feeding combined
into a technical package called UIP 'Plus:" (d) increase support to private
sector, non-governmental organizations and communities to deliver maternal and
child health (HCH) services; and (e) represent the first large scale coopera-
tion between IDA and UNICEF in India and builds- upon the last five years of
UNICEF/GOI experience with UIP. These aspects of the proposed project will
not only enbance policy dialogue, monitoring, supervision and evaluation of
the national program but will lead to the more effective delivery of essential
health services resulting in decreased rates of infant, young child and
maternal mortality and morbidity.
B. The National Fawfly Welfare Program
1.02      The Indian Family Welfare Program was established in 1951 and is one
of the most extensive programs of its type in the world. The program is
managed by the ilinistry of Health and Family Welfare (HOHFW) and provides
family planning tiP) and maternal and child health (MCH) services to reduce
morbAdity and mortality among mothers and children and to reduce fertility.
Since its inception, the program has contributed to reducing the infant
mortality rate from about 135 per 1,000 live births in the early 1970s to
about 93 in 1989, and contributed to the decline in fertility from a total
fertility rate (TFR) of almost 6.0 in the 1960s to 3.9 in 1988, largely the
result of increases in contraceptive prevalence to 421 effective prevalence in
1989 although there remain widespread regional variations (Annex 1). Despite
its successes, there is a consensus that the program today ?aces four major
challengess (a) how to improve access to services, particularly to disadvan-
taged areas; (b) how to improve the quality of services; (c) how to improve
the efficiency of services, especially the productivity of workers! and (d)
how to maximize impact by increasing the provision of temporary family
planning methods to families at the beginning of their childbearing years
since the highest risk of infant death is observed for fourth and higher
parity births among women in their forties, followed by first order births



- 2 -
among teenagers. Towards these aims, the Government of India (GOI) is
promoting a Revised Family Welfare Strategy that aims to make the program more
responsive to the segmented market for the provision of family welfare servic-
es. The Health System infrastructure, including a description of the organi-
zation of health services, is outlined in Annez 2.
1.03      The term *family welfare, covers two distinct but interrelated parts
of the current program: Family Planning 'FP) and Maternal and Child Health
(MCH). The policy of the program is to promote family planning on a voluntary
basis through the independent choice of the family planning method which is
best suited for the acceptor, and maternal and child health through immuniza-
tion and other prophylactic interventions, ante- and post-natal care and
nutritional awareness. In order to convey messages of the small family norm
and maternal and child health, motivational, educational and persuasive
methods including financial incentives are made without resort to any form of
coercion. The goals and achievements of the National Family Welfare Program
are outlined in Annex 3.
C. Performance of the Family Welfare Program.
1.04      India's Family Welfare Program has grown rapidly during the Sixth
and Seventh Five-Year Plan periods (1980-1990). Progress in the construction
and upgrading of facilities as well as the training and posting of additional
staff has been satisfactory, and the key Seventh Five-Year Plan goal of one
female health worker for 5,000 population has virtually been achieved.
Improvement in the availability of services has resulted in steady growth in
the performance of the program: over 402 of the eligible couples in India
currently use some form of modern contraception, and immunization coverage of
both pregnant women and children has improved substantially.
1.05      The number of women and children receiving some service from the MCH
component of the Family Welfare program in 1988 was aboat 32.6 million and
about 105 million respectively. The MCH portion of the Family Welfare Program
includes six schemes (Annex 3, Table 2) and about ten others which are shared
with the Family Planning part of the Family Welfare program. Mothers and
children have been covered primarily by immunization and prophylaxis against
nutritional anemia and blindness (Annex 4). The growth in MCH coverage under
the National Family Welfare program is shown graphically in Charts 1 and 2.
D. Problems and Issues in the Family Welfare PrograN
1.06      Despite the above achievements, the present level of infant and
maternal mortality in India remains unacceptably high, partly because of
demographic, socio-economic and cultural factors, and partly due to a number
of constraints and weaknesses ea the Family Welfare program itself. Shifte in
program design are therefore necessary. The quantity and quality of service
delivery, especially at first referral level, have to be expanded and im-
proved, and the management of the program strengthened. There are several
factors which directly affect the quality of service delivery, including; (a)
the training and management of personael of facil'ties where family welfare,
nutrition and other health services are delivered, i.e., subcenters, PHCs,
CHCs and post-partum units at district hospitals; (b) the location of facilit-
ies for service delivery; and (c) the availability of good quality supplies.



1.07      Infant Mortality.  Children up to 14 years of age constitute nearly
40? of India's total population. At the start of the Eighth Five-Year Plan
period (1990), ther':' were an estimated 330 million in this age group. In the
0-5 age group the population was estimated at 100 million. In India, the
infant mortality rate (IMR) was 93 per 1,000 live births in 1989, and while
there has been a discernible decline over the last decade, it is still
unacceptably high, with significant differences between States. The lowest
rate is in Kerala (28 per 1,000 live births) and the highest in Uttar Pradesh
(127 per 1,000 live births). Within States, even larger differences exist
between districts. Peri-natal mortality rates are above 50 per 1,000 live
births in the most populous States, reflecting not only the poor health status
and care of women during pregnancy, but also the poor quality of services at
birth. The child death rate among those below five years of age is estimated
at 38.4 per 1,000 children, and is much higher in the States of Uttar Pradesh,
Bihar, Madhya Pradesh and Orissa - between 60-70 per 1,000 children. Though
the infant mortality rate does not vary much with gender, the mortality rates
among those below five years of age have been consistently higher for females
than males, in both urban and rural areas. In 1985, the age specific death
rate for children below five years was estimated at 45.3 for girls and 41.4
for boys in rural areas and 21.1 for girls and 19.4 for boys in urban areas.
This reflects the slower response of both the community and the health care
system to the health and medical needs of the girl child. 11
1.08      There are several contributory factors to high mortality rates among
young children (Chart 3). First, there is prematurity and low birth weight in
at least 301 of all births. Second, high rates of exposure to unfavorable
environmental conditions and endemic and communicable disease such as tetanus,
measles, respiratory infections, malaria and diarrhoea are the major causes of
death and illness in young children. In fact, the 600,000 deaths per year
-rom acute respiratory infections (ARI) in India account for more than a
quarter of all such deaths in the world. Over a third of deaths in the world
from dehydration due to diarrhoeal diseases (1,500,000 per year) are also in
India. Third, malnutrition is a major underlying cause of death in 30-50
percent of the 0-5 years age group and over 400,000 children become blind
every year due to Vitamin A deficiency.
1.09      Maternal Mortality and Morbidity.  Many women step on the road to
maternal mortality when they are born as unwanted female infants. As young
girls, they are undernourished, overworked, and uneducated. They begin
bearing children at a young age, continue until an advanced age, and have many
pregnancies during their reproductive life span. From infancy until death,
females have limited access to health services. Poverty and tradition often
contribute to rendering them malnourished, uneducated, and unaware of their
bqsic rights. Moreover, they suffer because of their lowly status and little
recognition of their important contribution to family and community life.
1.10      The sex ratio in India is shown in Chart 4.  It has declined over
the decades. The slight improvement noticed in the 1981 Census has not been
maintained in 1991; rather, the ratio of women to men has suffered, a decline
1/ Selective Discrimination Against Female Children in Rural Punjab,
India; Das Gupta, Monica - Population and Development Review,
Volume 13, 1987.



-4-
of £ive points from 934 in 1981 to 929 in 1991. Studies in India have given
some answers for this general disparity in the sex ratio. Some have identi-
fied a preference for male children resulting in a neglect of female babies.
Others have suggested the relative gap in health conditions between males and
females and certain types of mortality which are sex selective. The adverse
sex ratio is also due to lower expectation of life at birth for females
compared to males, part of which is d'ae to high maternal mo'tality. India
accounts for 252 of the total maternal deaths in the world. The major
immediate causes of maternal deaths are bleeding, puerperal sepsis, eclampsia,
abortions and obstructed labor (Chart 5). The maternal mortality rate in
India is unacceptably }.'gh, estimated to be about 400 per 100,000 live births
a year, with wide in-t-State variations. It compares with 280 per 100,000
live births for the s,  d as a whole and 80 per 100,000 live births in Sri
Lanka.  There are r,  chan 25 million births a year in India, and around 302-
402 of these are t. -ripk pregnancies, dangerous both to the mother and
child.  In this piv   s, ue than 100,000 women are estimated to die each
year, while over 1.6 mi'lie  women suffer morbidity of varying degrees.  Early
marriage and early and frequent child bearing with short-spaced pregnancies
contribute to risk. These conditions are aggravated by over-work, malnutri-
tion and anemia, low levels of female literacy, lack of access to health
facilities and the lack of maternity benefits. If India's maternal mortality
ratio and total fertility rates were on par with the developed countries,
there would be 2,000 instead of 100,000-150,000 maternal deaths each year.
However, it should be noted that both high-risk and low-risk women develop
obstetric complications, thus necessitating prompt and adequate medical care
for all women who develop complications.
1.11      For every woman who dies, as many as sixteen suffer from untreated
complications of pregnancy and childbirth. These unreported and undetected
disorders cause untold suffering and misery to individual women, who may
become rejected and even divorced, thereby reducing their already low status.
Maternal ill-health associated with child-bearing may wrongly be regarded in
society as unavoidable, no matter how severe. The frequently untreated
complications of the immediate post-delivery period include cardiovascular
disorders, jaundice, acute prolapse of the uterus, and puerperal psychiatric
illness. Long-term sequelae of childbirth complications are related to
obstructed labor, obstetric hemorrhage and puerperal infection, and include
vesico-vaginal or rectal fistulae, vaginal and perineal tears, and injuries
which affect menstruation, sexual intercourse and future child-bearing. Also
common are pelvic inflammatory disease following infection, and prolapsed
uterus associated with traumatic delivery. 1/ What is needed is provision
of adequate care to reduce this large pool of unreported and untreated
morbidity.
1.12      Training and Personnel Management.  Health and Family Welfare
Training programs are outlined in Annex 5. Section 1. The delivery of family
planning and MCH services is carried out primarily by female health workers
(Auxiliary Nurse Midwife (ANM) or female multipurpose worker] who are the

linchpin of the program. While job descriptions for all levels and types of
staff have been specified, responsibilities are not evenly distributed, and
increased rationalization of the task load of workers is required. Personnel
practices are also weak. Efforts to improve personnel management through more
systematic performance appraisal methods, enhanced career development opportu-
nities, and clearer job definition and selection criteria, require priority
attention. IDA is supporting MOHFW efforts to address the task loads, worker
routines, job descriptions, career development and the institutionalization of
Personnel Management Information Systems (PHIS) under the Sixth and Seventh
Population Projects.
1.13      Several major problems also affect the quality of pre- and in-
service paramedical training (Annex 5. Section 2). These include insufficient
linkages between training curricula and job requirements, failure to attract
suitable talent to training institutions, limited faculty development efforts,
over-reliance on didactic/lecture teaching methods, and limited use of demon-
strations and field-based training. Clinical training is also weak; many
staff are ignorant of the advantages/disadvantages of temporary methods and
birth spacing. Training in key MCH interventions, such as how to manage
programs for low birth weight infants or how to recognize lat-risk' women is
limited. Moreover, orientation of key administrative staff in program manage-
ment is often lacking. There is, therefore, a need to both assess and modify
the existing curricula and to strengthen the practical aspects of health
worker training. Based on a number of training needs assessments carried out
under several area projects (para 2.09) supported by DANIDA, USAID and IDA,
gaps in the knowledge and skills of workers and their training needs have been
clearly identified. 1/ Therefore, IDA is assisting the MOHFW and the States
to address these issues of training more systematically under the Fourth,
Fifth, Sixth and Seventh Population projects where a total of over 2.0 million
health workers, supervisors, administrators, community workers and other non-
health department functionaries will be trained and updated in family welfare.
1.14      Facilities, Supplies, Logistics and Maintenance.  Inadequate
essential obstetric functions at subcenters, PHC and CHCs which are the
primary locus for family welfare activities, constitute a major constraint in
the area of maternal survival. Studies have shown these facilities are often
understa'fed and, in some areas, inconveniently located relative to the
population thereby contributing to low utilization. 2/ All seven IDA-

1.15      While the overall availability of supplies is adequate, distri-
bution, maintenance and inventory control systems require strengthening.
Decentralization of financial control, redesign of inventory assessment and
control procedures, and improved monitoring of stocks will be important steps
to improving this situation. Significant strides have been made to rectify
theee problems under the Seventh Five-Year Plan with the assistance of UNICEF.
1.16      Limited availability and poor maintenance of vehicles because of
budget constraints limits performance at all levels of the system. For
example, in a total fleet of about 2,000 vehicles in a State, at any one time
over 60? are out-of-service. This fact, when combined with the lack of
ambulances or other forms of motorized transport at the village level, has a
devastatingly negative impact on emergency evacuations of women when they have
obstetric complications. Steps to decentralize financial responsibility for
vehicle maintenance, ease restrictions on the use of private sector repair
facilities, and more systematic procedures for allocating vehicles, would lead
to significant improvements in the efficiency of the available fleet in the
States. UNICEF's input to the Universal Immunization Program (UIP) includes a
significant number of vehicles as weli as support to State Health Transport
Organizations (SHTOs) to improve their maintenance and logistic systems. IDA
has provided support for the construction and equipping of two vehicle repair
and maintenance facilities and about 300 vehicles under the Sixth and Seventh
Population Projects.
1.17      Administration, Management and Evaluation of selected aspects of the
family welfare progLam is centralized while implementation is left largely to
the States. A number of evaluations of program administration and management
have been completed and the MOHFW has also commissioned a number of studies to
assess current management practices in the program. 1/ The MOHFW has re-
cently taken certain actions which, over time, would decentralize some aspects
of program administration, as well as strengthen state-level capacity to plan,
manage, and evaluate program operations. Under the Fifth, Sixth and Seventh
Population projects, the respective States and Municipalities have been
encouraged to prepare detailed Annual Action Plans which take more account of

1.18      Monitoring of program performance has consisted primarily of
compiling family planning and MCH service statistics which are assessed
against annual targets. The almost exclusive reliance on annual targets to
assess program impact enhances the focus on family planning increases and
reinforces the neglect of the MCH side of the program. A major effort is
required to develop reliable ways of estimating MCH coverage rates. The
Concurrent Evaluation which is presently being undertaken by the MOHFW in
coordination with 17 major States (Annex 6) will provide the basic information
for this reform.
1.19      Management Information Systems (MIS) are necessary for personnel,
financial and material resource management. 1/ An effective MIS can supply
information for improved scheduling of activities, establishment of MCH and
family planning priorities among target couples, follow-up of high-risk cases,
and the local estimation of MCH and family planning coverage rates. The
information can also permit field workers to evaluate their own perfcmmance
much more effectively. Currently, system design and MIS training have been
accomplished in Karnataka and Kerala under the Third Population project, West
Bengal under the Fourth project, and in Bombay and Madras in tS' Fifth
project. Work is now underway to upgrade MIS in Uttar Pradesh, Andhra
Pradesh, Madhya Pradesh, Bihar, Jammu and Kashmir, Punjab, Haryana and Gujarat
under the Sixth and Seventh Population projects.  In addition, with UNICEF's
assistance, the MOHFW has developed and implemented effective monitoring and
reporting systems for the Universal Immunization Program (UIP).
1.20      The potential contribution of operational research to improved
implementation has not yet been fully appreciated in family welfare program
design. As a result, this activity is neglected, and the results of the few
studies which are undertaken are ignored. One way of strengthening operation-
al research would be to make increased use of the private sector and academic
institutions on a contract basis. Under the Fifth, Sixth and Seventh Popula-
tion projects, IDA is supporting an extensive program of Operational Research
which would directly address the concerns generated by the setting of artifi-
cially high numerical targets and measurement of performance for both family
planning and MCH. The results of research will be used to modify the target
setting system in the family welfare program which has led in the past to many
distortions in implementation.
1.21      Demand Generation and Social Marketing.  The single most important
way to improve the demand for these services would be to overtly recognize
that the 'market' for family welfare services consists of multiple segments,
each characterized by distinctive levels of awareness, predispositions,
attitudes, needs, opportunity costs and incomes. While the use of mass media
campaigns has increased awareness of family planning, particularly permanent
1/ Management Information Systems in Health and Family Welfare-State
Level Status Reports; Administrative Staff College of India;
Hyderabad, 1989.



-8-
methods, there is now an urgent need to develop and implement IEC strategies
which effectively increase the use of temporary methods and maternal and child
health services. The critical role of the health worker and the complemen-
tarity of mass media and interpersonal communications in this process is
recognized. 1/ MOHFW has agreed that IDA could assist in a thorough review
of its national IEC program. Preliminary work in this direction has begun in
the context of a study on Women's Health (para 2.20).  At the same time, in
the context of each of the on-going population projects, inputs have been made
to assist States and Municipalities to devise location and target-specific IEC
programs and to emphasize more interpersonal communication in the training of
family welfare workers.
1.22      As part of the 'cafeteria' approach of making all forms of con-
traceptives available, the GOI initiated a Free Distribution and Social
Marketing of Condoms program in 1968. The Social Marketing of Contraceptives
program was undertaken with the participation of twel e public and private
organizations to promote the use of condoms, while 1 ee distribution was
carried out through the Health and Family Welfare network of PHCs and Sub-
Centers. The social marketing program was expanded in 1987 to include Oral
Contraceptive Pills (OCP) with the participation of four pharmaceutical
companies and two non-goverNmental organizations. A comprehensive social
marketing program for oral contraceptives, which includes the accepted
practices of market surveys, audience segmentation, and product promotion, is
a part of the assistance provided by IDA to MOHFW under the Seventh Population
Project.
11. GOVERNMENT PLANS AND POLICIES IN FAMILY VELFARE
A. Health and Population PolicY
2.01      In 1983, the GOI established a National Health Policy in the context
of the world-wide objective of "Health for All by 2000 A.D.0 The broad goals
of this policy are to achieve by 2000 A.D.: (a) reduction of maternal mor-
tality to below 200 per 100,000 live birth; (b) reduction to 10? or less the
proportion of low birth weight babies defined as those weighing less than 2500
grams at birth; and (c) reduction in infant mortality from 94 to 50 or less
per thousand. The specific intermediate and long-term goals of the policy are
outlined in Annex 7. While many of these goals are ambitious and may not be
fully achieved by 2000, substantial progress has been made in immunization.
With some reorientation of program design, implementation and financing, most
other targets could be approached by 2000.
2.02      The Seventh Five-Year Plan (1985-1990) and Revised StrateRy.  Under
the Seventh Five-Year Plan (1985-1990), the important family welfare goals
remain as outlined in the National Health Policy. To achieve these, the Plan
included: (a) compensation/awards to acceptors of sterilization and IUDs; (b)
1/ - Family Planning Practices in India; Third All-India Survey; Operations
Research Group, Baroda, 1990.
- USAID Area Development Project; Communication Needs Assessment Report-
Health and Family Welfare Center; Ahmedabad, Gujarat.
- IEC Training Plan-Operation Strategy; Indian Institute of Health
Managements and Research, Jaipur, 1989.



- 9 -
promotion of temporary contraceptive methods for couples for whom steriliza-
tion is inappropriate; (c) intensive implementation of the Universal Immuniza-
tion Program (UIP); and (d) IEC efforts to increase knowledge and acceptance
of both family planning and MCH components of the program. In support of the
family welfare program, female education would be expanded and compliance with
the legal ages for marriage, which is 18 for females and 21 for males, would
be enforced. Other features of the Plan cal.led for increased involvement of
local communities, particularly through the Village Health Guide (VHG) Scheme.
The use of Medical Termination of Pregnancy (MTP) in cases of contraceptive
failure, which was provided for under legislation enacted in 1972, Was also
included.
2.03      In 1986. the MOHFW revised its strategy in order to make the
National Family Welfare Program more responsive to the segmented market for
the provision of family welfare services. The Revised Strategy calls for new
efforts to raise the average age of marriage of women to over 20 years, by
intensifying female literacy programs, curbing the school dropout rate for
girls, and offering better employment opportunities for rural women. To
supplement this, greater emphasis was to be given to IEC and an effort made to
move away from simple publicity to more targeted programs to overcome provider
and client resistance to temporary methods. Linkages were established with
adult education programs, schools, colleges, trade unions, employers associa-
tions and PVOs which are seen as most effective in motivating people at the
grassroots level. The effort to reduce infant mortality below 60 per 1,000
births was to be concentrated in the universal imnunization program (UIP).
The goal was to immunize every child and expectant mother by 1990. However,
by the end of 1990 coverage had reached only 70?. A nutrition intervention
program was also to be expanded to distribute iron tablets, Vitamin A, and
iodized salt. This has been less successful, due mainly to a shortage of
resources. The Integrated Child Development Services program (ICDS) was
expanded to provide immunization, nutritional supplementation, nutrition
education, maternal and child health check-ups, pre-school education and
female adult literacy to a larger number of deprived areas. ICDS now covers
402 of India's rural blocks.
2.04      Safe Motherhood:  Goals and Strategies.  In March 1990, the India
Government outlined its Safe Motherhood Goals and a Strategy for implementa-
tion. The major goals are: (a) reducing maternal mortality from the existing
level of at least 400 per 100,000 live births to under 200 per 100,000 live
births by 2000; (b) creating an optimum child-bearing pattern by reducing the
absolute number of pregnancies, in particular those which are dangerous to
mother and child; (c) improving the quality of maternal services to ensure a
healthy outcome for mother and child; and td) reducing the proportion of low
birth weight babies from the existing level of 302 to less than 102 by 2000.
2.05      These goals are to be achieved through the following improvements in
existing services: (a) increasing antenatal services to pregnant women from
the existing level of 40? to near total coverage; (b) the proportion of
deliveries conducted by trained birth attendants from the existing level of
40? to near total coverage; (c) expanding immunization coverage with tetanus
toxoid from 60? to near universal coverage; (d) increasing the contraceptive
prevalence rate from 44? to 602 with special emphasis on birth spacing; and
(e) expanding coverage of pregnant women for anaemia prophylaxis from 30? to
60?. In addition, the GOI strategy emphasizes the reorientation of on-going



- 10 _
programs, expansion of first-level referral services and institutional
deliveries, an increased role for NGOs and information programs, and social
and other measures to delay the age of marriage and reduce the incidence of
teenage marriage. The Safe Motherhood Strategy is outlined in detail in
Annex 7.
2.06      The Maternal and Child Health Plan (1991-1995).  The end of the
Seventh Five-Year Plan period (1989/90) coincided with the advent of severe
budgetary pressures in India, and this was further aggravated by the Gulf
crisis. The result is that expenditures on MCH services declined significant-
ly from Seventh Plan levels during 1990/91. The GOI is now trying to maintain
MCH services at least at levels attained at the end of the Seventh Plan. For
the 1991-95 period, the Plan calls for the implementation of an integrated
package of services to improve both maternal and child sur-vival and to deliver
these services in a more efficient and effective manner. An affordable
holistic program has been developed, aimed at better health of women and
children within constraints imposed by across-the-board cuts in public
expenditures. In addition, the enhanced identification of high-risk cases
calls for the provision of appropriate services at existing institutions, i.e.
CHCs, PHCs and subcenters. The strategy is to use the opportunity and
experience created by the National Immunization Mission or Universal Immuniza-
tion Program (UIP), in terms of continued contact with mothers and young
children, to implement a UIP "Plus" package of services combining immunization
with other basic MCH interventions. The components would be: (a) For young
children: newborn care. primary immunization by 12 months, administration of
Vitamin A and iron as prophylaxis against blindness and anaemia, pneumonia
therapy (ARI), and control of diarrhoeal diseases; and (b) For mothers: ante
natal care, immunization against tetanus, anaemia control during pregnancy,
screening and referral of high risk cases, care at birth, and promoting birth
spacing and timing. In operational terms, the strategy would require unified
training for all levels of health workers and supervisors. The logistics
system developed for UIP would have to be strengthened to handle the addition-
al interventions, and the monitoring and supervision of all interve-itions at
the State and district levels would have to be unified. Making one Team at
the district level responsible for all MCH interventions would be a main
objective. The interventions for mother and child health care will be
developed and implemented in each district in a phased manner, with particular
attention to districts with high maternal mortality. The infrastructure and
resources of the States/districts will also be taken into account when
defining the level of service delivery. A Phased Implementation Program is
shown in Chart 6. The specific districts chosen for the phased implementation
of UIP "Plus" and Safe Motherhood in 1991 are shown in Annex 8.
B. Financing of Family Welfare
2.07      The Central Government finances one hundred percent (100Z) of
capital and recurrent expenditures of the Family Welfare Program (FWP). A
unique feature of the FWP is that during the successive Five-Year Plans, no
liability of the previous Plans has either been transferred to the recurrent
budget of the States or the Central government. Resource allocations over the
last two decades reflect increasing government commitment to the Program.
Financial outlays under the Seventh Plan (Rs.3256.0 million) were six hundred
times greater than those under the Third Plan. The share of family welfare to
total health expenditures increased from 10 percent during the Third Plan to



- 11 -
almost 50 percent during the Seventh P'lan and as a share of total public
sector expenditures, increased from 0.3 to 1.8 percent. MCH is a key compo-
nent of FWP, accounting for one fourth of expenditures in 1990, up from 16
percent 5 years earlier. Operational expenses account for three-fourths of
the expenditures, drugs and vaccines for over 1OZ, while staff training
absorbed 3Z of total capital and recurrent expenditures, implying an emphasis
on quality improvement in service delivery.
2.08      As public sector budgets increased, family welfare and specifically
MCH activities, competed favorably for more than proportionate increases of
resource allocations. However, during a tight budgetary squeeze, they were at
a disadvantage. The 'tzrrent diffictlt budgetary conditions, further aggravat-
ed by the Guif crisis, resulted in reduced allocations for family welfare
programs. The 1990/91 FWP allocation of Rs. 6750 million was 14Z less than
1989/90 expenditures (Rs. 7810 million). For MCH activities, allocations were
reduced by 3 percent from Rs. 1,970.0 to Rs. 1,900.0 million. The decline was
the first in the history of these programs and implies vulnerability during
periods of fiscal constraint and reductions in government budgets. The
proposed project will reverse the sluggish start of the first year of the MCH
Plan as GOI adjusts its resource allocation criteria in favor of high priority
projects during a period of fiscal constraints and budgetary adjustments
(Annex 9). The proposed 1991/92 MCH allocation of Rs.5,743.0 million is
Rs.384.3 million over the 1990/91 allocation and attempts to recover some of
the decreases suffered during 1990/91. The proposed allocations for the
1991/92-1994/95 period would have to increase at about 112 p.a. over the
1991/92 allocation in order to meet fully the outlays proposed for the
national MCH Plan during this period. These levels are both appropriate and
sustainable as explained in paragraph 4.08. It should also be noted that the
proposed outlays for MCH would still represent a small share of overall health
sector expenditures. Lastly, these levels are also essential if basic MCH
services are to be provided to the specifically targeted clientele.
2.09      International assistance has supported health and family welfare
activities in India since 1963. Support has come from UNFPA, UNICEF, and WHO,
as well as from the governments of the United Kingdom (ODA), Norway (NORAD),
Denmark (DANIDA), Sweden (SIDA), and the United States (USAID). The Area
Projects program (para 2.13) is also being assisted by UNFPA, ODA, DANIDA, and
USAID (Annex 10). Specifically, USAID has assisted Punjab, Haryana and
Gujarat and UNFPA, Bihar. The International Planned Parenthood Federation
provides financial support to the Family Planning Association of India (PPAI).
UNICEF has concentrated its efforts on UIP and under the Seventh Five-Year
Plan, has been successful in mobilizing and coordinating support from a number
of bilateral donors for this program. Among private organizations, the
Population Council, the Ford Foundation, and the Rockefeller Foundation
support demographic and biomedical research. All of the Area Projects were
completed in 1986. Most of the donors noted above have concluded agreements
with the GOI for a second round of projects. DANIDA will continue to fund
activities in selected districts in Tamil Nadu and Madhya Pradesh and has
committed about US$20.0 million for the three year period ending in 1992.
UNFPA will concentrate its efforts in Rajasthan, Himachal Pradesh and Assam
and will contribute about US$60.0 million over a five-year period. ODA will
contribute about US$40.0 million over a five-year period and will continue its
activities in Orissa. WHO will provide US$0.3 million during 1990-91 for



- 12 -
Family Planning and MCH services in urban areas and for developing the Mid-
Term Plan for the Prevention and Control of AIDS.
C. ID-Assisted P2pulation (Family Welfare) and Nutrition Projects and
Sector StrateRy
2.10      Since 1973 IDA has supported seven population and three nutrition
projects for a total of US$700.0 million (Annex 11). The First Population
Project (Cr.312-IN) was confined to 6 districts in Uttar Pradesh (UP) and 5
districts in Karnataka. It started in 1973 and was expected to be a five year
project closing in 1978. It was extended for two years and closed in 1980 for
a duration of seven years. The full amount of the Credit of US$21.0 million
was fully disbursed. A Project Completion Report was prepared in May 1981.
The Project Performance Audit Report (No.3748) was completed in August 1981.
The Second Population Project (Cr.981-IN) concentrated on 3 districts in
Andhra Pradesh (AP) and 6 districts in UP, and introduced the Area Projects
concept. These projects were designed for implementation in 67 backward
districts in the 15 major States and were assisted by three bilateral donor
agencies (USAID, DANIDA, '!"A) and the UNFPA and IDA. The project started in
1980, was expected to be completed in five years by 1985, but was extended to
1988 for a duration of seven years. About 96Z of the Credit of US$46.0
million was disbursed and the remainder canceled. The Project Completion
Report was completed in June 1989.
2.11      The Third, Fourth and Fifth Population Projects were designed along
a pattern similar to the Second project. The Third Population Project (FY84)
(Cr.1425-IN), covers 6 of 20 districts in Karnataka and 4 of 14 districts in
Kerala. The Fourth Population Project (FY86) (Cr.1623-IN) covers 4 of 16
districts in West Bengal.  The Fifth Population Project (FY88) (Cr.1931-IN)
covers the area under the jurisdiction of the Bombay Municipal Corporation
(BMC) in Maharashtra, and the Madras Municipal Corporation (MMC) and the
district of Chingleput in Tamil Nadu. The Sixth and Seventh Population
Project (Cr.2057-IN; Ln.3108-IN) (Cr.2133-IN; Ln.3199-IN) deviate from the
Area Project approach and cover in their entirety the States of Andhra Pradesh
(AP) Madhya Pradesh (MP) and Uttar Pradesh (UP), Punjab, Bihar, Gujarat,
Haryana, and Jammu and Kashmir. The Sixth Project was approved by the Board
in FY89 and the Seventh Project in FY90.
2.12      Implementation of the on-going Third, Fourth, and Fifth population
projects has been largely satisfactory, with delays only in some areas of
construction. Under the Third Project, Kerala has met most of its project
objectives, while the results in Karnataka have been mixed and the project
will be closed in March 1992. Very encouraging results are also emerging from
the Fourth and Fifth Projects. In West Bengal, contraceptive prevalence rates
have increased, and the training of staff and construction of facilities have
been implemented with a large degree of success. In Bombay and Madras, in
addition to increases in contraceptive use, both cities have pursued "beyond
family planning" strategies including maternal and child health, sanitation
and clean drinking water with the active cooperation of non-governmental
organizations, private medical practitioners and their respective public
health departments. The Sixth and Seventh projects are still relatively new.
As implementation of these projects has proceeded, both GOI and IDA agreed
that in order to have a meaningful impact on fertility, maternal and tnfant
mortality, more emphasis would have to be given to some activities and



- 13 -
coverage expanded from limited districts to the entire State. The Third and
Fourth projects have been restructured to become state-wide projects and as
flexible in design as the Sixth and Seventh Project to cover most of the
Schemes of the Family Welfare program in the specific States and municipali-
ties. The Board approved these changes in January 1990. This flexibility
allows for broader program support, quicker disbursements and speedier
implementation. It also allows for IDA's inputs to have a greater impact on a
larger group of beneficiaries.
2.13      The Tamil Nadu Integrated Nutrition Project (TNINP)(Cr.1003-IN) was
appraised in 1980 and completed in 1989. A Project Completion Report
(No.9259) was issued in 1990. This project succeeded in halving the rate of
severe malnutrition among young children, by providing growth monitoring,
nutrition education and maternal and child health services on a outreach
basis, together with supplementary feeding for malnourished children. Much of
the project's success was attributed to the detailed attention paid at the
design stage to ensuring that field workers' jobs were manageable, that their
daily and monthly work routines were clearly defined and regular refresher on-
the-job training was provided to ensure they could professionally carry out
the limited number of tasks they were assigned. This experience has been used
in the design of the Second Tamil Nadu Integrated Nutrition Project
(Cr.1580-IN) which was approved in 1990 and the First Integrated Child
Development Scheme Project (Cr.2173-IN; Ln.3253-IN) which was approved in
1991.
2.14      Lessons Learned.  Previous IDA projects, as well as projects
supported by other donors, particularly DANIDA and USAID, provide several
lessons for the development and implementation of performance and quality
improvement efforts in the MCH aspects of the Family Welfare Program.
Previous experience has shown that: (a) maternal mortality and morbidity will
remain at high levels unless there is prompt and adequate medical care for
those who develop obstetric complications; (b) the quantity and quality of MCH
care are far below desired levels; (c) within the community in general and
particularly among pregnant and lactating women, there is low awareness of the
need for effective care, including ante-natal check-ups, improved nutrition,
temporary methods of family planning, and the benefits of child spacing; (d)
improving the quality and management of family welfare services is critical in
influencing the demand for services; (e) an analysis of local demographic and
sociological characteristics is fundamental to the efficient and effective use
of available staff and equipment at local levels; (f) although the need for
local community and private sector involvement to create support for family
welfare is recognized, skills and aptitude in community mobilization are
limited among medical and para-medical staff; (g) clinical skills in aspects
of reproductive and maternal health, including safe delivery techniques,
knowledge and use of the referral structure, and temporary methods of contra-
ception, are weak among all levels of staff and can be improved through short-
term in-service and recurrent on-the-job training efforts; and (h) detailed
attention must be paid at the project design stage, to ensure field workers
jobs are manageable, that their daily and monthly routines are clearly
defined, and that they are continuously trained and updated on-the-job to
perform better the limited tasks they are assigned (para 2.13). Results from
West Bengal under the Fourth Populat'on Project, Madras under the Fifth
Population Project, and Tamil Nadu under the Integrated Nutrition Project are
particularly instructive. The Sixth and Seventh Population Projects will



- 14 -
utilize these lessons in th four largest States of Uttar Pradesh, Madhya
Pradesh, Bihar and Andhra Pradesh as well as other States.
2.15      The First (UP anl (arnataka) and Second (UP and AP) IDA assisted
population projects attempted to address these issues through the implementa-
tion of management improvement efforts focused on tei.ning medical staff in
management and improving the efficiency and utilizatiorn of management informa-
tion and evaluation systems (MIES). Lessons learned in these efforts includet
(a) to achieve significant changes in performance, it is desirable to forge
linkages between training efforts and improvements in the use of MIES systems;
and (b) management interventions, including trainJng efforts and/or changes in
performance measurement, require strong commitment and support from top
administrators.
2.16      MIES have been subjected to considerable attention in all previous
IDA projects.  Efforts to improve these systems have been useful in highlight-
ing the nature of the problems but generally less successful at implementing
solutions.  An experimental effort to employ microcomputers to streamline data
collection and facilitate more rapid analysis of data in the Ahmedabad
district of Gujarat has shown that district and block level staff can use such
a system effectively to analyze both quantitative and qualitative aspects of
performance at the local level. Further development of MIES at the field
level will focus on the use of MIES data by field workers to prioritize their
activities within the framework of a 'cycle of care" approach to the delivery
of family welfare services.
2.17      The project design also incorporates recent research findings on
Vitamin A and supplementation for iron deficiency anemia. For Vitamin A,
close attention has been given to: data which showed that 502 of corneal
blindness occurs after measles when the episode is associated with Vitamin A
deficiency; a World Health organization (WHO) 1988 recommendation that Vitamin
A should be delivered through Expanded Programs of Immunization to reach
vulnerable children and mothers in order to control Vitamin A deficiency which
is the most important preventable cause of blindness 1/; and the United
Nations Administrative Committee on Coordination Subcommittee on Nutrition
(ACC/SCN) conclusion in 1989, that a significant reduction in child mortality
would be possible following Vitamin A dosing. This recommendation was based
on experiences in Indonesia, Thailand, Sudan, China and Nepal 2/; and a 1990
study in Tamil Nadu, as reported in the New Engiand Journal of Medicine, which
demonstrated a significant reduction in mortality among children receiving a
small weekly dose of Vitamin A. 3/ Regarding iron deficiency anemia, a
1985-86 study in India showed that the lack of adherence to a supplementation
regime is a major constraint to the effectiveness of this prophylaxis inter-

vention. The dropout rate from the regime varied from 9S to 8.: between
States for an average of 58Z. Over 802 of the dropouts cited the poor supply
of tablets as the major reason for their discontinuation. I/ An inadequate
and unreliable supply of supplementation and low service utilization are also
major constraints in most iron supplementation programs. The ACC/SCN in 1990
suggested that program performance and compliance with a supplementation
regime depends on outreach, effectiveness of service delivery and improved
community understanding and participation. 2/
2.18      The proposed project, to the extent possible, takes into account
most of these lessons and builds on the capacity-enhancing activities support-
ed by the previous seven population and three nutrition projects (Annex 11,
Section 2). Since UNICEF will be an active co-financier of the project, th,e
lessons of UNICEF's successes in supporting the UIP would be further expanded.
With UNICEF's assistance, GOI has been able to train effectively about 2.5
million workers over the past five years. The national immunization effort
has increased coverage to above 70X with estimates in some States at about
852. The development of sentinel centers and an effici.ent logistics system
including a cold-chain which has worked well, suggests the project will build
on a program with a strong performance and which has had many successes. As
regards Safe Motherhood, MOHFW will undertake and complete an assessment of
first level referral facility requirements (para 3.24). The MOHFW will also
undertake and complete within the first project year, a detailed plan for
districts selected for the phased implementation of the Safe Motherhood
initiative. The project will also assist the MOHFW to improve coordination
with other relevant Ministries as well as between its Family Planning and MCH
Divisions in the support given to ma.ernal health i,, particular.  in brief,
the major lesson which the proposed project will institute is that instead of
trying to put more family planning into the MCH program, it would assist in
putting more and better MCH services alongside the national family planning
program and build on those parts which work best.
2.19      Sector Strategy.  In January 1987, India and IDA agreed on a
Population Sector Strategy which would guide future collaboration in the
sector. It was agreed that IDA's broad goal in the family welfare sector is
to support GOI's National Family Welfare Program. Assistance would be guided
by the central objective of helping GOI create an environment in which the
different types of clients for family planning and MCH (actual or potential)
have the requisite knowledge, attitudes, and access to services which are
provided in a cost-effective manner. In so doing, IDA support would assist in
(a) reorienting the family welfare program from its present static facility-
based orientation to include a larger element of outreach to the communities

a more balanced mix of contraceptive methods emphasizing increased use of
temporary methods by younger couples; (c) increasing the attention given to
the implementation of the maternal and child health elements of the program.
Programs which enhance service delivery, training and IEC, and are concentrat-
ed in urban slums and backward high fertility States would be supported as a
priority.
2.20      To maximize the use of resources, a program of sector work and
expanded lending for health and family welfare has been started. The Bank's
population, health and nutrition sector work program in India was initiated
only in 1988. Three studies have been completed so far. A review of the
Indian Family Planning program: 'Family Welfare Strategy in India: Changing
the Signals' (1989) emphasized the need to offer temporary family planning
methods as well as sterilizatior., which has accounted for the bulk of accep-
tors in India. It also stressed the need to deliver family planning services
in the context of an integrated package of KCH services. Another 1989 study
"Improving Family Planning, Health and Nutrition Outreach in India" compared
the designs of three successful Bank-assisted outreach programs in India
including the Tamil Nadu Nutrition program. In addition to noting that the
other projects shared TINP's emphasis on clearly specified, manageable tasks
and strong in-service training for field workers, this study concluded that
involvement of community groups was an important factor in project success.
The community mobilization component of the proposed project is designed to
draw on these lessons. A third study, 'Strengthening the Role of NGOs in the
Health and Family Welfare Sector fn India (1989),' analyzed the relationship
between health NGOs and GOI, and zecommended steps the government could take
toward a greater NGO role in thsit gamily welfare program.  A study entitled
'The Status of Women's Health irn India" is presently underway. The study is
based on an analysis of the sociological context of women's health in India
and an examination of the current epidemiological situation. It then assesses
the services available to address women's health needs in both the public and
private sectors and identifies imp 'ovements in existing services as well as
additional services that would be  squired if the identified needs are to be
met. Particular emphasis is placed on communications aspects of strengthened
services and on the relative cost-ef.-ectiveness of alternative intervention
strategies. A Health Financing Stu(v .s being prepared for implementation
during the secoid half of 1991.  The szudy will collect detailed information
on primary health care expenditures In twelve districts within four States.
The study aims at providing the analytical basis for informed dialogue between
GOI and the Bank, leading to a more focused attention on priority issues.
2.21      To date, IDA has supported a nimber of important program develop-
ments. These includes reorienting the emphasis given to the sterilization of
older higher parity women to increasing birth spacing and the use of temporary
methods of contraception by younger lower parity couples; c: nging the target-
setting system; increasing the role of Private Voluntary Organizations; and
changing the administration of program incentives. The proposed project
reflects movement along this path, and will allow IDA and GOI to address more
clearly some of the systemic issues and policies which retard effective MCH
program implementation.



- 17 _
III. TME PROJECT
A. Rationale and Strategy
3.01      Rationale.  First, tla project is a critical part of IDA's strategy
of supporting human capital development and poverty alleviation in India.
Second, it provides IDA with the opportunity to extend rapid but targeted
assistance to the most vulnerable groups, i.e., poor women and children, who
are at risk of falling through the social safety net during a period of severe
budgetary constraints on publicly-financed social programs. Third, the
project supports a revamped national maternal and child health program which
takes into account equally the issues of maternal and child mortality and
morbidity, overcoming a previous program bias which focused on children to the
detriment of mothers. Fourth, the project is based on a mix of practical and
successful local experiences in implementing a national immunization program
while at the same time, providing for trials and the introduction of new
technologies and approaches to Safe Motherhood and prophylaxis interventions
based on recent research both in India and elsewhere. Fifth, the project
includes several areas where the private sector, non-governmental organiza-
tions and local communities will play a major role in service delivery, i.e.,
social marketing of oral rehydration salts (ORS) and contraceptives, the
design and implementation of information, education and communication (IEC)
and training programs, not only because goverDnment can no longer afford to
provide all these services, but because the non-government sector is more
efficient and effective in these areas. Finally, the project represents the
first large-scale cooperation between IDA and UNICEF in India and gives IDA
the chance to work closely with an organization which already has extensive
contacts with the medical community and a wide network of offices and person-
nel in India. This will enhance policy dialogue, project monitoring and
supervision, as well as overall program evaluation.
3.02      Strategy.  Improving service quality, and assuring appropriately
planned and implemented expansion, necessarily involves focusing project
resources on improving the institutional and managerial procedures which
influence both worker and consumer perceptions of the goals and expected
services to be delivered from the program. Thus, while demographically
diverse, all States need to focus ons (a) strengthening the system's capabili-
ties to deliver MCH services and to deal with obstetric complications; (b)
improving program capacity to deliver MCf nert-ices to younger, lower parity
families, and especially to pregnant and lactating women and children; (c)
strengthening outreach capacity and worker skills in providing counselling and
follow-up services to these families; (d) adjusting current monitoring and
evaluation efforts to better reflect the family welfare program's MCH and
service quality objectives; (e) assuring that workers have the appropriate
skills in both the educational and clinical aspects of their jobs; and (f)
varying service delivery and IEC strategies to meet local needs.
3.03      The proposed project's strategic approach to these issues will be to
focus investments on: (a) further support for the efforts started under the
IDA-assisted Sixth and Seventh Population and the Nutrition Projects as well
as UNICEF-supported UIP, ORT and ARI training for basic, in-service and on-
the-job training of all family welfare program workers. These will be based
on training systems and procedures which concentrate on experiential learning
and performance-based curricula which are closely linked to careful definition



- 18 -
of task and job requirements; and (b) assisting the MOHFW and States to
improve further institutional capacity to ensure that strengthened training
efforts are matched with appropriate changes in the key functional aspects of
program management, particularly in work routines, logistics, IEC, management
information and maintenance systems so they complement and support the invest-
ments made by UNICEF and GOI in the expansion of the UIP, UIP "Plus,O and the
initiation of a Safe Motherhood Program. The proposed project interventions,
the problems which they will directly address, and the expected outcomes are
outlined below in Table 3.1.
