B. Project Goals and Objectives
3.04      Goals.  The goal of the proposed project is to provide further
support to the Government's National Family Welfare program. More specifical-
ly, this operation would (a) assist in financing the incremental costs
associated with the expansion and enhancements to the national Maternal and
Child Health program; (b) help GOI to make the Family Welfare program and
infrastructure more responsive to maternal and child health concerns, espe-
cially improving the capacity of the maternity care system; (c) assist GOI to
sustain services at levels achieved at the end of the Seventh Five-Year Plan,
especially to maintain the social safety net achieved, to expand services to
currently underserved areas and beneficiaries, and to undertake further
detailed planning, trials and phased implementation of the new, integrated and
cost-effective interventions proposed for the 1991-95 period; (d) put more
emphasis on targeting resources at the truly needy, more participation of
community representatives, PVOs, the private sector, and on district-level
planning and management; and (d) promote a holistic care approach to the
delivery of maternal and child health services and Safe Motherhood. The main
beneficiaries of the proposed project would be about 115 million mostly poor
women and about 190 million children.
3.05      Objectives.  The specific objectives would be to: enhance child
survival through improved health status and prevention of child disability;
reduce maternal mortality and morbidity; and increase the effectiveness of
service delivery:
(a) the Child Survival objective would be attained through supporting
Child Survival Programs which include: sustaining the Universal
Immunization Program (UIP), at least at the levels attained at the
end of the Seventh Plan period, as well as strengthening diarrhoea
control programs including the social marketing of oral rehydration
salts through the private sector, control of acute respiratory
infections IARI), prophylaxis against blindness and eye lesions due
to Vitamin A deficiency, enhanced newborn care and the active promo-
tion of breast feeding, which together are called UIP *Plus;*
(b) support for the reduction of maternal mortality and morbidity
objective would be achieved through strengthening and further devel-
oping a Safe Motherhood Program which would include providing a
general improvement in maternity care and preventive care programs
for all women, enhanced emphasis on pregnant women with high risk
factors, strengthening essential obstetric care and capabilities to
treat obstetric complications, while concurrently proroting birth
spacing, timing, post-partum counseling, and prophylaxis and control
of nutritional anaemia; and
(c) the increase in effectiveness of service delivery objective would be
achieved by the strengthening of institutional systems including
improving and expanding on-going training programs, especially with
assistance from PVOs, expanding communications and information
dissemination activities with the active participation of women and
other community groups, and strengthening existing State/district
management information, supervision, planning, logistics and mainte-
nance systems.



- 22 -
3.06      The project would be national in scope, but some of the interven-
tions, specifically those for UIP Plus and Safe Motherhood will be started in
a limited number of districts as part of the phased implementation which is an
integral part of the draft Eighth Plan. The project v,ould cover the incre-
mental costs between actual Seventh Five-Year MCH Plan expenditures and
proposed outlays for the 1991-95 period which include the specific enhance-
ments outlined above. These costs are calculated to ensure that external
financing would not cover those costs associated with the on-going liability
of GOI to cover recurrent costs (salaries and drugs) from previous Five-Year
Plans, or the costs of the Medical Termination of Pregnancy program (MTP)
(Annex 9, Table 2). The key measurabLe indicators of project progress are
discussed later (para 4.16 and Annexes 29 and 30).
C. Child Survival (Proposed Outlay USS128.31 million equivalent) 1,
3.07      Sustaining the Universal Immunization Program.  At the end of 1990,
because of the success achieved under the Seventh Plan, immunization coverage
levels had reached an average of about 70% in India. However, wide variations
continue to exist between the States. Special emphasis, including the use of
independent vaccination coverage evaluations to assess the reliability of
data, would be given to improve the immunization coverage status in Uttar
Pradesh, Orissa, Madhya Pradesa, Rajasthan, Assam and Bihar.  It is also
important that high coverage levels be sustained during the next years in the
face of budgetary and other constraints or else communicable disease outbreaks
will result. The thrust of the national program during the 1991-95 period
therefore is continued control of vaccine preventable diseases and achievement
of 'zero' case levels, especially of neonatal tetanus and poliomyelitis.
Booster doses will be administered to prevent outbreaks in both pre-school and
school-age children. Coverage would also be aimed at eliminating remaining
pockets of low or moderate coverage. The new cohort of children born each
year is estimated to be more than 25 million. The Government has outlined the
implementation strategy for the 1991-95 period indicating how acti.vities
additional to those undertaken during the Seventh Plan would be implemented.
It includes how the level of services would be sustained; how the previously
unreached would be reached, the number of children and mothers to be re-
immunized and the feasibility of introducing oral polio vaccine (OPV) at
birth.
3.08      A description of UIP, its cold chain system, immunization schedules,
procurement of vaccines and the protocols on needles/syringes use are given in
Annex 12.  Durir.g negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it
shall (a) prepare and submit to the Association for review and comment each
year by January 31, commencing in 1992, an Annual Plan regarding implementa-
tion of the UIP Program; and (b) take into account the comments and sugges-
tions, if any, made by the Association in the implementation of that program.
The project would provide support for USIP by financing the costs of training,
and communications (IEC), s"ringes, needles, vaccines, immunization cards,
vehicles, and operation and maintenance of the cold-chain and vehicles.
1/ This and other references to proposed outlays are tentative base costs
based on the Maternal and Child Health Plan for the 1991-95 period.



- 23 -
3.09      Diarrhoeal Disease Control and Management would have three major
elements and would continue the strategy of the National Program for the
Control of Diarrhoeal Diseases which is outlined in Annex 13. First, it calls
for educating mothers and communities to enable them to provide home care for
children suffering from diarrhoea, with homemade or home available fluids,
continuing feeding during diarrhoea, and recognizing early signs of dehydra-
tion. Second, it emphasizes improving the case management of diarrhoeal cases
at all health facilities by training health personnel involved in primary
health care (para 3.29), including those at district hospitals and medical
colleges. Fifteen Diarrhoeal Training cum Treatment Units have already been
established in the medical colleges of Assam, Andhra Pradesh, Gujarat,
Haryana, Karnataka, Kerala, Maharashtra, Madhya Pradesh, Orissa, Punjab,
Rajasthan, Tamil Nadu, West Bengal and New Delhi. The training includes
eliminating the use of unnecessary anti-diarrhoeal drugs, and the period
required at the triage area by treating the children with oral rehydration
therapy (ORT) at the hospital. Third, the program includes providing free
oral rehydration salts (ORS) packets at all health facilities as well as
expanding the Social Marketing of ORS through the private sector so that
packets are available at cheap rates in the community. The Government has
also developed plans for the social marketing of ORS including alternative
strategies to be tried in different parts of the country (Annex 14). While
many diarrhoeas can be treated by ORS and home preparations, there are life
threatening diarrheas made up of acute watery cases, persistent cases,
dysenteric cases and complicated cases (those with accompanying problems such
as measles, malaria and respiratory infection) which cawae the majority of
deaths. Among the acute watery cases, many respond to ORT but many others do
not. The policy conclusions are clear: namely, much more emphasis on primary
prevention and on a more comprehensive approach to case management of all
life-threatening episodes. In operational terms there are two major challeng-
es. First, to put in place an effective system for the management of all
diarrhoeas. This will involve going well beyond the focus on ORT, to embrace
nutritional management and the correct use of drugs. Education of parents and
training of health personnel will be key elements in a national program.
Second, and perhaps of greater priority, is the vigorous implementation of the
available cost-effective preventive interventions. In particular these
include measles vaccination, promotion of breast feeding, improved hygiene and
weaning practices, improved water and sanitation facilities, and the delivery
of new vaccines as they become available. 1/ The GOI periodically reviews
its strategy on Diarrhoeal Disease Control with WHO and UNICEF. The project
would support diarrhoeal disease management and control by financing training,
IEC, vehicles, and supplies (ORS).
3.10      Control of Acute Respiratory Infections.  ARI are a major cause of
morbidity and mortality in India, especially among children below five years
of age. (Para 1.08). The total episodes of ARI in the country are estimated
to be around 300 million per year in children up to five years. Most of the
A-RI episodes are of a mild and self-limiting uiature, while about 10? to 15? of
these, i.e., 30 million to 45 million, may progress to moderate/severe ARI or
pneumonia. ARI are caused by a variety of pathogens including bacteria,
viruses and mycoplasma. In India, most serious respiratory infections are
1/ Diarrhoeal Diseases in Disease Control Priorities in Developing
Countries; Maitines, Phillips and Feachem, 1991.



- 24 -
caused by bacteria which are still amenable to currently available anti-
microbials. It is, therefore, expected that with proper and timely institu-
tion of antibiotherapy, significant reduction in ARI mortality can be
achieved. The Government htas developed a clear policy on ARI in children and
has developed a program implementation strategy. This is outlined in
Amnex 15.
3.11      The main components of the ARI program would bet (a) home care for
coughs and colds; (b) administration of anti-microbials by para-medics to
children at home, subcenters and out-patient departments of hospitals; (c)
referral of severe cases to hospitals; (d) promotion of immunization to cover
all eligible children; and (e) health education training of staff including
doctors and para-medical workers. The services under the ARI Control Program
will be provided through the existing health network as a part of primary
health care. The training strategy would be reinforced by the creation of
district-level resource teams or District Training Teams (DTT) consisting of
doctors from medical colleges, chief medical officers and the district
immunization officers (para 3.29). T.ie project would support the supply of
the anti-microbial Cotrimoxazole and the training of workers.
3.12      Prophylaxis Against Blindness due to Vitamin A Deficiency.  It is
estimated that 40,000 young children become blind every year due to Vitamin A
deficiency. Also, Vitamin A deficiency is associated with a higher risk of
infections and an increase in childhood mortality. In measles, giving Vitamin
A immediately lirits eye damage and contributes to increased survival. The
national program against blindness due to Vitamin A deficiency was introduced
in 1970, but reaches less than 20? of the relevant population. The objective
of the national program during the 1991-95 period is to eliminate blindness
caused by Vitamin A deficiency and ensure that at least 80X of children aged
nine months to three years are covered. The Government has clarified that
priority would be given to children in the hyperendemic belt of the country;
that all children will receive the prescribed doses during the 1991-95 period;
and that the timing for administration of the first dose of Vitamin A at nine
months is more appropriate than at 6 months as originally instituted (para
2.17). These periodic doses of high potency Vitamin A provided in early
childhood need to be coupled with efforts to improve dietary sources of
Vitamin A for young children as a definitive long-term solution to the
deficiency. WHO guidelines for prophylaxis and treatment of Vitamin A
deficiency would be followed. The earliest symptoms of the deficiency would
be treated and the public informed of the early signs of Vitamin A deficiency
as well as the preventive measures which can be taken. The inadequate supply
of Vitamin A from local producers has also acted as a constraint on the
ability of the program to expand coverage beyond the current 20Z of the target
population. The Government has prepared a plan to overcome Vitamin A shortag-
es and the project would support this plan which includes diversifying the
source of supply to a larger group of Vitamin A formulators. The programs for
training, communication, logistics and supply, as well as the management
information system for HCH services, will include Vitamin A supplementation as
an integral component and be supported under the project.
3.13      Newborn Care.  Training support would be provided to improve care
for newborns at the home level with particular reference to aseptic delivery
practices, appropriate feeding and warmth. Traditional Birth Attendants
(TBAs) and other community health workers would be trained to identify low



- 25 -
birth weight babies and to give them priority attention (para 3.29). This
would complement similar efforts supported by the Bank under the Integrated
Child Development Services (ICDS) program.  A pilot project was implemented in
the Seventh Plan with UNICEF assistance under which TBAs were provided with
low cost color-coded portable scales for identifying low birth weight babies.
Suitable advice for the home level and/or referral was given. Ten districts
were involved in this intensive effort during the Seventh Plan. Similar
activities will be supported under the project.
3.14      Breast Feeding.  Early initiation to exclusive breast-feeding for
all infants for the first months of life will be promoted and the emphasis
given to changing behavior. Health workers, doctors, families and mothers
will be reminded of the unique properties of breast milk and the dangers of
anything less than exclusive breast-feeding. Steps will be taken to promote
change in hospital practices to facilitate rooming-in and maternal counsel-
ling. The communication strategy (para 3.32) for young child survival and
health will include breast-feeding as an integral component.
3.15      Diarrhoea management, control of ARI, Vitamin A supplementation, new
born care and breast feeding have been combined by the Government into a
technical package called UIP "Plus". Individual components have been tried
out on a pilot basis and in some cases on a larger scale, and the Government
plans now to implement the package in a more systematized way at the national
level. Since UIP has been successfully implemented, the UIP 'Plusw package
would focus around the immunization sessions since both mother and child would
be present for the immunization. Other potential *cycles of carew could be
envisioned in the future including one which begins with the adolescent girl
rather than the pregnant or lactating mother only. In any case, effectiveness
of program delivery depends on the systematic training of all health workers
and administrators (para 3.29), intensive and appropriate IEC activities (para
3.32), improved supervision of personnel, materials and supplies (para 3.34)
and an enhanced MIS (para 3.33). The GOI has developed guidelines for
States/districts which outline: (a) the implications of this expanded techni-
cal package on the work routines of ANMs, supervisors and doctors; (b) block
level supervision routines; (c) training of paramedical workers; and (d) the
logistics for the Family Welfare Program. Because the introduction of UIP
"Plus" would be phased by districts, during negotiations, the Government
provided assurances that it shall (a) prepare and submit to the Association
for its review and comment by January 31 each year, commencing in 1992, an
Annual Plan for the implementation of the UIP "Plus' program; (b) ensure that
no district is included in the project unless it has completed a district UIP
"Plus" Plan based on guidelines agreed with the Association (para 4.14); and
(c) take into account the comments and suggestions, if any, made by the
Association in the implementation of the said program. The project would
support UIP "Plus" by financing equipment, vehicles, training, medicines and
drugs, incremental staff salaries and operation and maintenance of vehicle
costs.
D. Safe Motherhood (Proposed Outlay US$97.21 million)
3.16      Several of the interventions which are classified as part of the
Child Survival strategy also impact maternal mortality and morbidity. The
Safe Motherhood Initiative which would be launched in a phased manner, would
include general improvement in maternity care and preventive programs for all



- 26 -
women, enhanced emphasis on pregnant women with high risk factors, strengthen-
ing essential obstetric care capabilities to treat obstetric complications,
while concurrently promoting birth-spacing and timing, a-a post-partum
counselling. Over the project period it is estimated tlhat over 115 million
lower-income women in rural areas and urban slums would iienefit from this
project, among whom, the major objective would be to brirng about significant
reductions in mortality and morbidity. In acknowledg)ng that women with no
risk factors can also develop obstetric complications, the project emphasizes
strengthening the health service delivery system to provide prompt and
adequate treatment of obstetric complications for all woman. The minimum core
of maternity care proposed for all women, particularly those at high-risk
would consist of: (a) management of anaemia; (b) strengthiening and further
development of community-based maternity care and also community-level
management and referral of emergencies during and after delivery, particularly
early recognition and referral for women with risk factors; and (c) provision
of support to first referral level institutions and enhancing their capacity
for essential obstetric functions.
3.17      Prophylaxis and Control of Nutritional Anaemia.  In India anaemia
affects around 702 of all women and as much as 852 of pregnant women. About
50? of pre-school children are also affected. Anaemia is frequeuitly a
contributing factor to complications such as hemorrhage and infection. During
childbirth blood loss of up to one liter will not kill a healthy woman, but a
loss of even 150 ml may be fatal to an anaemic woman. Anaemic women are poor
anaesthetic and operational risks. Anaemia lowers resistance to infection and
contributes to slow healing of wounds following obstetric surgery. The
National Nutritional Anaemia Prophylaxis Program (NNAPP) was launched in 1970
but is covering at most 30? of the target group with minimal impact. There
are multiple issues and constraints which affect this program including those
related to service utilization, distribution, supplies, absorption and
compliance.
3.18      Past policy was to provide one tablet containing 60 mg of elemental
iron and 0.5 mg of folic acid. In 1990, the Government reviewed its strategy
and endorsed a new policy of prevention and control (Policy on Control of
Nutritional Anaemia, MOHFW, January 1991). The policy was reviewed by the
appraisal mission and found appropriate. However, it has not been implemented
yet because of supply related issues including the supply of 100 mg elemental
iron tablets. The GOI's policy can be summarized as follows: (a) all pregnant
women will be given 120 mg of iron and folic acid for 100 days at a health
clinic or during a home visit by a health worker; (b) clinical assessment for
anemia during immunization sessions; (c) those found anaemic will be recom-
mended to take 180 mg of iron and folic acid; (d) women with a history of worm
infestation will be de-wonmed; and (e) dietary advise for consumption of
adequate quantities of iron-rich foods will be given. Recommended doses for
under-five preschool children and nursing mothers are also provided.
3.19      Under the project, it is proposed to increase coverage to 85? of
pregnant women and 502 of young anaemic children through improved and expanded
distribution through the subcenters, PHCs and CHCs as well as increased
collaboration with the ICDS program and the use of Anganwadis. A pilot
project would also be undertaken in a few blocks to test the use of Hemoglobin
meters as a technique for determining anaemii. The project would also
recognize that there are certain areas where IFA supplementation may not be



- 27 -
effective because of the incidence of wonms. The Government has proposed that
in all hyper-endemic areas, i.e., any district where over 60% of the popula-
tion has worms, de-worming efforts would be undertaken to supplement the
distribution of IFA tablets. Improved quality, packaging and distribution of
iron and folic acid tablets will also be supported.
3.20      Birth Spacing and Timing.  The importance for the health of women of
delaying marriage and first pregnancy until adulthood is critical. In
coordination with other parts of MOHFW i.e. Family Planning Division and other
Government Departments, efforts would be made to enhance public awareness to
postpone marriage until after the age of 18 years. The spacing of births, by
a variety of methods, wl.ll be wtdely promoted as a key element of the health
and well-beitig of women. Three specific programs of the National Family
Welfare Progeam address birth spacing and timing. The national IEC efforts as
well as the many local IEC efforts continue to stress birth spacing. Under
the Eighth Plan, this is to be expanded ard will be further supported under
the Seventh Population Project. The Social Marketing of Contraceptives
(condoms and oral pills) (SMC) through the private sector is the second major
effort to promote birth spacing and this too is being supported by the Seventh
Population Project. Annex 16 describes the SMC program. The third program
calls for the early but phased introduction of newer temporary methods of
contraception.. The GOI has under serious consideration the introduction of
Norplant 6, the injectable Net-Oen, and the weekly oral pill Centchroman. The
various protocols have been submitted to the Drug Controller for approval,
training materials are being prepared, and information programs being insti-
tuted. The project would build on and benefit from these programs. Communi-
ty-level health workers would be trained to educate community members regard-
ing reproduction, sex education, and to enhance understanding of risk factors
related to spacing and timing of births. Particular attention would be given
to training health workers in public and private sectors on the specific
techniques of reversible contraceptive methods. Post-partum contraceptive
counselling would also be enhanced from current levels. Family life educa-
tion, featdring the voluntary control of fertility, will be supported not only
through health, but also through formal education and literacy classes aimed
at adolescent girls, farmers cooperatives, and various community organiza-
tions. These efforts while not new, will be related to the Safe Motherhood
initiative to reduce maternal mortality and morbidity. Improvement in the
delivery of contraceptive supplies through related MCH supply routes would be
developed and coordinated with HOHFW schemes. The Government is already
submitting to the Bank for review and comment on an annual basis under the
Seventh Population Project, an Annual Action Plan for Social Marketing of
Contraceptives and IEC.
3.21      Strengthening and Further Development of Community Based Maternity
Care. The Traditional Birth Attendant (TBA) forms part of a community-based
maternity care system with clear links to the subcenter level of the health
service delivery system. They work independently and are paid by the people
that they serve. However, to encourage them to be known to the health
service, upgrade their quality, and motivate them, the health services pay
them a modest fee for each reported delivery. Since 70Z of all deliveries
continue to take place at home, and in order to raise the quality of home-
based delivery care, Traditional Birth Attendants (TBAs) would continue to be
trained as a medium-term strategy (para 3.29). Support would be provided for
more effective methods of training, support and supervision of TBAs. Special



- 28 -
attention would also be paid to refresher training, given the role of TBAs in
the early recognition of complications of pregnancy, delivery and the postna-
tal period.
3.22      Because in some areas deliveries are carried out primarily by family
members, another approach would be to provide basic information to women at
the community-level on the importance of prenatal care, safe delivery tech-
niques and postnatal care. They would also be introduced to the concept and
simple practices associated with the early recognition of complications of
pregnancy, intranatal and the postnatal period. Additional resources would be
sought at the cc munity level through the use of female volunteers to serve as
community resources for information and advice, and together with the TBA
where appropriate, facilitate improved access and use of maternal care
services. Targeted IEC approaches to the general community would enhance
social and practical support for the effective development of community based
maternity care (para.3.32).
3.23      Located at the community level, the subcenter would function as a
key element in community-based maternity care and be strengthened through
additional and refresher training to the Auxiliary Nurse Midwife (ANM) and the
provision of essential equipment and supplies. Its function would consist
primarily of being a center with a village outreach for MCH/FP, with particu-
lar emphasis on early recognition and referral of complications of pregnancy,
delivery and the post natal period. The subcenter supervises and supports
TBAs in the local area. District specific guidelines for referral of women
with risk factors and for emergency referral for obstetric complications have
been defined by GOI. Monitoring and recording responsibilities for community-
based maternity care would be among the routine tasks of the ANM (para.3.33).
The provision of a Contingency Transport Fund at block and district levels to
facilitate emergency obstetric evacuations will be supported under the
project.
3.24      Provision of Support to First Referral level Institutions and
Strengthening Essential Obstetric Care Capabilities CEOC). Because of the
critical role of first referral level institutions in the management of high-
risk women, and the provision of emergency life-saving obstetric care,
essential and affordable obstetric services would be strengthened at selected
CHCs, and at district and subdistrict hospitals. Support would include
specialist skills training and the corresponding provision of medical equip-
ment and supplies for strengthening essential obstetric medical and surgical
capabilities. Support would also be provided for ancillary services of
laboratory, anaesthesia, blood transfusion, transportation and pre-delivery
accommodation. The GOI, with the assistance of UNICEF, completed a facility
survey prior to negotiations to determine first referral level health facility
requirements especially the EOC, in the districts selected for the first phase
of the Safe Motherhood Initiative. In the State of Bihar, Uttar Pradesh,
Madhya Pradesh Punjab, Jammu and Kashmir, Gujarat, Haryana and Andhra Pradesh,
the Association is already providing support for the strengthening of
subcenters, PHCs and CHCs.
3.25      Paramedical staff at PHC and referral levels would also receive
technical skills training. In addition, medical and paramedical staff would
receive training in project planning, implementation, management, and monitor-
ing. Attention would be given to supervisory and support mechanisms necessary



- 29 -
to ensure effective functioning of the referral mechanisms linking referral
facilities to those at lower levels. Management of obstetric emereencies
would include development of community level alarm and transport sy2tems,
adapted to local conditions. The Government engaged consultant services to
prepare a Safe Motherhood Model Plan prior to negotiations. This will facili-
tate the development of district-specific Safe Motherhood plans for implemen-
tation beginning in 1992. The Government also undertook a series of workshops
with selected States prior to negotiations to strengthen the details of imple-
mentation of the Safe Motherhood component. During negotiations, the Govern-
ment provided assurances that it shall (a) by January 31 of each year commenc-
ing in 1992, prepare in consultation with the Association and in accordance
with a phased implementation schedule agreed with the Association, a draft
Safe Motherhood Plan, and submit it to the Association for review; (b) by
January 31 of each year commencing in 1992, prepare and furnish to IDA en
annual implementation plan for the Safe Motherhood Program for the following
fiscal year of the Borrower; and (c) implement the Safe Motherhood Plan in a
timely manner and based on the Safe Motherhood Model Plan prepared by MOHFW
acceptable to the Association. An outline of a Safe Motherhood Plan is shown
in Annex 17.
3.26      At national and regional levels, Maternal Mortality Review Commit-
tees would be established to monitor, evaluate and assir' the MOHFW in the
development of future strategies and the reporting system for maternal
mortality and morbidity. During negotiations, the Government provided an
assurance that it shall, in consultation with the Association, establish by
April 1, 1992, National and Regional Maternity Mortality Review Committees
comprising such members and having such functions and powers necessary to
monitor, evaluate and assist the MOHFW in implementation of the Safe Mother-
hood program. The project would support Safe Motherhood by financing equip-
ment including disposable delivery kits, MCH supplies, civil works, vehicles,
drugs, vitamins, expert services and training. The incremental salaries of
additional staff, fees to TBAs, and operation and maintenance costs for cold
chain equipment and vehicles would be financed on a declining scale.
S. Institutional Systems DeveloPmmet (Proposed Outlay US$108.74 million)
3.27      The Child Survival and Safe Motherhood interventions will be
supplemented and supported by activities covering training including upgrading
of State/district level health management and planning capacity, information,
education and communications (IEC), management information system (MIS),
materials and supplies management (logistics) and health equipment mainte-
nance.
3.28      Training.  The existing training infrastructure for health personnel
will be used to impart training.  It will build on the successful efforts of
UNICEF in training for UIP under the Seventh Plan (para 2.18). Modifications
in training methodologies will be made to reflect the integrated approach of
health workers to their work in UIP Plus and Safe Motherhood. The strategy of
a closer link between training and service delivery proven effective in the
immunization program will be expanded to include UIP Plus and Safe Motherhood
elements. This includes national and State core resource training teams" and
the development and use of training mcdules for all interventions of the
program. Modules have been developed already for intensified Dai training,
and work begun on modules for ARI training. The project will assist in



- 30 -
improving modules developed earlier for immunization and ORT programs by
redesigning them to eliminate overlaps and inconsistencies. Modules and other
instructional materials for Safe Motherhood interventions will be designed
under the project. The status of trained family welfare personnel is shown in
Annex 5, Section 3. Also shown are the numbers and categories of workers to
be trained, as well as the topics, location and duration of training under the
project. These tr&ining strategies and modifications are consistent with the
policy, organizational and curriculum changes of the Government supported by
IDA under the Sixth Population Project. In 1989, the GOI issued the Essential
Elements of the Family Welfare Training Program which called for a new
emphasis on training (Annex 5. Section 4).
3.29      New skills training will cover immunization including cold chain,
stores management, surveillance, management of diarrhoeal diseases, control of
ARI, safe motherhood including referral for obstetric interventions when
appropriate, control of Vitamin A deficiency and anaemia, new born care, ante-
natal care, care at birth, birth spacing and timing. Specific safe motherhood
skills such as risk assessment and management, prevention and management of
obstetric emergencies and guidelines for referrals would be inicluded. In
addition to technical courses, training would also include a managerial
component which addresses the organization of the work of field staff,
supervision, and the planning and administration of health programs at State,
district and village levels. The District Training Team as developed under
and stupported under the Sixth and Seventh Population Projects, would be used
to deliver this training after their own training of trainers course provided
by the "Core Training Resource Groups." Training materials would be developed
to facilitate learning by senior and mid-level managers, paramedical workers
and TBAs. The State Institutes of Health and Family Welfare (SIHFWs) and the
Consortium of Training Institutions, including the National Institute of
Health and Family Welfare (NIHFW), will contribute in this area. Training and
orientation programs for Private Voluntary Organizations and other NGOs also
will be supported. Continuous in-service and on-the-job training would be
emphasized. It should be noted that all externally-fun(led population projects
include training components. IDA assisted Sixth and Seventh Population
Projects would together train by 1997, about 600,000 family welfare workers
through public sector training institutions as well as wfth the assistance of
private organizations especially NGOs and PVOs. The GOI has already agreed to
submit to IDA for review, annual training plans covering the eight States
covered by these two projects.
3.30      Work Routines.  Under the project, an effort will be made to
systematize the work of field workers so that; (a) they concentrate on those
tasks which have maximum impact on mortality; (b) tasks are divided between
village level workers to maximize their effectiveness; and (c) workers concen-
trate most of their time on those clients who are at most risk of death or
illness. With regard to (a), the UIP Plus and Safe Motherhood package
consists of those interventions which will have maximum impact on the death
rate. With regard to (b), Annex 18 sets out the proposed distribution of
tasks between the various workers at the village level and for (c), it is
proposed to introduce two new work patterns under the project. First, the
monthly immunization sessions already held throughout the country will be
expanded into immunization/MCH sessions, at which UIP Plus interventions such
as Vitamin A and IFA will be made available. Second, Health Workers (F) would
devote the majority of their home visit time to regular contact with pregnant



- 31 -
and lactating women, especially the newly pregnant. They would also offer a
weekly ante-natal care clinic at the subcenter. Altogether, these work
patterns would take up about half of the HW (P) time in a month. The use of
the remainder of the health worker's time would be decided at the local level.
according to local priorities.
3.31      The focus on regular visits to pregnant and lactating women is of
key importance to the Safe Motherhood component of the project for identifica-
tion of risk factors, education and early recognition and referral. Health
and nutrition education during pregnancy and lactation will also lay the
foundation for good child care and improved child survival. However, the
adoption of these new work patterns, which follow regular monthly schedules,
has to be made more compatible with the current approach to family planning
work, which is intensively undertaken between January and March each year.
Substantial field experience in India now shows that a stronger focus on
regular provision of MCH care does not threaten the achievement of family
planning targets. On the contrary, the mothers confidence in health workers
built up through the provision of good MCH care actually facilitates the
achievement of these targets, especially for temporary methods. Prior to
negotiations, the Government agreed with the States on the proposed work
routines in a series of workshops organized by the Center. During negotia-
tions, the Government provided an assurance that it shall, by January 1, 1992,
institute on a national basis, job descriptions, distribution of tasks and
work routine changes for health workers satisfactory to the Association.
3.32      Information, Education and Communications.  The GOI has identified
the need to focus IEC efforts, within the MCH program, on increasing utiliza-
tion of MCH services and enhancing client home care health management.
Additionally, there is need to promote national awareness and generate demand
for Safe Motherhood initiatives. Lessons learned from the TINP, UIP and ICDS
area projects indicate that well designed and implemented IEC activities can
effectively stimulete awareness and behavioral change in support of health
service program iniziatives (paras 2.14-2.18). Under the project, communica-
tion activities would have three pivotal dimensions: i.e. better interpersonal
communication by health functionaries to promote behavior change; use of
community groups, e.g. Mahila Mandals and Mahila Mukya Swasthya Sang Commit-
tees to increase home-based health compliance and utilization of services; and
use of the mass media for reinforcing health care promotion messages. This
component will directly support GOI's MCH service delivery objectives by: (a)
enhancing the quality of health services by upgrading and increasing the
communication skills of key health functionaries; (b) developing integrated
programs supporting communication strategies and activities for community
outreach and demand generation; (c) enhancing community education by health
functionaries through a mix of media approaches targeted at specific audience
segments; (f) increasing program efficiency and impact through use of opera-
tions research and ongoing monitoring and tracking exercises; (g) generating
and sustaining client demand for specific health care including ORT, attended
deliveries, antenatal and postpartum services. The primary focus of IEC
activities will be service-delivery units, (subcenters, primary health
centers, hospitals, clinics). IEC activities will inform the public about the
services available, how they can avail themselves of services and motivate
them to use the services. Service delivery units will be used as linformation
posts' for a range of MCH and other development interventions. Existing
institutions/organizations will be supported to function as a network of



- 32 -
"primary health care resource centers" which will be developed (with emphasis
on MCH) to disseminate/provide information about health care for people, as
well as health technologies which could be used by individuals and organi-
zations. Prior to negotiations, the Government discussed and agreed with the
States on the IEC strategy for the project. An outline of the IkC strategy is
given in Annex 19.
3.33      Program Monitoring.  The program monitoring systems developed for
UIP will be expanded and strengthened. UIP 'Plus' interventions will be
included and extension of the reporting system to all referral facilities and
hospitals will also assist in monitoring disease outbreaks (essential surveil-
lance) as well as reporting Safe Motherhood services provided. Data will be
computerized and collated at district level through The National Informatics
Center (NIC) system being installed throughout India, and appropriate data
will be sent to both State and central levels. Much of the development of
this system has already baen completed.  Formuuts and manuals have been
prepared, and pilot implementation started in several States. It is expected
to become fully operational by the end of the project and will greatly
facilitate prompt reporting, data analysis, program decision-making and the
development of reliable feedback systems. Guidelines for Field-level Record-
keeping are outlined in Annex 20. Special emphasis will also be given to MIS
expansion under the program that was developed by the Nationa?. Industrial
Development Corporation (NIDC) for UIP. The district-tested NIDC system
monitors availability of equipment, distribution, replacement needs, equipment
and down-time and will be extended to cover the entire MCH package. The
family welfare program has been geared to report services provided rather than
coverage levels achieved. UIP has therefore assessed program coverage through
coverage evaluation surveys (CES) rather than service statistics. Some 825 of
these surveys have been carried out in various States since 1985. The surveys
will be modified to include additional selected services and will include the
-ollowing areas: (a) coverage per se; (b) source of coverage; (c) reasons for
utilization; (d) knowledge, attitudes and practices on the part of clients;
(e) disease surveillance of polio and neonatal tetanus; (f) gender-specific
data; (g) prevalence of sexually-transmitted disease (STD) in women in
different comnunities; and (h) prevalence of morbidity in women in different
communities. Supporting studies to assess implementation of specific aspects
of the UIP "Plus" and Safe Motherhood packages would also be supported. In
addition, and complementary to computerized MIS systems at district level and
above, and to coverage evaluation surveys (CES), the rationalization of field-
level recordkeeping and the development of the capacity to calculate local
service coverage rates, which have been objectives of IDA-supported population
projects, will continue. To achieve these goals, the Government provided
assurances during negotiations that it shall: (a) review the management
information systems currently used in its health and family welfare programs
in order to assess their conformity wlth the functional guidelines agreed with
the Association (Annex 20); and (b) indicate to the Association by April 1,
1992, the modifications it will make to its systems to bring these into
conformity with such guidelines.
3.34      Materials and Sugplies Management.  The project would support the
provlslon of essential supplies such as disposable delivery kits, syringes and
needles, medicines and equipment. Steps will be taken to ensure national
level availability, supplemented by a well designed and tested logistics
system to ensure availability at the level of implementation. The system of



- 33 -
storage, distribution and logistic support developed under the Family Welfare
Program and UIP will be used to the extent possible. At present, IFA tablets
are located at Regional Medical Stores and released periodically to various
health facilities. The logistics for both ORS and Vitamin A differ in both
storage and delivery schedules. States would be given assistance for improved
systems of planning, ordering, monitoring stock levels and usage. Special
efforts will also be made to support supplies at the village, subcenter,
primary health center and first level of referral, for interventLons for Safer
Motherhood and child surviva. and health.  The WHO Guidelines "Essential
Obstetric Functions at First Referral Level' would be used. Facilities for
conducting deliveries for at-risk mothers will be strengthened by standardiz-
ing simple instruments and pharmaceutical sets for birth support, at subcenter
and primary health center levels. A Report of the Expert Committee on the
Standardization of Drugs and Equipment at Subcenters, PHC and CHCs has already
been prepared, reviewed and found satisfactory by the appraisal mission. The
medicines recommended for all interventions have been agreed to by the Indian
Medical Association (IMA) and the Indian Council for Medical Research (ICMR).
The quality of medicines which are manufactured, marketed and sold in India
are ensured by the Drug Directorate. T'he process by which drug quality is
ensured in India is outlined in Annex 21. Self-explanatory wall posters which
go into each standard set which will show what each medicine and its supply
is, and what are the indications and precautions for its use. These will be
developed so that they are correctly used by health workers. A 'visual
formulary' for each State on basic drugs/supplies showing use, indications,
precautions and related aspects will be developed.
3.35      Health Equipment Maintenance.  During 1985-89, 26,000 ice lined
refrigerators and freezers, 125 walk-in-freezers and coolers plus other non-
electrical cold chain equipment were provided. Support would be provided to
the State Governments for maintaining and repairing this equipment to ensure
potent vaccines for the immunization program. This would be an expansion of a
program supported by UNICEF under the Seventh Five-Year Plan. During 1991-95,
support will also be given to: (a) strengthen and further develop present
State Health Transport Organizations (SHTOs), and Health Equipment Repair
Organizations (HEROs); (b) improve the efficiency of maintenance, personnel,
managers, supervisors and technicians through training and proper guidance;
(c) improve the efficiency of spare parts management; (d) cooperate with the
State governments in expanding the network of Mobile Maintenance Units (MMUs)
and Static Maintenance Units (SMUs), auxiliary workshops and central workshops
for motor vehicles as well as for hoalth equipment repair; (e) assist states
in formulating their Plans of Action for improvement of existing maintenance
facilities; and (f) ensure proper maintenance and speedy repair of health
equipment and cold chain components; and organize HER services for the
maintenance and repair of cold chain components such as walk-in-coolers, deep
freezers, refrigerators and cold boxes. The UNICEF supported program will be
further reviewed and appropriate steps will be taken to strengthen and improve
maintenance. During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it
shall, by April 1, 1993, transfer the maintenance of the cold chain system to
the States.
3.36      Community Participation.  Given the complexity of the UIP Plus and
Safe Motherhood interventions, the project would include a community partici-
pation dimension to implementation. Community participation is defined as a
practical and systematic process of involving communities in the attempt to



- 34 -
reach project goals; i.e. increased child survival and reductions in maternal
mortality and morbidity. The process involves the integration of national and
State macro-planning objectives with micro-level district/village perspec-
tives. Past experience with both government and private sector-supported
health projects demcnstrate that a well-planned community participation
initiative can be built in and successfully implemented in a project. The
project would therefore include the following indicative activities which
would necessarily vary by State and district given the strength, interests and
capabilities of the various interest groups and communities. Activities
include the design and development of a series of community participation
strategies addressing: (a) Supporting Group Potential: Voluntary/NGO sector,
organized sector, industry/business sector, religious communities; (b) Common
Themes: emergency transport, scheduled caste/tribe, backward sector issues,
waiting homes, income-generation; and (c) Work-related systems: Prior to
negotiations, the Government discussed community participation as an integral
part of implementation with the States during workshops. A draft outline of a
community participation plan is included in the project file. During negotia-
tions, the Government provided an assurance that it shall cause the States to
institute district planning including community participation based on MOHFW
guidelines acceptable to the Association. This Plan would include an evalua-
tion of on-going activities and a description of the activities planned for
the new year. The project would finance the services of local consultants, as
well as the specific agreed activities to be undertaken by PVOs, NGOs and
community groups including Mahila and Yuvak Mandals and Panchayats.
3.37      To improve the quality of service delivery in the child survival and
safe motherhood programs, the staffing of family welfare institutions would
have to be strengthened. Both family planning and MCH programs have expanded
under the Seventh Five-Year Plan and training undertaken to enhance their
skills. Under the proposed program, a number of key staff would be appointed
and or deployed to complete the staffing. A Schedule of Deployment of Key
Additional Staff is given in Annex 22. During negotiations, the Government
provided an assurance that it shall appoint staff or cause the States to
appoint key additional staff according to a schedule satisfactory to the
Association. The project would support further development of family welfare
institutional systems to more effectively deliver child survival and safe
motherhood programs by financing civil works, equipment, vehicles, local
training and consultants, and supplies, operation and maintenance, and the
salaries of additional staff.
3.38      Part of the proposed project will be carried out in the State of
Jammu and Kashmir, which includes areas over which India and Pakistan have
been in dispute since 1947. In making the proposed credit to India, IDA does
not intend to prejudice the final determination of the claims of the parties
involved.
IV. PROJECT COST, FINANCING. IMPLEMENTATION AND DISBURSEMENTS
A. Costs
4.01      Summaries of Costs.  The total cost of the project is Rs. 10,687.70
million or US$329.58 million equivalent. A breakdown of costs by component and
categories of expenditure is summarized in Tables 4.1 and 4.2. Detailed costs



- 35 -
by component, categories of expenditure and time are given in Annex 23.
4.02        Basis of Cost Estimates.   The cost of the project is essentially
equal to the incremental costs between the proposed outlays on the national
MCH program 1991-1995 and the actual expenditures on that program for the
Seventh Five-Year Plan (1985-1990). In calculating that increment, ongoing
liabilities for salaries, the costs for the Medical Termination of Pregnancy
Program as well as the costs of customs duties and taxes have been excluded.
The detailed calculation of project costs is shown in Annex 24.

4.03     Customs Duties and Taxes.  All imported goods are subject to customs
duties and taxes. The estimated cost of the project includes import duties
and taxes estimated at about US$15.90 million (4.11).
4.04     Contingency Allowances.  Estimated project costs take account of
physical and price escalation contingencies. Physical contingencies are
estimated at 102 of all physical components and 5 for salaries of incremental
staff, training costs, consultants and operation and maintenance. Price
escalation contingencies are estimated as follows: for civil works, goods,
salaries, and technical assistance--foreign costs: 3.2S in FY92 through FY95;
local costs: 7.0 in FY92, 6.52 in FY93 and 5.51 in FY94 and 95.
4.05     Foreign Exchange Component.  The estimated foreign exchange component
of US$46.53 million is calculated on the basis of estimated foreign exchange
proportions as follows: (a) civil works 11Z1 (b) vehicles 92; (c) equipment
35X; (d) drugs and vaccines 102; (e) vitamins 402; and (g) operation and
maintenance of costs at 52.
B. Financing Plan
4.06     The cost of the national MCH program for the 1991-95 period is
US$922.63 million. The estimated cost of the project is US$329.58 million.
IDA and UNICEF would co-finance the project in parallel, with an IDA Credit of
US$214.53 million equivalent and a UNICEF grant of about US$67.80 million.
Much of the UNICEF funding would come from bilateral donors. Total external
financing would cover about 902 of project cost net of taxes and duties but
would only cover about 312 of the national MCH program for the 1991-95 period.
The GOI would finance the remaining project costs of US$47.3 million and all
taxes, equal to US$15.9 million. The financing plan by categories of expendi-
ture is shown in Annex 25. The UNICEF Grant (Plan of Operations 1991-1995)
was signed in May 1991. The fulfillment of the conditions precedent to the
effectiveness of the UNICEF agreement will be a condition of effectiveness of
the Credit.
C. Recurrent Costs and Sustainability Implications
4.07     When the Project is completed in FY95, it would require about
Rs. 1,524.2 million (US$44.8 million) annually in recurrent costs. These
expenditures would be borne fully by GOI. Assuming no growth in GOI expendi-
tures, except for the proposed investments in the project, it is estimated
that GOI expenditure on MCH would be about Rs. 31,569.80 million (US$1,291.38
million) for the Ninth Five-Year Plan Period (1995-1999). This would be
significantly less than the growth experienced previously from the Fifth to
Sixth and Sixth to Seventh Five-Year Plans. In this case, the recurrent cost
implication of the proposed project would be 3.42 of the projected 1995-1999
expenditures on maternal and child health. In addition, the recurrent cost
impact of the proposed project is equal to an almost negligible share of total
present GOI expenditures on health and family welfare. Additional information
on the calculation of project incremental costs is detailed in Annex 24. As
noted above, project costs and related external financing, were determined on
the basis of plan budget allocations to the national MCH program at certain
levels during the 1991-95 period. Thus, during negotiations, the Government
provided assurances that it shall: (a) maintain the levels of planned budget-
ary allocations for the National MCH program as agreed with the Association



- 37 -
for fiscal years 1991 through 1995; (b) consult with the Association before
proposing any major changes to such allocations; and (c) by November 30 of
each year commencing in 1992, furnish to the Association for its review, the
annual plan budget allocation for such program for the following fiscal year.
4.08     Sustainability.  Under the present financial circumstances in India,
it would be difficult to present a quantifiable set of indicators ensuring
availability of financial resources to sustain family welfare activities at
meaningful levels beyond the project's life cycle. However, in addition to
government assurances, several qualitative factors provide positive indica-
tions of program sustainability: (a) the proposed allocations for maternal and
child health represent a small share of total expenditures on health and
family welfare; (b) the Central Government finances one hundred percent of
capital and recurrent expenditures of the FWP/MCH program. All liabilities
will continue to be paid by the central government from its Plan budget.
Inasmuch as central government revenues are more stable than those of State
governments, the family welfare activities are less likely to experience major
fluctuations in resource allocations; (c) the immunization program (UIP) is
one among six special schemes comprising the 'Technology Mission' for which
the Central government has made a commitment to allocate financial resources
on a priority basis; (d) the Central Government launched the FWP program with
a distinct orientation to maternal and child health to redress the growing
inequitable distribution of health expenditures as States government exmendi-
tures favored second and tertiary level services (mainly hospitals) at the
expense of primary, preventive care services. Family Welfare activities
disproportionately benefit the poorer segment of the population as FWP
expenditures represent a larger share of total health expenditures in states
with lower per capita income compared with the more endowed States; and (e)
successful implementation of an integrated package of MCH activities under the
project would set in place an effective infrastructure across the country,
supported by institutional mechanisms, information, monitoring and evaluation
systems and trained staff that would not require duplication in future years,
thus easing the pressure for continued increases in financial outlays. The
cost of maintaining these efforts would increase but at much lower levels than
initial program set-up. In addition, the need to sustain the MCH program will
be especially important in times of fiscal constraint to assist in mitigating
the consequences of macroeconomic stabilization. Further details on project
sustainability are outlined in Annex 26.
D. Status of Preparation
4.09     Design and Construction of Health (PHCs, CHCs) and Storage Facilities
for Safe Motherhood. Preliminary designs for the construction of storage
facilities and additions to existing health facilities for the Safe Motherhood
Program and training already exist. The amount of additional facilities
required is based on the results of the Facilities Survey (para 3.25).
4.10     Sites.  Sites for the storage facilities have been identified in 15
districts. The existing health facilities to be extended for the purposes of
Safe Motherhood have been identified through the Facility Survey (para 3.24).
4.11     Equipment, Vehicle, Drugs. Vaccines and Consumable Material.  Lists
have been prepared by GOI and UNICEF and are based on WHO standards in use by
MOHFW and UNICEF. These were reviewed and found acceptable during appraisal.
4.12     Local Consultants, Studies, Training Plans, and Operational Research
A significant amount of preparation has already been completed by GOI and



- 38 -
UNICEF in these areas. Draft terms of reference for local consultants have
been prepared and the detailed work of developing curricula for the various
types of training has begun. Training and communication needs assessments as
well as baseline surveys have been started in some States as part of the on-
going IDA-assisted Population projects. A list of operational research topics
has been prepared. Revised work routines and schedules for health workers
have been prepared.  Draft IEC, Community mobilization, Safe Motherhood and
UIP "Plus' Guidelines have also been developed, and are being discussed with
States/districts prior to the development of district-specific implementation
programs. Arrangements for the procurement of goods and services are in place
(para 4.22-4.28) as is the logistics system to transport and store the drugs,
medicines and other supplies.
S. Project Implementation
4.13     Administration.  The Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW)
would be responsible for implementing the project. It has a Division headed
by a Joint Secretary, which is responsible for coordinating and monitoring the
implementation of all externally-aided projects. At present, the Joint
Secretary in charge of this Division also has the responsibility for the
management of the Maternal and Child Health component of the National Family
Welfare Program. Coordination of activities between the various Divisions of
the Ministry especially as regards Safe Motherhood is being enhanced under the
project. An outline of the mechanism of coordination between MOHFW and the
Women and Child Welfare Department (WCD) and the ICDS program is shown in
Annex 27. During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it
shall (a) maintain its Maternal and Child Health Care Advisory Committee and
(b) by January 1, 1992 institute mechanisms, satisfactory to the Association,
for purposes of coordinating the activities of the MCH program and Integrated
Child Development Services Program under the project (Annex 26).
4.14     At the State level, the project would be implemented by the respec-
tive Directorates of Health and Family Welfare under the administrative
control of the State Secretary of Health and Family Welfare. No special
Project Implementation Units (PIU) are envisaged to facilitate the implementa-
tion since the project represents a part of the program for which they have
been established to implement. Additional staff would need to be appointed to
cover the expansion of activities (Annex 22). The project also gives special
attention to the generation of pro'ect implementation ideas and modalities
based on the differences among and between various districts. The need for
this flexibility is recognized as each district which is selected for UIP
*Plusw and Safe Motherhood would have to develop a district-specific plan. The
Government discussed and agreed on the guidelines for district planning with
specific States prior to negotiations. During negotiations, the Government
provided an assurance that it shall cause the Statea to institute district
planning including community participation and civil works programs based on
MOHFW guidelines acceptable to the Association (Annex 28). In addition, to
facilitate the execution of the project, the Union Government will obtain from
each state participating in the project a Letter of Undertaking, indicating
that it will execute its part of the project in a manner consistent with the
project design.
4.15     Civil works.  For the implementation of the civil works portion of
the project, architectural designs will be prepared by consultant architects
and construction would be carried out by the PWD in the States. During



- 39 -
negotiations, the GOI provided an assurance that it shall cause the States to
(a) institute district planning including civil works programs; and (b)
provide the required budgetary support for undertaking civil works under the
project.
F. Monitoring, Evaluation and Operational Research
4.16     At present, the MOHFW collects information on all Schemes which make
up the national family welfare program. This level of monitoring would be
further supplemented under the program. Currently, each scheme has some form
of built-in evaluation, and the implementing institutions utilize these to
make adjustments when and if necessary in their delivery of services. The en-
hanced MIS (para 3.33) would be designed to upgrade this function. Overall
monitoring and evaluation, i.e., use of credit proceeds, achievement of
objectives, etc., would be undertaken by MOHFW with the assistance of other
specialized institutions attached to the MOHFW. As part of its normal
reporting responsibility, the MOHFW would submit Semi-Annual Progress Reports
to IDA. The format and content of these reports would be devised jointly, and
would have as their principal objective, the adequate monitoring of activi-
ties. As regards evaluation, the Government has discussed with the Associa-
tion, key indicators (Annex 29) and also indicated a willingness to undertake
special studies i.e. operational research to determine whether program
objectives are being achieved. Rapid Low Cost Studies (RLCS) as a technique
to achieve this end would be utilized. UNICEF has used this mechanisms as
part of the UIP monitoring system. Under the project, a number of these
studies would be undertaken. The Operational Research Program of UNICEF and
proposed studies to be undertaken during the project are outlined in Annex 30.
During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it shall (a)
utilize the key performance indicators satisfactory to the Association for
evaluating the progress of the project (Annex 29); (b) by January 1, 1992,
furnish to the Association for review, a phased program of operational
research and studies, including proposed implementation arrangements (Annex
30); (c) furnish to the Association annual monitoring reports and end-of-
project summative evaluation reports focusing on the impact of the project;
and (d) take necessary corrective action as might be required in implementa-
tion of the integrated package of services of UIP 'Plus' and Safe Motherhood
Programs to achieve project objectives.
G. Disbursements
4.17     Disbursement Percentages.  The IDA Credit would be disbursed against
90? of expenditures on civil works, 10O? of foreign CIF and local ex-factory
cost or 802 of other local costs of equipment, vehicles, medicines, vaccines,
vitamins, IEC materials, disposable delivery kits and MCH medical supplies,
90? of consultant services and training and an average of about 75? (902 of
local expenditures until March 31, 1993 and 602 thereafter) of total incremen-
tal expenditures on salaries of additional staff, reporting fees for TBAs, and
operation and maintenance costs of cold chain equipment and vehicles. Since
IDA is financing the incremental costs of a large program, disbursements could
have been designed so that it would be disbursing against a small share of the
total program. However, this would have resulted in a very large number of
expenditures, especially those covering operating costs. Disbursement
percentages have therefore been used in the disbursement schedule which are
consistent with those used for population and nutrition projects in India. In
order to ensure that IDA remains engaged in the project over its entire length



- 40 -
and does not disburse a disproportionate share of the credit on salaries, the
cumulative amount of money that can be disbursed on the project each year has
been capped; the cumulative amount of total disbursements which can be used
for incremental operating costs has also been capped. In addition, IDA would
review with GOI actual expenditure on these programs each year as well as the
planned annual expenditure for the next year (para 4.07). Together with the
disbursement caps this will ensure that IDA financing is confined to the
incremental costs of program development (Annex 31). During negotiations, it
was agreed that disbursements against expenditures incurred by any State would
be made only after the Government has obtained and submitted to the Associa-
tion, a Letter of Undertaking from that State.
4.18     Reguired Documentation.  Disbursements in respect of contracts for
civil works, goods, consultant's services and training valued less than
US$75,000 equivalent and for incremental operating costs would be made against
statements of expenditure (SOEs) certified by MOHFW. Documentation would be
retained by the MOHFW and the States and made available for review by IDA
during the course of supervision missions. All other disbursements would be
made against fully documented withdrawal applications.
4.19     Special Account.  In order to accelerate disbursements in respect of
IDA's share of expenditures pre-financed by GOI, and in order to allow for
direct payment of other eligible local and foreign expenditures, a Special
Account would be opesed in the Reserve Bank of India with an authorized
allocation of US$18.0 million equivalent to cover four months of expected
requirements for IDA financed items.
4.20     Retroactive Financing up to US$21.0 million, or 9.8Z of the proposed
Credit, is recommended to cover eligible expenditures after November 30, 1990.
Detailed discussion of the 1990/91 MCH program was held in October 1990.
Procurement arrangements, as well as the purposes for vihich the items were
being used were reviewed and found appropriate.
4.21     Disbursement Profile.  The proposed IDA credit would be disbursed
largely over four years with a small amount in the fifth year. This is
realistic given the fact that the project covers four years of development of
the national MCH program. The appraisal mission carefully examined the
expenditures incurred after November 30, 1990 and evaluated those investments
which are planned for the four years (April 1991 - March 1995) to determine
their level of preparedness. Specific emphasis and analysis was given to the
procurement of vaccines and drugs, the limited size of the civil works program
and to the use of WHO approved standard lists of equipment and medical
supplies. The project is expected to be completed on March 31, 1995 and the
Credit closed on September 30, 1995. The standard profile for population
(Family Planning and HCH) projects for all Regions is eight and a half years
and in Asia is nine years. The nature of the project, the status of prepa-
ration, and the measures have been taken to expedite procurement and disburse-
ment reinforce the justification for a four-year disbursement profile.
H. Procurement
4.22     Procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 4.3.  Annex 32
outlines in greater detail, the justification for the methods of procurement



- 41 -
to be used. Contracts for civil works (US$23.50 million) would be carried out
by force account. Because of the small value of contracts (maximum value of
US$0.1 million equivalent), no foreign firm is expected to participate.
4.23     Contracts for equipment, medical supplies, IEC materials and pharmac-
euticals (US$147.75 million) ,w.uld be procured partly by IC- from UNICEF's
central stores overseas and partly through LCB or other pr.cedures acceptable
to IDA. Procurement would be bulked to the maximum extent possible and any
individual contract exceeding US$200,000 equivalent would be procured using
ICB. The average size of each package will be small however, and it is likely
that the only ICB package will be for glass syringes. For all contracts over
US$200,000 procured using ICB, a 15? domestic preference or the prevailing
import duties, whichever is lower, would be granted to qualifying local
manufacturers. Because MOHFW is attempting to standarize the type of four-
wheel drive off-road vehicle it uses, vehicles (US$7.40 million) would be
procured by UNICEF using direct contracting procedures to ensure a reliable
supply of spares and availability of maiatenance.
4.24     Vaccines.  The justification for the methods to be used for the
procurement of specific vaccines (US$44.6 million) is described in greater
detail in Annex 32. In line with 'WHO's policy objective that developing
countries like India develop a domestic manufacturing capacity for essential
vaccines, contracts for all vaccines except for BCG and measles, regardless of
value, would be procured mainly from local WHO approved sources using LCB
procedures acceptable to IDA. Only shortfalls in local supply would be met by
foreign suppliers selected using international shopping procedures. The use
of LCB is justified because the risk of degradation and loss from heat
variations or delays during delivery to the approximately 120,000 regional
subcenters where the vaccines are to be administered mandates use of sources
located as close to the end-v.er as possible. All vaccines must be shipped in
a refrigerated state, and even a brief exposure to heat or extreme cold,
drastically reduce their shelf-life. To be competitive, a foreign supplier
would have to invest in additional transportation and 'cold-chain, equipment
to keep the vaccines properly refrigerated from the point they leave the plant
abroad, during air shipment to India, clearance through Customs and up to the
point of delivery at the regional walk-in coolers. It would also have to
accept the risk of loss resulting from any delays that occur during the
initial delivery and customs clearance process prior to handover to the GOI.
LCB will also allow greater flexibility in packaging and labeling, adjustment
of volumes to reflect programmed dosages and maintenance of vaccine invento-
ries at or below the cold storage holding capacity. Adequate quality controls
are maintained by the MOHFW and WHO, and the prices of all locally manufac-
tured vaccines on the WHO list of "essential vaccines" are controlled so they
are reasonable.
4.25     MOHFW would be allowed to procure BCG and measles vaccines (US$7.61
million) from the sole WHO-approved manufacturers of the strains of vaccines
needed for the program using direct contracting procedures. Limitation to
local sources is justified for the same reasons of WHO policy and logistics as
outlined above. There is only one WHO-approved manufacturer of BCG in India;
BCG Laboratories, India. Regarding measles, there is only one source in the
world of the E-Z strain which is recommended by WHO for use in the program, a
Yugosla'-ian firm which has a collaboration agreement with an Indian manufac-
turer.
