4.26     Vitamins (US$3.90 million).  The procurement of Vitamin A will be
through LCB procedures because of the specific nature of the program. Vitamin
A in liquid rather than capsule form is preferred because of ease of adminis-
tration to young childrer. during clinic visits, to ensure higher compliance
and because capsules are subject to degradation under extreme temperatures.
The liquid formulation is not available elsewhere in the world.
4.27     Contracts for the purchase of items valued at below US$200,000
equivalent each may be awarded on the basis of LCB procedures acceptable to
IDA up to an aggregate of US$69.90 million equivalent. Other items valued at
less than US$50,000 equivalent each, may be procured up to an aggregate of
US$28.0 milllon oquivAlent using international and/or local shopping.  All
items procured using ICB, as well as the first three LCB contracts estimated
to cost the equivalent of US$75,000 or more per contract each year for
vaccines, medical supplies, vitamins and pharmaceuticals will be subject to
IDA's prior review. Approximately 351 of the value of the IDA Credit will be
subject to prior review. With the exception of the pharmaceutical ORS, which
will be pu.rchased directly by the State Government from the numerous local
suppliers available regionally, procurement for the IDA-financed portion of
the Credit will be handled by MOHFW in conjunction with the Government's
supply agency, the Directorate General of Supplies and Disposal (DGS&D) or
with technical assistance from other qualified agencies, such as UNICEF.
During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it shall, by
Decemiber 31, 1991, enter into an agreement with UNICEF, satisfactory to the
Association, for assistance in: (a) the procurement of selected equipment and
vehicles; and (b) development and institution of a program to improve the
capacity of MOHFW and the States regarding procurement and logistics for other
equipment and materials necessary for the effective implementation of the MCH
program.
I. Accounting and Auditing
4.28     The project would be subject to normal GOI accounting and auditing
procedures which are considered satisfactory to IDA. The audit reports for
the on-going population projects are in a form satisfactory to IDA and would
be used for this project. The MOHFW would maintain a separate project
account, and a semi-annual statement of expenditures provided to IDA. During
negotiations, the 6vI and each State agreed that: (a) accounts and financial
statements of the project including Statements of Expenditure (SOE) and the
Special Account for each fiscal year would be prepared and audited by indepen-
dent auditors acceptable to IDA; (b) documentation supporting statements of
expenditures would be maintained in accordance with sound accounting practic-
es, be maintained at least one year after the completion of the audit for the
fiscal year in which the last withdrawal was made and a separate opinion on
SOEs be included in the annual audit; and (c) certified copies of the audited
accounts and financial statements for each fiscal year, in the form of a
Consolidated Report certified by the auditor, would be furnished to IDA as
soon as available, but not later than nine months after the end of each fiscal
year. All of the audited accounts of on-going Population and Nutrition
Projects which are due have been received by IDA.



- 44 -
V. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
5.01     Since the proposed project would cover the national MCH program for
the 1991-95 period with some retroactivity, there are several Jmmediate
benefits. First, in the absence of sustaining the immunization program,
another 250,000 children would die from tetanus, 500,000 would die from
measles and 170,000 would risk paralysis each year due to poliomyelitis.
Second, the project would assist in sustaining the level of MCH services
during a period when the scale of publicly financed social services could be
severely and negativel7 impacted by GOI budgetary constraints. Third, the
project would contribute to a GOI review of its current national MCH policies
and to appropriate adjustments in terms of content, targeting and delivery.
Fourth, the project period allows the GOI the time and opportunity to under-
take vigorous trials at the district level of the UIP *Plus' and Safe Mother-
hood models which are presently being developed. The ultimate long-term
benefit of the project is that it is a necessary precursor to a more efficient
and effective national family welfare program which would lead to significant
reductions of infant, young child and maternal morbidity, mortality, and
disability thereby enhancing the quality of life. Reduced maternal and infant
mortality rates would have a significant favorable impact on fertility through
contributing to the willingness to accept the small family norm. The program
wr-ld alleviate women's suffering through improved care and expanded access to
health care. It would increase mothers' survival, which is crucial to the
well-being of children, whose mortality rate increases by 250Z when a mother
dies. Giver the significant role women play in economic production and social
development, the death of a woman during her most productive years imposes a
substantial loss on the family and society. The improved health of adults
would show rewards in the productivity of labor, and as morbidity decreases,
productivity would improve. Healthier children would learn more, and dropout
and repetition would decrease hence increasing the efficiency of the school
system. The reduced disease burden of women and children will allow for
better care of the truly sick by reducing the workload of health staff and
allowing scarce resources to be directed to those most in need. Finally, the
direct cost benefits are substantial, since the costs of treating the compli-
cations of pregnancy and childbirth are far higher than the costs of prevent-
ing such complications and/or treating them at an early stage.
B. Risks
5.02     There are some risks to the project.  It calls for a significant
increase in the number of activities to be undertaken at the point of service
delivery as well as a change in the way the Health Worker and Supervisor
perform their tasks. While training can go a long way in making this possible
and the GOI has demonstrated under the Se%enth Plan with UIP that it can
mobilize and train over 2.5 million workers, to deliver immunizations promot-
ing enhancements in service quality on the scaie and depth proposed would be
challenging. Previous IDA support to the GOI under the Population Projects to
assist in the development of regular on-the-job training supported by appro-
priate supervision should alleviate this risk. The work to be undertaken by
GOI in conjunction with the States to review the job descriptions and work
routines of health workers, particularly the female Multipurpose Worker as
well as the flexibility in project design and the close monitoring devices



- 45 -
which are built in to each component, will also go a long way in minimizing
this risk. The project also builds on the extensive collaboration between GOI
and UNICEF and the fact that UNICEF has a wide network of offices and person-
nel in India who will actively participate in project monitoring and supervi-
sion. There are also risks associated with the Safe Motherhood Program. If
there is insufficient commitment at the local level because of socio-cultural
and other traditions, some implementation aspects of the Safe Motherhood
program may be delayed. The project would include activities to increase
awareness and action through involvement of the Panchayats, Women's Groups
including Mahilla Mandals and Private Voluntary Organizations. In addition,
IEC programs would be designed to specifically address some local customs
which are dangerous to women's survival. Also, local communities, through
their representatives and/or with the assistance of PVOs, would be mobilized
as an integral part of the planning for Safe Motherhood in that community.
Other risks relate to the capacity of individual States to implement programs
in a timely manner. These include delays in procurement and difficulties in
quickly developing appropriate IEC and training programs. Appropriate
measures have been taken and are in place to see that these risks are mini-
mized. These include the joint development of detailed action plans for
training, logistics, IEC, social mobilization and procurement, and agreeing
with GOI on the implementation phasing of these action plans. IDA has and is
continuing to support the strengthening of State capacity to deliver family
welfare services under seven Population (Family Welfare) projects. Finally,
there are questions of sustainability. The proposed project deals with
Government's highest health priority and the annual ,llocation for the MCH
program would be agreed with IDA. In addition, the recurrent cost implica-
tions of the project would be a negligible proportion of total GOI expenditure
on health. These factors should reduce concerns about sustainability.
VI. AGREEMENTS RE&CHED
6.01     During negotiations, the Government provided assurances that it
shall:

UIP "Plus' Program (para 3.15), and the Safe Motherhood Program (para

any, made by the Association in the implementation of the said
programs;
(b) (i) ensure that no district is included in the project unless it has
completed a district UIP 'Plus, Plan based on guidelines agreed with
the Association (para 3.15);
(c) establish in consultation with the Association by April 1, 1992, Na-

such members and having such functions and powers necessary to
monitor, evaluate and assist MOHFW in the implementation of the Safe

equivalent) to India at standard IDA terms with 35 years maturity.

CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Organization of Medical and Health Services
1.      Policy-making for the family welfare program is the joint responsibility
of the Central Government and the States, while program implementation is
primarily the responsibility of the States and Union Territories. Since the
program is of national importance, it is funded entirely by the Central
Government. At the Center, the Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW) is
responsible for overall program management. Within the Ministry, both the
Department of Family Welfare and the Department of Health Services are headed by
Secretaries of GOI.
2.      All States and Union Territories have separate Departments of Health and
Family Welfare, but with mild variations among them. The basic structure
consists of a Directorate of Health Services which functions under the overall
control of the State Secretary. A Director heads the Directorate and has a
number of Joint Directors, Deputy Directors and Assistant Directors who report
to him. However, in some States, the Directorate of Health Services is separate
from the Directorate of Medical Education. In other States, even though the two
Directorates are functionally separate, there is only one Director. In a number
of other States, there is a Director of Health and Family Welfare, a Director of
Medical Education, and a Director of Training. Some States have a Director
General for Health Services under whom various Directors function. Finally,
some States have a post of Secretary and Commissioner Family Welfare.
3.      There are 431 Districts in India, each of which has a Chief Medical
Officer (CMO) or District Health Officer (DHO) who is in charge of the health
infrastructure at the district level. In each district, there is a hospital of
200-400 beds with specialist services, catering to local needs and meeting the
referral services from the periphery. The task of the CMO and the District
Family Welfare Officer (DFWO) who works for the CMO, are basically managerial.
The CMO's tasks include the planning and implementation of IEC, training,
community mobilization and monitoring programs, plus administration, including
establishment and control of vehicles.
4.      Community Health Centers are established at a sub-district level and
cater to populations of about 100,000. They provide specialist services
including a physician, surgeon, gynecologist, dental surgeon, and, in some
cases, a pediatrician. At the Block level, there is generally a Primary Health
Center. Originally, the PHC was planned for a population of 100,000, but as
part of the Seventh Five-Year Plan expansion program, this has been    reduced
to 30,000. The PHC is the nucleus from which curative and preventive services
radiate in the rural areas, and it is usually staffed with three doctors. In
some States, the third medical officer is a practitioner of one of the Indian
Systems of Medicine (ISH) which includes the Ayurvedic, Homeopathy or Unani
systems of medicine. In other States, of the three doctors, one is always a
female, in order to overcome the reluctance of rural women to being ezamined by
male doctors. PHC's are also staffed with two male and two female Health
Assistants and Health Workers each, and a Community Health Officer.



-51-                                          ANNE 2
Page 2
Below the new PHC is the sub-center which covers a population of 5,000 and is
staffed with a male and female Health Worker.
5.           At the Village level, for every 1.000 people, there is a Health Guide
who is a volunteer from the village, and is paid an honorarium of R8.50 per
month. In addition to Health Guides, traditional birth attendants (TEA) or
Dais are paid Rs. 20 per delivery. Both the Health Guide and TBA are given
replenishable medical kits consisting of 18 types of drugs. Trained TBAs play
a very effective role in delivering MCH services as they are trusted by the
village community. The Health System infrastructure is outlined in the
following table.

Section 2: Schemes which Make up the National Family Welfare Program
The 26 Schemes which make up the National Family Welfare Program can be
categorized as follows:
(a)   Family Welfare Services including for Family Planning (i)
sterilizations (vasectomy, tubectomy), (ii) IUD insertions; (iii)
free distribution of oral pills and conventional contraceptives
(condoms, jelly creams, foam tablets, diaphragms) and for MCH,
(iv) immunization of pregnant women and children (tetanus, DPT,
Polio, BCG and measles), (v) prophylaxis against nutritional
anemia among women and children and against blindness due to
Vitamin A deficiency; and (vi) Oral-rehydration therapy.
(b)   Facilities to deliver these services:  construction, furnishing
and equipping (vii) under the Minimum Needs Program, Community
Health Centers (CHCs), Primary Health Centers (PHCs) including
operation theaters, and Sub-centers including quarters for Female
Health Workers: (viii) Post-Partum Centers at district and sub-
district Hospitals and selected Medical Colleges; (ix)
sterilization beds; (x) IUD Rooms at Rural Family Welfare Centers;
and (xi) Health Posts in urban slums under the Urban Revamping
Scheme;
(c)   Improving the Quality of Services delivered through the
appointment of new and upgrading of new and existing workers
through specialized training programs for (xii) Female Health
Workers; (xiii) Female Health Assistants; (xiv) Nursing Tutors and
Public Health Nurses; (xv) former Unipurpose workers to become
Multipurpose Workers (Male Health Workers and Assistants) (xvi)
Dais (traditional birth attendants) and (xvii) Village Health
Guides;
(d)   Improving the Management and Operation Systems through ensuring
the supply and distribution of (xviii) vaccines, (xix) cold-chain
equipment, (xx) vehicles; (xxi) contraceptives; and (xxii)
undertaking demographic research and evaluation; and
(e)   Increasing the Demand for these services from the public by
(xxiii) Information, Education and Communication programs (IEC);
(xxiv) increasing the involvement of Private Voluntary
Organizations (PV0s) in the program; (xxv) social marketing of
contraceptives (pills and condoms) and (xxvi) supporting family
welfare programs in the organized sector (Railways, Defense,
Labor, etc) and private sector companies.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Health and Family Welfare Education and Training Programs
Section 1 - Organization of Health and Family Welfare
Education and Training Programs
1.    Training Policies and Programs in Family Welfare.  Training is a key
aspect of the draft Eighth Five-Year Plan and Revised Family Welfare strategy.
A major premise in these policy documents is that the effectiveness of service
delivery would be enhanced, if all categories of workers are appropriately
trained, and the systems and procedures under which they operate 8 e suitably
altered and/or reformed. The training clientele and their needs therefore
have to be identified, the specific forms of current training assessed and
revised, and the organization and management of the training system examined
to determine whether it is effective in delivering the needed education and
training. The basic form of the training system is outlined below.
Pre-Service Training
2.    Medical Education.  Under-graduate and post-graduate medical education
is provided by the 106 Medical Colleges in the country. Each College is
attached to a general hospital and/or some specialized hospitals for purposes
of training. The training program is largely along the lines of a
conventional western curriculum, with an emphasis on curative rather than
public and preventive health care. In addition to these medical colleges
which follow the allopathic system, medical education is also provided in
traditional and Indian forms such as ayurveda, homeopathy, Siddha, Unani
medicine, nature cure, yoga, and Amchi (Tibetan) systems of medicine. Courses
for medical technologists in Radiology, ECG, and Cardiology are also provided
in hospitals.
3.    Nursing Education:  The 186 Colleges of Nursing in the States, provide
courses of four years duration and award a BSc in nursing. Community Health
is well integrated in the curriculum and is a major thrust of the program.
Consequently, most BSc nursing graduates are employed as teachers in Auxiliary
Nurse Midwife (ANM)/Health Worker (Female) Training Schools. In addition to
the Colleges, there are schools attached to the teaching general hospitals
which conduct 3 to 3 1/2 year courses in general nursing and midwifery.
4.    Paramedical Education.  Female Health Assistants who are the supervisors
of female health workers, undergo training of 2 1/2 years duration at 45 Lady
Health Visitors (LHV) Training Schools. These schools also provide short
courses of six months duration for Health Workers (Female) who have had a
minimum of five years service and are to be promoted to the assistant level.
Male Health Assistants are trained at 21 Health Assistant (Male) Training
Schools and at the 47 Health and Family Welfare Training Centers (HFWTCs).
The course is only of six months duration because the clientele are all male



- 57 -                       ANNEX 5
Page 2
Health Workers who are being promoted after many years of service as uni-
purpose workers in the malaria, leprosy and tuberculosis programs. Health
Inspectors/Sanitary Inspectors receive training of 6-12 months duration in
Health/Sanitation Inspectors Training Schools which are attached to Medical
Colleges. Female Health Workers are given one and half to 2 years training in
the 436 Health Worker (F) Training Schools, formerly called ANI Training
schools which are mostly affiliated with nursing schools and general or civil
hospitals. Some of these schools are government-operated, while others are
run by voluntary organizations, depending on the hospital to which it is
attached.
In-Service Training
5.    Organization.  The National Institute of Health and Family Welfare
(NIHFW) in New Delhi is the apex institution for family welfare in-service
training. At the regional level, seven institutions have been designated as
Central Training Institutes (CTIs). At present, there are no state-level apex
training institutions. However, under the Fourth IDA-assisted population
project, West Bengal will establish a State Institute of Health and Family
Welfare; under the Sixth Population Project, Andhra Pradesh, Madhya Pradesh
and Uttar Pradesh will establish SIHFWs; and under the Seventh Papulation
Projects, Bihar, Gujarat, Haryana, Punjab and Jammu and Kashmir will establish
SIHFWs. At the sub-state level, each State has a varying number of Health and
Family Welfare Training Centers (HFWTCs), for a total of 47 HFWTCs throughout
the country. In addition to these training institutions, medical colleges,
hospitals, and primary health centers (PHCs) all play a role in conducting in-
service training programs.
6.    NIHFW is organized into a number of departments covering the various
aspects of the family welfare program, including reproductive biology, MCH,
IEC, and training. The NIHFW has been active in the analysis of training
needs, atid the coordination and compilation of a series of management training
modules for all levels of family welfare staff. It played a major role in
establishing a national communications task force which produced a set of
communications training modules. It has carried out a variety of research
projects, including an evaluation of the Health Guide program, and is
currently coordinating MOHFW's efforts to involve NGOs more actively in the
family welfare program. It also undertakes the training of higher level
administrative staff and trainers of training programs for some of the
northern States.
7.    There are 7 national institutions including the NIHFW, which have been
designated as Central Training Institutes (CTIs) and which fulfill various
functions. The relationships among each other and to the HFWTCs are vague.
The All Indian Institute of Health and Public Hygiene (AIIHPH) in Calcutta is
a prestigious post-graduate institution with an international reputation for
research and teaching in public health and epidemiology. It also provides
support to the health and family welfare programs in West Bengal and
northeastern India through the training of officials, trainers, and health
workers, although this function occupies only about 151 of its time. The
Regional Family Welfare Training Center (RFWTC) at Bombay is a centrally



- 58 -
ANNEX 5
Page 3
funded institute responsible for training staff and trainers from the western
States. The Central Health Education Bureau (CHEB) in New Delhi is a national
institution having some overlapping functions with NIHFW. The Institute of
Rural Health and Family Welfare in Gandhigram in Tamil Nadu has achieved an
international reputation as a research and training institute specializing in
rural health services and IEC development. The International Institute for
Population Sciences (Bombay) and the Rural Health and Training Center
(Najafgarh) are the other two CTIs. These institutions vary in status: the
CHEB and the RFWTC, for example, are centrally controlled, AIIHPH is virtually
independent, while Gandhigram is a government-aided private trust. In
addition to the recognized CTIs, the Schools of Public Health in Madras and
Bombay, the Indian Institutes of Management (IIM4s) at Ahmedabad, Bangalore,
Calcutta and Lucknow, and the Administrative Staff College at Hyderabad have
all been involved in family welfare in-service management training.
8.    HFWTCs are located in each State to provide in-service training to the
medical and paramedical personnel working at various levels in the system.
These centers are fully financed by the Central government, and have a typical
staffing pattern of Principal, medical lecturer-cum-demonstrator, health
education instructor, social science instructor, public health nurse instruc-
tor, health education extension officer, statistician, and other support
staff. Vacancy rates in HFWTCs are typically high, particularly for social
science and health educators. The main programs presently run at these
centers include 30 days training to medical officers of the PHC and 75 days of
training to the Block Extension Educator (BEE). The HFWTCs also undertake
short-term courses for PHC staff as and when required.
9.    At the PHC/Block level, several types of in-service and on-the-job
training programs are undertaken. Male and female health assistants (HA) are
g-ven about 6 weeks supervisory training by the Medical Officer (MO) and the
BEE. At the end of that period, the HAs are expected to undertake a two-week
course in supervisory skills at the HFWTCs. The MO/PHC also runs an eight-
week course for male health workers and a six-week course for female health
workers. He is assisted by the BEE, health assistants, and the staff of the
HFWTC. Finally, the MO/PHC conducts a three-month program of in-service
training for Health Guides, and a one-month course for traditional birth
attendants. The training system is shown in the following table.

Section 2 - Problems and Issues in FamLly Welfare Training
10.       Several major problems affect the quality of medical and paramedical
education and training programs. They include (a) limited career development
prospects for staff working in the program, especially teachers and instruc-
tors; (b) anomalies in the organizational structure of the present system;
(c) a high level of inefficiency in the delivery of training; and (d) the
general low level of skills, knowledge and aptitudes of family welfare staff.
11.       Personnel Management and Career Development.  The delivery of family
planning and MCH services is supposed to be integrated at all levels and is
carried out primarily by health workers. Female Health Workers are the
linchpin of the program. While job responsibilities for all levels and types
of staff have been carefully specified, these have not been well disseminated
to staff, nor linked to pre-and in-service training activities, and in most
cases, field workers have a far greater task load than they can be reasonably
expected to handle. In addition, since male Health Workers are paid from a
variety of budget heads/programs, duplication of effort constrains effective
performance. Moreover, personnel practices are weak. The recruitment, selec-
tion, posting, transfer and promotion for all levels of staff are subject
to other than administrative and managerial influences with consequent
negative effects on staff morale. Efforts to improve personnel management
through more systematic performance appraisal methods, enhanced career
development opportunities, and clearer job definition and selection criteria
must receive priority attention if the quality of service is to be improved.
In the area of training, these problems are especially acute among the staff
of the Health and Family Welfare Training Centers (HFWTCs) and other training
institutes. The GOT has recognized this problem as it affects staff who
operate central as oppused to state-level institutions. In 1982, the GOI
restructured the Central Health Service to consist of 4 streams; i.e. public
health, teaching, non-teaching and general duty officers. Along with this
reform, a cadre review service was instituted which has considerably improved
the promotional prospects of members of the service. These reforms have not,
however, penetrated to the health services in the States. In the States,
medical and other officials who are deputed to training responsibilities view
this as a negative step in their careers. Although the programs at the HFWTCs
and other institutes are fully financed by the GOI, training staff belong to
the State services. Reform of the Health Services in the States is an
essential prerequisite to effectively upgrading the quality of health and
family welfare training programs.
12.       Organizational Structure of Family Welfare Training.  The present
system of designated Central Training Institutes (CTIs), and a large number of
State-level institutions involved in both pre- and in-service training
requires rationalization. Most CTIs with the exception of the National
Institute of Health and Family Welfare (NIHFW), operate courses for the Family
Welfare program but these are generally addendums to their main operations,
with the consequent lack of attention, resources and commitment. These



- 61 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 6
institutions have different foci and objectives, and in some cases, family
welfare is only a small part of their activity. In the States, the HFWTCs are
the key institutions and should be linked directly to thp NIHFW. Several
should be selected and upgraded to become either (a) regional centers to
replace the CTIs and provide services to a number of States; or (b) State-
level apex institutions which provide specialized services to the other HFWTCs
and schools in the particular state.
13.       The regional or State apex institution option would provide for the
development of selected HFWTCs to concentrate on conducting train.ng needs
assessments of all categories of workers, development of spesialiL.'± course
curricula and materials, conducting training of trainers programs, and provid-
ing technical assistance outreach activities to the on-the-job training
programs operated at the Post-Partum Centers, Primary Health Centers (PHCs)
and Community Health Centers (CHCs).
14.       Another organizational change that may be required is to make the
HFWTCs specialize. At present, all HFWTCs conduct essentially the same
mixture of pre-service and in-service courses. Appropriately trained staff are
not available locally to run simultaneous programs at numerous institutions.
It may be prudent therefore to have some HFWTCs specialize in clinical skill
and management programs for medical officers; others can cater to needs of
supervisors such as Health Assistants, Community Health Officers, and others
still to the Health Worker, Health Guides (HG) and Dai clientele. In many
ways this pattern is already manifest at all institutions. Because of social
and other factors, medical officers are not trained at the same time in the
same institution with Health Workers. The integration of the skills of
Medical Officers, Health Supervisors and Workers is supposed to take place on
the job, rather than at the training institutions. This highlights the need
to make recurrent on-the-job training an integral and major part of the
overall training infrastructure. The on-the-job training programs are for the
most part not well integrated in the system. The training activities at the
PHC are disorganized or largely never implemented, and the variations in
themes, quality, and intensity make what is offered irrelevant to the
integration process that is essential to the Family Welfare program. The GOI
and the States must therefore (a) accept the necessity for recurrent training;
(b) understand that on-the-job rather than one-shot institution based in-
service training must be the core of the training system; and (c) that
programs and staff must be developed and resources dedicated to having this
system implemented.
15.       Quality and Efficiency.  Several major problems affect the internal
and external efficiency of training institutions, and these ultimately affect
the quality of the training programs which they deliver. These problems
include poor linkages between training curricula and job requirements, failure
to attract suitable talent to training institutions, limited faculty develop-
ment efforts, over reliance on didactic/lecture training methods, and limited
use of demonstrations and field-based training. Clinical training is
especially weak. Many staff are unfamiliar with simple sterilization proce-
dures, the advantages/disadvantages of temporary methods and key MCH interven-
tions such as how to manage weighing programs. Training equipment and



- 62 -                          NNEX5
Page 7
materials are also in short supply. Training facilities are provided, but
have deteriorated due to the lack of maintenance in many cases. At. many
levels, facilities are under-utilized because the clientele cannot be released
for training when actively involved in achieving annual family planning
targets. This situation is pronounced in the last half of the fiscal year. In
some States, at some levels of training, there is excess capacity, while in
other areas the reverse is true, with overcrowding, and a backlog of
applicants seeking entrance for pre-service training.
16.       Skills, knowledge and aptitudes of family welfare staff.  Center,
State and district-level administrators are in charge of implementing all
health and medical policies and programs. There is a general consensus,
however, that few officers, including physicians, have a sufficient depth of
program knowledge to plan, manage, supervise, and evaluate all aspects of an
integrated Family Welfare program, particularly to support multi-tiered
management and technical training programs for medical and health services
personnel. In addition, the frequent transfer of administrative and medical
officers requires that a system for recurrent training in management be
instituted. Short training courses of 2-6 days for these officers have been
developed and carried out by the II1's, HFWTC's, NIHFW, and the CTIs. While
these institutions are all appropriate to carry out and evaluate this train-
ir.g, these programs have not been systematic, and many of the recently
appointed administrators remain untrained. In addition, there are questions
whether the short courses relate precisely to the responsibilities and
performance of the officers.
17.       The quality of India's medical and paramedical training programs is
a perennial problem. Budgetary allocations for training reflect the
priorities of investment in the program's human capital base. In spite of the
labor and skill intensive nature of family welfare work, the majority of
resources for training are dedicated to medical education as oppoLad to
paramedical education and training.
18.       Medical Officers (MOs) are responsible for the management and
delivery of all medical and health services in the system, and are trained at
the network of public and private medical schools. About 802 of all MOs are
male and have completed the MBBS degree, while 20? have post-graduate degrees
and diplomas. The medical education program emphasizes curative rather than
preventive health care, with little attention to the management aspects of the
public health system. Medical education also does not adequately address
public health administration and the management skills required for public
sector physicians.  Althou   several schools have post-graduate programs in
public health, the quality is questionable. This situation requires action at
several levels: (a) medical education curricula and the current patterns of
public health training for physicians need revision; (b) improving health
administration through the development of alternative career development
structures for clinical and ad&Jnistration specialists; (c) exposing practic-
ing physicians through upgrading training to more information about temporary
methods; and (d) in'reasing private sector involvement in the family welfare
training program, especially the skill upgrading of medical practitioners.



- 63 -                       ANNEX 5
Page 8
19.       In addition to the above deficits, the new emphasis in the GOI's
program on temporary measures of contraception and maternal and child health
is severely hampered by the lack of female MO's. Cutural inhibitions in many
geographic areas of the country against the physical examination of females by
male MOs precludes first hand supervision of female health workers, and the
management of emergency obstetric/gynecology (OBIGYN) cases and referral
services. No training program or entity currently exists which provides the
mix of management, medical knowledge, plus the skills and attitudes required
for the in-service training of medical officers. A training program could be
designed which utilizes the IIM's and other management institutions for
managerial diagnostic studies and the development of case study modules,
upgraded HFWTC's for carrying out non-clinical training, and district area
maternity hospitals with their Post-Partum centers, and private
medical hospitals and schools for continuing clinical medical education.
Another option might be to provide upgraded HFWTC's with clinical case
studies, along with visiting specialists in OB/C-YN and communicable diseases.
20.       Block Extension Educators (BEE) are responsible for implementing
Information, Education and Communication (IEC) programs throughout the
geographic area served by the PHC. They are supposed to be provided short
orientation training at HFWTCs, but this pre-service training of 6 months
duration generally has not occurred. Many of the existing BEE's were formerly
experienced uni-purpose workers or male health workers. More recently, social
science graduates have been recruited to undertake this assignment. Their
managerial deficits cover the full range of their responsibilities. A techni-
cal skills needs assessment should also be carried out to determine the extent
of their knowledge in the latest techniques in communications. Because of the
lack of appreciation of IEC on the part of MOs, ambiguities of BEE respon-
sibilities, and lack of training, some serve as administrative assistants to
the medical officer, resulting in block-level IEC activities not being under-
taken.
21.       Community Health Officers (CHO) are responsible for the supervision,
support, and guidance of all health personnel and community level workers such
as Health Guides and Dais. They assist the MO in organizing and implementing
all preventive and promotive aspects for all health programs, as well as train
the health workers. Because most CHO's were trained previously as unipurpose
workers, and their continued training and supervision by Medical Officers
largely does not occur, there are large managerial and technical skill gaps
among these workers.
22.       Female Health Assistant/Lady Health Visitor (Supervisors).  Female
Health Assistants (HA[F]) are responsible for the planning, supervision,
clinic scheduling, and community organization in sub-centers and villages.
In addition, many conduct the MCH and PP clinics in the sub-centers. Most of
the HAMF) were trained as Lady Health Visitors (LHV), although some have been
promoted from the ranks of Health Workers (F) and hence have not had the
extensive pre-service education of the LHV. Because of the diverse
backgrounds of HA(F) only about one half have been trained in IUD insertion.
Surveys indicate a wide range of managerial performance gaps among them.
Without systematic in-service training, their knowledge of health issues,



- 64-                        ANNEX5
Page 9
practice and skills will erode rapidly. This is particularly important in the
areas of counselling in temporary method choice, IUD insertion, sterilization
follow-up, and supervision of Female Health Workers HW(F) and Dais.
23.       Health Worker (Female) also known as Auxiliary Nurse Midwives (AiIM)
are responsible for implementing the MCH and PP programs. Most have completed
the 18 month pre-service training course as ANM's, but this training is
deficient because it is hospital centered. The supervision and on-the-job in-
service training which is intended to supplement this training has not been
satisfactorily implemented. Performance evaluations indicate the following
skill and knowledge gaps of HW(F): (a) antenatal care, labor and delivery: it
is estimated that only 402 of HW(F) could carry out a proper antenatal
examination, and even fewer could describe how to deal with the common
complications of pregnancies and delivery; (b) child health and nutrition:
less than 1OX of health workers have an adequate knowledge of vaccination
dosages and their administration, as well as sterile practices. About the
same percentage know what services are to be provided in child clinics, and
how to weigh and measure child growth; and (c) family planning less than 6Z of
HW(F) can insert IUD's or recognize the necessary contra-indications as a pre-
requisite to prescribing oral contraceptives. In summary, most HW(F)s cannot
satisfactorily perform their responsibilities. This is directly linked to
deficient pre-service training, weak on-the-job training, and ineffective
refresher or upgrading training. HFWTC programs and PHC training which are
supposed to meet this need require improvements if MCHI/PP service delivery is
to increase and improve.
24.       Health Worker (Male) are also responsible for sub-center activities
except MCH. Most have had some form of pre-service training when they func-
tioned as unipurpose workers in the leprosy, malaria and tuberculosis
prevention programs. A little more than half have received additional
training under the Multipurpose Scheme but only about 201 understand the
functions and methodology of work called for at the PHC including immuniza-
tion. Specific administrative skills gaps include organization of regular and
effective staff meetings, managing the drug supply and other logistics, record
keeping, community participation, referral services, and monitoring and
helping Health Guides and Dais.
25.       Health Guides (HG) also known as Village Health Guides (VHG) and
Community Health Guides are expected to cover 1,000 people. Their responsi-
bilities include outreach and health education in malaria, smallpox, communi-
cable diseases, MCH, FP, first aid and immunization. HG skill gaps include
general knowledge of health, family planning, immunization, drug administra-
tion, malaria, infectious diseases, and ayurvedic medicines. Few of the HGs
have received refresher training beyond the three-month induction program.
Identified training needs include diagnosis of coumu'nity health needs,
screening of cases, work planning, management of medicines, health education,
coordination of other workers, community leadership, and referral systems.
26.       Traditional Birth Attendants (TBA) are usually women over 40 years
old, illiterate, and are either married or widowed. The 30 days training they
receive is usually inadequate. Only 502 of them apply the principles learned,



- 65 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 10
particularly hygienic practices, when on the job. The continuing training
needs of the TBAs include the planning and scheduling of house visits,
identification of high risk mothers and children, creation of/or understanding
of referral systems, the proper management of antenatal and post natal care
and deliveries, health education, leadership and interaction with comounity
organizations, and procedures for receiving their honoraria. Technical skills
gaps analysis shows that: (a) only about 15? of the TBAs use sterile instru-
ments for deliveries; (b) only about 202 know how to tie the cord correctly;
(c) only about 50? attend to the new born appropriately, except for the
availability and use of clean cotton swabs; (d) about one third attend to all
the immediate care needs of the mother, although about 5OZ of them only do one
or two of the necessary treatments, i.e., check for excessive bleeding, verify
the complete delivery of the placenta, washing mother and giving a clean
dressing, and giving mother fluids to drink; and (e) less than 25? provide
appropriate advice on breastfeeding, weaning, immunization and family plan-
ning. The TBA is the bottom of the service delivery system, and at the same
time, plays a critical role in the Family Welfare chain of referral. The
training, skills, and knowledge gaps highlight the need for increased
training.

Section 4 - Essential Elements of the Family Welfare Training Program
Prepared by the Ministry of health and Family Welfare,
Government of India
1.          Strengthening State-level Management and Administration of
Training through the creation of Human Resources Development Cells in the
States. These Cells will develop training plans, personnel information
systems, MIS, and the monitoring and evaltation of training. More
specifically, the Cell will undertake the following activities:
(a)   defining the organizations' training needs both for the present
and future;
(b)   maintaining an up-to-date inventory of personnel assets and
management talents;
(c)   provide a climate for individual growth, improve effectiveness and
preparing staff for higher responsibilities;
Cd)   establishing training programs which are designed to meet specific
and identified needs;
(e)   creating a system of employee performance appraisal;
(f)   filling key positions with staff who are qualified and competent
to achieve organizational goals;
(g)   ensuring that training schools, in meeting the challenge Health
for All by 2000 (HFA) or Net Reproduction Rate (NRR) of unity
through the primary health care approach, follow a more productive
strategy which focuses not only on the new tasks to be performed,
but also on the people who are expecteu to change their behavior.
it is essential that changes in personnel values occur in line
with the desired changes in organizational values in order to
facilitate and reinforce the realignment of personnel and
organizational goals;
(h)   designing a training strategy which would be inter-active with the
work setting and facilitate organizational change; and
(i)   preparing management on how to: (i) induce line managers to
release their staff for training; (ii) inculcate an organizational
value that reinforces training as a legitimate policy and strategy
for improving individual and organizational efficiency and
effectiveness; (iii) induce supervisors to recognize continuing
education as an aspect of supervision; (iv) upgrade, re-focus and
improve the skills and knowledge of trainers to meet the
challenge; (v) enable trainers to be more effective and assertive
about their relevance to the need of functionaries, and their role
as facilitators of effective change and implementation; and



- 72 -
ANNEX 5
Page 17
(vi) by utilizing training techniques that meet the identified
skill gaps.
2.          Rationalizing the Training Infrastructure to systematize pre-
service and in-service programs. New structures will include SIHFWs, Regional
Training Centers, Dietrict Training Centers and District Training Teams. In
basic education, the Manpower Development Cell will monitor the quality of
training, the type of interactions, defining the needs of the work setting and
required facilitier so that field/institutional placement remains a positive
learning experience. Further, the selection and recruitment policy for the
candidates to be admitted to the basic education schools will be properly
defined. Simultaneous efforts will be made to create a cadre of effective
trainers.
3.          In-service/Continuing Education.  Health personnel will under%o
professional and management training at least once in six to seven years,
depending on the capacity of training institutions in the State.
4.          Promotion policy will be in.egral to the training policy.
Promotion will be linked to the successful completion of the training program.
5.          States will consider creation of training cadres, and/or package
of financial or other incentives to recruit and retain in service good
personnel.
6.          There would be a continuous evaluation system to evaluate training
efforts from a svstem's perspective.
7.          There would be periodical assessment of training needs to ensure
the relevance of trainin- to the organization. This may involve
review/revision of 4ob descriptions, role specification, etc.
8.          Revision of pre-service curriculum (basic training curriculum for
health worker (female)] with emphasis on increasing practical HCH content and
field practice.
9.          The Indigenous System of Medicine will be viewed as integral to
the healt:L care service delivery system, and their training and involvement
will be ensured in preventive and promotive aspects of F.W. programs.
10.         Review and revision of the work and supervision schedules of
Health Assistants and Health workers will be undertaken systematically. This
will include revised measures of workers' productivity.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Concurrent Evaluation of the Family Velfarc Program
1.        The Concurrent Evaluation Program, which started in August 1989,
continues the tradition of program evaluation and monitoring by MOHFW that has
included the establishment of eighteen Population Centers nationwide, and on-
going field evaluations and monitoring by eight Mobile Teams. This latest
effort is by far the most ambitious and comprehensive and will be an integral
and on-going part of MOHFW program evaluation. The Concurrent Evaluation
consists of four data collection surveys covering: (a) Family Planning
Acceptors; (b) MCH beneficiaries; (c) Households; and (d) District profiles,
including Primary Health Centers and Sub-centers.
2.        A total of 3,800 data items will be collected per month.  The topics
of the survey are described briefly below:
(a) Schedule l.Family Planning Program
(i) The P.H.C, its staff and facilities;
(ii) Eligible Couple Registers and Acceptor profiles;
(iii) community involvement and Information, Education,
and Communication (IEC) program;
(iv) immunization coverage; and
(v) staff training
(b) Schedule 2. Sub-Centers
Ci)  the Sub-center, staff and facilities;
(ii)  supervision and Village Health Committees;
(iii)  inter-sectoral collaboration (teachers, anganwadi
workers, revenue officers, etc.);
(iv)  field visits and availability of supplies; and
(v)  staff training (frequency and kind)
(c) Schedule 3.1 Sterilization Acceptors
(i) demographic profile of acceptors and family;
(ii) reasons for accepting;
(iii) where procedure done, and follow up; and
(iv) compensation, if any
(d) Schedule 3.2 ITJD Acceptors
(i)  demographic profile of acceptors end their family;
(ii)  reasons for accepting;
(iii)  where procedure done, and follow up; and
(iv)  includes items on immunization and medicine provided for
pregnant women



- 74 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2
(e) Schedule 3.3 Nirodh and Oral Plll Acceptors
(i) medical checkup, and follow up;
(ii) complications, if any; and
(iii) source of motivation
(f) Schedule 3.4 MCH Beneficiaries (EPI, MCH)
(i)  Services received, where and by whom (e.g. visited by an ANM?);
(ii) Prenatal, delivery, immunization, family planning
and follow#-up services received, where and by whom
(g)  Schedule 4. Household Survey (clients)
(i)  demographic profile;
(ii)  socio-economic profile;
(iii)  family, F.P. method used, if any; why or why not;
(iv) where medical health services sought; and
(v)  source of health information and treatment
(h) Schedule V.S., Village Survey
Ci)  demographic profile;
(ii)  socio-economic profile;
(iii)  availability of health and family welfare facilities
and staff;
(iv)  number of F.P. acceptors, by type; and
(v)  analysis of eligible couples
'i) Schedule D.P., District Profile
Ci)  demographic profile, including religion;
(ii)  H. and FW staff sanctioned and posted;
(iii)  targets and achievements;
(iv)  MCH services provided;
(v)  Family Planning supplies on hand;
(vi)  logistics difficulties, deaths due to sterilization
and sterilization failures; and
(vii)  staff trained, action planned and performance
indicatcors, if any.
3.        The Concurrent Evaluation surveys will be conducted in the 17 major
States and will cover 90 percent of India's population. Nineteen Districts
will be selectad from these States each month for surveys, thus 228 discrete
districts per year will be surveyed. All of India will be covered, therefore,
every two years (there are currently 438 Districts in India).
4.        The Concurrent Evaluation was launched in order to provide
systematic, reliable, valid, and comparable data about the status and quality
of the Health and Family Welfare program. In addition, it will provide



- 75 -
ANNEX 6
Page 3
national data which is comparable by item, treatment and analysis. In time,
it should replace the various scattered, segmented, incomplete, and non-
comparable data sets provided by individual project *baseline surveys.
5.        Twelve private and public research organizations have been
contracted by MOHFW to carry out the monthly surveys in the 17 States. The
schedules contain 200 data items that are coded for data input and treatment.
The 200 items from each of 19 districts per month will provide 3800 as a
points each month. The 12 Survey Agencies will complete their survey
instruments containing raw iata and then send them to two additional agencies
which are contracted to scrutinize the monthly schedules for completeness and
accuracy. These 'scrutiny' firms will then submit the raw data to Data Entry
Firms/Agencies. Thes machine readable data (in the form of discs), will then
go to the National Informatics Center (NIC), where it will be treated and
reported upon. NIC is part of the Planning Commission and is also carrying
out the National Computer mapping effort. This Report will contain 150 tables
derived from data treatment and analysis. NIC started providing Quarterly
Reports beginning the first quarter of 1990.

SAFE MOTHERHOOD STRATEGY
The strategy would include the following
(a) information on the root causes of maternal death would be dissemi-
nated widely to policy makers, as well as among families in villages
and slums. Because social attitudes, especially in men is detrimen-
tal to safe motherhood, this would receive special attention.
Program activities would be intensified with special emphasis on
raising the status of women, literacy and education for women,
improving nutrition, and opening up of income generating opportu-
nities, etc. Intersectoral linkages would also be promoted and
synchronized multimedia campaigns to mobilize public opinion in
support of the minimum age of marriage as well as other issues
related to safe motherhood, would be undertaken;
(b) the infrastructure of 20,000 Primary Health Centers and 130,000
subcenters which are now in place would be energized La function at
optimum levels in order to deliver an essential package of services
designed to reduce maternal deaths;
(c) the level of support presently being given to the TBA and ANM would
be augmented to address the priorities of safe motherhood, with
emphasis on identifying and dealing with mothers at risk;
(d) first level referral services will be strengthened. 2,100 CHCs will
be progressively developed to serve as the first level of referral
to deal with mothers requiring emergency obstetric services. Criti-
cal care units will also be established in various urban areas;
(e) institutional deliveries would be actively supported and encouraged
and the quality of services improved,
(f) these essential services for safe motherhood would be provided
universally in a phased manner within the decade. Training, commu-
nication, management information, logistics and supply will be
supported;



- 78 -                           ANNEX 7
Page 3
(g) existing programs and services will be fully utilized for providing
essential services of safe motherhood. For example, ICDS, adult
literacy program, water supply, etc., will be utilized. Community
contacts through existing health programs such as UIP and Family
Planning, will be used for activities for safe motherhood;
(h) given the regional variations in the country, area specific ap-
proaches and support would be developed to achieve the stated goals
in each region. This would be implemented with emphasis on decen-
tralized planning and implementation. Specifically, the panchayats
(local self Government) would be involved;
(i) NGOs will be collaborative partners and aggressively involved in the
entire process of planning, implementation, monitoring and evalua-
tion;
(j) services would be made available to all pre. :ant women, and all
efforts made to reach every pregnant women ijr the first time, so as
to educate her for continued safe motherhood and dissemination of
information in the neighborhood;
(k) social and legal measures would be emphasized and enhanceC for
delaying the age of marriage. Medical and social initiatives would
be taken to ensure that Medical Termination of Pregnancy (MTP)
services are within close reach of all women who need these servic-
es.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
The Univrsal X uniaation Proar!n
Section l Background of the Program
Introduction
1.          The Expanded Program on Immunization (EPI) was initiated in India
in 1978 with the objective to reducing morb!dity and mortality caused by
diphtheria, pertussis (whooping cough), tetanus, poliomyelitis, and childhood
tuberculosis. Measles vaccine was added to the program in 1285. Achievement
of self-sufficiency in v-accines production was also a part of the program.
2.          The Universal Immunization Program (UIP) was started in 1985 to
expand coverage and to improve services, until all districts in the country
are covered. Starting with 30 districts in the catchment area of 30 Medical
Colleges, the program by 1987 covered 182 districts within the catchment areas
of all of the country's 106 Medical Colleges. The program goals were intended
to include the reduction of mortality of pregnant women due to tetanus, as
well as research and development on improved and new vaccines.
3.          The success of the program in lowering the incidence of Tetanus,
Diphtheria, and Pertussis, has been dramatic despite it not having fully
reached the program target of 85Z coverage by 1990. With coverage of infants
over 30Z and coverage of pregnant women to almost 5O0 by 1987, a new program
was launched to continue the momentum of the UIP as wel as focus on aspects
of the program inhibiting further progress.  The prog-   49 planned not only
to assist the expansion of coverage, continue the MomILcum gained in the
decrease of tetanus. diphtheria and pertussis, but also to gain significant
decreases in measles, poliomyelitis and tuberculosis. The *Technology Mission
on Vaccination and Immunization of Vulnerable Population* was designed to
accomplish these objectives and includes four major components: storage and
distribution of vaccines; administration of vaccines, monitoring and
evaluation; R&D for new and improved vaccines; and production of vaccines.
4.          In 1990 the Ministry of Health and Family Welfare produced
guidelines to implement the National Immunization Program (NIP).
The National Mission on Immunization
5.          The four mini-missions comprising the National Mission was
launched in 1988 and are divided between two Ministries. The two mini-
missions for implementing the program, "Storage and Distribution of Vaccines',
and *Administration of Vaccines, Monitoring and Evaluation", are the
responsibility of the Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW). The two
mini-missions dealing with the research and development vaccines, 'The R&D for
New and Improved Vaccines', and the 'Production of Vaccines' are being
carried-out by the Department of Biotechnology of the Ministry of Science &
Technology. The National Mission will integrate strategies to reduce infant



- 94 -
ANNEX 12
Page 2
mortality with existing other interventions including antenatal - prenatal
care, breast feeding and nutrition, safe drinking water and better sanitation,
improved rural health care, control of diarrhoea and ORT, family planning,
health education and female literacy. The strategy not only will add posts
for immunization specialists at the State and district levels, including an
officer-in-charge of the cold-chain and a district refrigeration mechanic to
work with existing health workers, but also will coalesce collaboration with
government institutes, organizations and community-based groups.  These
include, for example, the Indian Council of Medical Research, the All India
Institute of Medical Sciences and local groups such as ICDS workers, school
teachers and PVOs. PVOs include Rotary International, the Indian Medical
Association and its branches, Impact India, All India Women Conferences,
Pariver Sanatha Kendra and the Family Planning Association of India.
National Immuni7ation Program Standards and Training
6.          In 1990, the MOHFW published revised standards and training
manuals in four major topics that had been adopted earlier from World Health
Organization publications: conduct disease surveillances; manage the cold
chain system; conduct the immunization session; and evaluate vaccination
coverage.
(a) Conduct Disease Surveillance. This whow-tot manual provides
standards for case definitions, a system for recording and
reporting data, the analysis and use of reports for local action,
how to conduct investigations of coverage, how to take action and
to report the results. Action flow-charts are provided as are
reporting forms, sample uork schedules, and how to calculate
coverage and vaccines needed. The target audience is the health
center and district-level health workers.
(b) Manage the Cold Chain System. This manual establishes basic
requirements for maintaining the potency of all vaccines at each
transfer point from the manufacturer - airport - state/region -
district stores - primary health center - sub-ceni.er and the
villages. Formulas and proformas are provided for calculating the
Vaccines Administration Rate (VAR) and the Wastage Multiplication
Factor (WMF) as a part of estimating vaccine requirements for each
of the six major vaccines used in the program (TT, DPT, OPV, BCG,
DT arad Measles). Forms are provided for pregnant women's
antenatal care (ANC), infants, immunization records and the
stability of each vaccines at various temperatures.
(c) Conduct Immunization Session. This manual focuses on task
descriptLons and lob responsibilities for health workers and
volunteers from the district to the village level. There are
sample work sheets for learning all of the activities required for
carrying out the program from the Action Plan to the Reporting and
follow-up forms. Samples of IEC materials are included as well as
calendars for program implementation.
(d)   Evaluate Vaccination Coverage.  This manual provides detailed
instruction on accurately measuring vacciaition coverage,
including participating agencies, areas with good and poor



- 95 -
ANNEX 12
Page 3
coverage, whether vaccines are being given at the right age, and
identifying areas of poor attendance and high drop-outs. As with
the other Ohor-to* manuals in the NIP implementation series,
reporting forms and anal 'is are provided.
7.          The Universal Immunization Program (UIP) is a well developed and
planned program that overlaps and contributes to overall Health and Family
Welfare activities. The challenge to reach program targets of 100Z coverage
of pregnant women and 852 coverage of all infants will require continued and
increased funding as well as concentrated and improved training, outreach,
coordination and integration with other programs, logistics, and vaccines R&D
and manufacture.

Section 2s Logistics for the Universal Immunization Program (UIP)
9.          Vaccines retain their potency during storage and transportation at
a temperature range of +2 to +8 degree Celsius. The cold chain system
functioning in India under the Universal Inmrnizatior Program (UIP) ensures
that the vaccines are protected until it is delivered to the beneficiaries.
10.         For storage, distribution and transportation of the vaccines, the
following equipment are being used in the program: Walk-in Cold Rooms, Walk-
in Freezer Rooms, ice-lined refrigerators, deep freezers, vaccine carriers/day
carriers, vaccine delivery vans/jeeps.
11.         Walk in Cold Rooms (WICS) are used for storing the bulk supply of
vaccines received from the manufacturers/central stores. From the WICS, the
vaccines are distributed to the commnand districts through vaccine delivery
vans.  Each WIC aaSrv3 about 4 - 7 districts and WICs are situated at 104
locations in the country. It is proposed to establish additional WICS in
places where the travel time from the district headquarter is more than 6
hours.
12.         Each of the WIC is also supplemented by three Deep Freezers (300
lts) and 10 Cold Boxes (22 lts). The deep freezers are used to store Oral
Polio and Measles vaccines which have to be stored at -20 deg. C. The deep
freezers are also used to produce the ice-packs, which are required for the
transportation of vaccines in cold boxes from WICs to the districts.
13.         Walk in Freezer Rooms are located in a few selected locations.
These are used for the bulk storage of OPV and Keasles vaccines received
directly from the suppliers through air cargo. These locations are: Medical
Stores Depot, Karnal, Medical Stores Dept, Bombay, Medical Stores Dept,
Calcutta, Medical Stores Depot, Madras, Directocate of Family Welfare, Govt.
of UP., Lucknow, Directorate of Family Welfare, Govt. of Punjab, Chandigarh,
Directorate of Family Welfare, Govt. of MP, Bhopal.
14.         Maintenance of Cold Chain Equipment.  When the Cold Chain System
was established, freezers and chest refrigerators were installed and
maintained by commercial agencies under contract. For general care and
maintenance of the equipment, the post of Cold Chain Officer at State Level
and posts of refrigeration mechanics at district level were created.
15.         With the expansion of the cold chain, it is proposed that the
maintenance of equipment be taken over by the existing State Health Transport
Organization (SHTO) and Health Equipment Repair Organizations (HERO).
16.         A meeting to discuss cold chain maintenance during the Eighth
Five-Year Plan period (1990-95) was convened in New Delhi on 13-15 December,
1990. During this meeting, it was pointed out that several States have either
separate organizations for health transport maintenance or health equipment



- 97-                                                -
ANNEX 12
Page 5
repairs or a combination of transport ma.lntenance and health equipment
repairs. Although the units have different names in different States, their
functioning are essentially the same. The field units of these organizations
are either headed by a Junior Engineer or a Foreman. The other staff
available are, general mechanic, electrician, refrigeration mechanic, helpers
etc.
17.         A consensus was achieved in the meeting that the strength of the
refrigeration mechanics available in all the command districts served by each
WIC should be pooled and stationed at the WIC location. A small workshop with
workbench, almirahs, racks for machinery and tools will be provided adjacent
to the WIC room. The ref. mechanics who are available would be pooled into a
team and they would go to the field in a rooter, to attend to the break-down
as well as preventive maintenance. This arrangement will be more effective
once this team is supported by transportation and telephone facilities. While
the arrangement has been accepted as the ultimate goal, for the interim phase
it is proposed to maintain the equipment following one of two other
alternatives available:
(a)   through a maintenance contract with private comiercial agencies;
and
(b)   through an Association of Retired Defence Personnel.
18.         To date, the Governments of Assam, Orissa, Maharashtra, Kerala,
Mizoram and Union Territory of Andaman & Nicobar Islands have taken over the
maintenance of cold chain equipwmnt through their SHTO/HERO. The Governments
of Andhra Pradesh and Union Territory of Chandigarh have established
maintenance contracts with private agencies. The Governments of Karnataka and
Bihar are likely to enter into contracts with private agencies. The remaining
States are also in the process of deciding their mode of maintenance.
Section 3: Procurement of Vaccines
Government Policy
19.   When the Universal Immunization Program was started under the aegis of
the Technology Mission, self-sufficiency for the various inputs, particularly
vaccines, was laid down as one of the objectives of the Program. This
objective was included as one of the integral parts of the Immunization
Mission since continued availability of a supply of good quality vaccines is
the basic pre-requisite for the sustained implementation of the Gniversal
Immunization Program (UIP).
20. Until 1978, all vaccines required for the then Immunization Program were
being met out of the 'captivel production facilities available to the Ministry
of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW). These establishments are the Central
Research Institute, Kasauli, Pasteur Institute of India, Coonoor, and B.C.G.
Vaccine Laboratcry, Guindy, Madras.
21. When the Oral Polio Vaccine was introdtuced in 1978 none of the
production units of the MOHFW had the capacity to provide Oral Polio Vaccines.
It was therefore decided to procure the Oral Polio Vaccines required for the



- 98 -
AN=EX 12
Page 6
Expanded Program of Imuunization from the Haffkns' Bio Pharma Corporation
Ltd. (HBPCL), Bombay, which was blending the vaccine after importing the bulk.
When the Universal Immunization Program (UIP) was initiated in 1985, it was
realized that the captive production capacity available to MOHFW would not be
enough to meat the requirements of vaccines for the Program. It was. 4ecided
to procure the remaining requirements of the various vaccines from tit open
market. A decision was also made to set up an additional production unit for
the manufacture of Oral Polio Vaccines. Under these circumstances, a new
commercial undextaking, under the administrative control of the Department of
Biotechnology, BICOL, is being established -with a targeted production capacity
of 100 million doses by 1995-9C
22.   Achieving self-sufficiency was a conscious decision of the GOI given
the fact that Immunization Programs were being expanded throughout the world.
Unless concerted efforts for increasing indigenous production capacities were
made by all countries, particularlj India whose requirements are quite large,
and in view of the large numbers who would be immunized every year, there
would be a global shortage of all the vaccines, particularly of the Oral Polio
Vaccine (OPV).
23.   Because the total requirement of vaccines was being met earlier from the
captive production capacity, a policy decision was taken that the procurement
of the total amount of vaccines required for the Universal Immunization
Program is to be met from the following sources in order of priority:
(a) entire production capacity from the captive production facilities of the
MOUFW i.e. Central Research Institute, Kasauli, Pasteur Institute of
India, Coonoor and BCG Vaccine Laboratory, Guindy, Madras, will be
procured for the Program; and
(b) a balance if required, to be procured from the open LAarket.
24.   All procurement is presently undertaken by the MOHFW.  The DGS&D
facilitates procurement from non-MOHFW production units.
25. All drugs before being dispatched by the firms are subject to a quality
control analysis by the firms themselves. In addition, they must obtain a
certificate of standard product from any of the laboratories approved
by the Drug Controller of India. They also issue a certificate of warrantee,
assuring the replacement of the entire issue amount if any batch is found to
be sub-standard until its expiry date. Each batch of the medicine is subject
to a similar process.
26.   For vaccines, every batch is first analyzed by the firms. Samples are
then drawn by the Inspecting Officer and are sent to the National Drug Control
Laboratory at CRI, Kasauli, and only after it is declared as being of standard
quality, is the vaccine dispatched to the various consignees maintaining a
proper cold chain.
27. As far as quality assurance is concerned, no distinction is made between
the vaccines procured indigenously and the vaccines which are imported to fill
the gap between availability and requirement. All vaccines (that is, all
batches of each of the vaccines) must be declared as being of approved
formulation and having a potency acceptable for use.

Section 4: Use of Disposable Syringes and Needles
28.          Current Policy and Practices.  The Government of India (GOI) uses
glass syringes and needles for the Universal Imnunization Program. The
following are the sizes used for different vaccinations/purposes.
Syringes         Needles        Vaccination/Purposes
2 ml.                23 g             DPT, Measles
1 ml.                26 g             BCG
5 ml.                20 g             For the purpose of
reconstitvtiton
29.          During 1987-88, GOI introduced the use of reusable plastic
syringes.  These were normally expected to la^x about 200 sterilizations, but
in practice they became unfit for further use after 20-25 injections. This
was due largely to the improper sterilization methods adopted bl the Health
Workers. Because of repeated sterilization/boiling, the syringes expanded and
the nozzles did not fit the needles. The markings on the barrel were also
spoiled after successive use. The Government had therefore stopped further
procurement of these syringes.
30.          The use of disposable syringes and needles has not been encouraged
by the GOI because of high costs and the higher risk of misuse. Nonetheless,



- 100 -
ANNEX 12
Page 8
(turing the Special Immunization Days observed in 1990 - known as 242 - 3.5
uiillion disposable syringes were procured (both Imported and local).
31.         Costs of Disposable Syringeslneedles (unit).  One 2 ml disposable
syringe (with needle) costs Rs. 2.25. One 1 ml. disposable syringe (with
needle costs Rs. 2.50.
32.         Costs of Reusable glass syringes/needles (unit) are as follows:
2 ml. syringes - Rs. 7.00
1 ml. syringes - Rs. 16.00
23 g. needles   -  Re. 10.00 per box of 12
26 g. needles   -  Re. 10.00 per box of 12
33.         The total cost of reusable glass syringes and needles for 1991-92
is estimated to be around Rs. 91.50 million, whereas tha cost of similar use
of disposable needles/syringes would be around Rs. 416.41 million. It is
therefore 4 1/2 times costlier to use disposable materials. There is no
indigenous technology to provide self-destructible needles.
