INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
National Program for the Control of Diarzhoeal Diseases
and Policy on Management of Diarrhoea through Oral Rehydration Therapy
Introduction
1.          Diarrhoea is globally associated with some 5 million deaths per
year. It is estimated that 1.5 million of these occur in India. A majority
of these deaths are preventable, caused by dehydration and linked to
malnutrition. Diarrhoea particularly affects children under five years of
age.   Because the incidence of diarrhoeal disease is less between 0-6 months
in breast fed children, the majority of deaths in that group would occur in
infants between 6 months and one year of age. The magnitude of disease
incidence is unknown due to the self-limiting nature of the disease and except
for cholera is non-notifiable. The disease is classified generally as
Diarrhoea, its major symptom, and includes Cholera, Dysentery and
Gastroenteritis. The major pathogeas causing the disease are bacterial, viral
and parasitic. The treatment of choice for most of these organisms is fluid
replacement because only a few of them cause intestinal wall damage where
drugs or antimicrobial therapy is indicated. Most of the organisms release
toxins when entering the intestinal tract that not only prevent absorption of
fluid from the large intestine but also increase cell permeability which
causes more secretion of body fluid to the intestines causing increased
movements and dehydration. Multiple socio-economic and climatic factors
contribute to the high magnitude of the disease including unsafe water supply,
poor hygiene and sanitation, illiteracy, drought, floods and inadequate
treatment of the disease, particularly withholding of food and fluids.
Findings in Rural India
2.          In coatrast with the high paediatric bed occupancy rates (25 to
40Z) due to diarrhoeal disease, the proportion of outpatient visits to public
facilities was only 5-7Z. This was one reason that diarrhoeal disease was not
given priority in past public health programming. A national study on
Diarrhoea in Rural India 1/ showed that home made solutions were widely
used, and that 65Z of all epicodes consulted a health 'professional' outside
the home with more than SOX of these consultations to private sector
practitioners generally called "doctors", but by and large, neither fully
trained nsr always licensed.
Rice-Based Oral Electrolyte Solutions
3.          Recent studies have indicated that cral rehydration solutions
prepared from rice not only ameliorate dehydration but also decrease
1/ Diarrhoea in Rural India - A Nationwide Study of Mothers and Practitioners-
Indian Market Research Bureau (IMRB) with the assistance of UNICEF and USAID,
Hema Viswanahan, Jon Eliot Rohole, Vision Books, 1990.



- 102 -                       ANNEX 13
Page 2
diarrhoeal fluid loss. They were found to promote greater absorption and
retention of fluid and electrolytes than did a glucose-based solution. 2/
The cited disadvantages of rice solutions, however, include the need for
cooking, the possibility of incorrect preparation, and rapid fermentation. In
addition, some infants may not tolerate whole starch.
The Current Plan of Action
4.          The major efforts during the Seventh Plan period (1985-90) were in
community awareness, training for diagnosis and treatment, providing pre-
packed oral rehydration so'-. 'on (ORS) as widely as possible, and the use of
simple home made solutionb.   ii strategy includes the oral rehydration
program as an integrated ,-- ,rRnt of the child survival package which
includes maternity and c   '-vlfare services as well as the universal
immunization program.  Tnc  [vt; was introduced into 90 districts in 1987-88
and is implemented under guidc,in6s provided by MOHFW with active cooperation
from other ministries and orgar.izations such as the Ministry of Human Resource
Development, Urban Development, und International and Voluntary Bodies. Most
districts were to have been covered by the end of 1990. Implementation at the
state level starts with a State Program Officer at the rank of Deputy/Joint
Director who is responsible for providing guidance, support, training, and
monitoring. The District Health Officer designs the training calendar for all
workers from the village level up, issues training guidelines, supplies CRS
and instructions for supervision, monitoring and coordination with other
agencies. The PHC Medical Officer arranges for staff training, procures ORS,
and is responsible for the day-to-day implementation of the program. He/she
is also responsible for extending the program to the village level through
training and inter-sectoral coordination with all field level organizations
such as block and ICDS workers.
Training
5.          Training at all levels of the service delivery system is
fundamental to the success of the program. State and district level staff
have received training at the National Institute of Communicable and Enteric
Diseases in Calcutta. Staff from India's 47 Health and Family Welfare Centers
(HFWTCs) have received training as trainers in Calcutta and in turn have
trained PHC staff in their catchment area. The Indian Medical Association,
the Indian Academy of Pediatricians and the Schools and Colleges of Nursing
have included ORT training for their students and practitioners. The Medical
and Nursing Councils of India hive included diarrhoea management as part of
their training syllabi. International and voluntary organizations have
published or assisted in mass health education efforts. For example, the
National Institute of Cholera and Enteric Diseases, Calcutta, produced in 1989
a complete package of training for Doctors including a "Course Facilitator
Guide", 'Treatment and Management%, and "Nutritional Care During Diarrhoea".
Radio and television also has been active in the dissemination of the ORT
message.
2/ N. England Journal of Medicine, 1991; 324: 517-21.



- 103 -
ANNEX 13
Page 3
Supply of Rehydration Solution
6.          ORS has been supplied nationwide by the end of the Seventh Plan
period. Both public and private enterprises have been producin- the solution
with a sodium bicarbonate formula to help achieve cplf-sufEiciency. Further
development includes cost reduction methods such as replacing expensive
aluminum foil packaging with polytene and adding sodium citrate so as to
extend shelf life of the packets. Extensive demand generation and comunuity
health education campaigns are still needed if targets of reducing mortality
by 50X and increased utilization are to be achieved.
Monitoring and Evaluation
7.          Program implementation is monitored through an MIES and Sentinel
Centers at selected PHCs indicating trends on incidence, treatment and
mortality. Training is monitored by the quarterly status reports. Several
evaluation studies are underway. The principal difficulty in assessing
progress is that except for cholera diarrhoeal diseases are non-notifiable.
In addition, they are caused by a broad spectrum of multiple organisms that
are different from each other in morphology, patho-physiological effects and
approach for control. At present there are still 15-20? of the organisms
causing diarrhoeal diseases that are unknown. Also, the Rotavirus held
responsible for many diarrhoeas in children is not easily identifiable, and
needing more refined diagnostic tools. Different organisms prevail in
different geographic areas of the country.
The Government of India's Policy on Diarrhoeal Management
8.          The policy aims at reduced case fatality, improved nutrition and
reduced costs, while seeking long-term solutions to the diarrhoeal problem.
The case management strategy elucidates home treatment of diarrhoea, oral
rehydration solutions, feeding during and after illness and prevention of
further diarrhoea. The policy emphasizes that anti-diarrhoeals have no place
in the treatment of children. When dehydration is severe, children should be
referred to health facilities where intravenous fluids are indicated. The
policy states that antibiotics will not be given routinely. However, in cases
of bacillary dysentery, Co-trimoxazole or Nalidixic Acid were indicated. In
other situations, the pationt will be referred to an appropriate institution.
The Government periodically reviews its policy on Diarrhoeal Disease Control
with WHO and UNICEF.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Social Narketing of Oral Rehydration Salt (ORS)
Introduction
1.       Diarrhoeal cases, during the pre- and post-monsoon season in India
amount for as much as 40 percent of paediatric beds and a third or more of
paediatric outpatient visits. Each year it is estimated that some 1.5 million
infants and children, die due to dehydration related to diarrhoeal disease.
Over the past years several research activities have been undertaken which
provide solid data and information on India's diarrhoeal problem. Research
findings particularly germane to the oral rehydration social marketing
programme are:
*     the incidence and death due to diarrhoeal diseases vary widely.
Maximum incidence and death occur in the urban slums and rural
areas.
*     71.9 percent mothers surveyed on an average provided extra fluid
to children suffering from diarrhoea;
*     14.2 percent mothers gave oral rehydration salts (ORS) to
children;
*     sugar/salt solution was used by 16.8 percent of mothers while
another 31.5 percent gave some other home made fluids.
*     1.9 percent of patients needed IV fluids;
*     regarding treatment, 56.7 percent used traditional remedies.
?           Good information of client and select health practioner,
knowledge, attitudes and behavior also has been gathered. The following
points are particularly noteworthy.
*     Efforts are needed to increase mothers' cn-.cern and actions early
in the course of onset of diarrhoea, prior to signs of significant
dehydration.
*     In most parts of the country diarrhoea is recognised as an
important illness and mothers can be readily convinced to pay
specific attention to the illness itself and the problems arising
therefrom.
*     Those who have ever used ORS have a high degree of satisfaction
with the product. Not only is it perceived to be effective in
overcoming anorexia, weakness and lethargy, but also, for those
who expected it to stop diarrhoea, it was perceived to have
worked.



- 105 -
ANNEX 14
Page 2
*     Rural families demonstrate a high level of trust and respect for
the opinion of private sector practitioners regarding their
treatment of diarrhoeal episodes.
*     Mothers were, by and large, willing to pay for medical care during
diarrhoea in their child. In general, it was found that families
expected to pay, and valued services for which they paid in
dealing with this illness.
*     The major response of village-based practioners regarding the.r
management of diarrhoea illness is sale of medicinal items aimed
at stopping the diarrhoea. While all were aware of ORS in one
form or another, relatively few prescribe it routinely, believing
it to be indicated only in cases of dehydration.
*     Most practitioners derive their income from the sale of drugs,
medicines, syrups and even injections administered to treat
diarrhoea. Practioners obtain supplies by travelling to the
nearest town or city where they consult regularly with a chemist
of their choice, and, purchase drugs for resale in their practice.
A major influence on prescribing practices was the regulax
interaction with the chemist and the supply of recommended
products for sale.
*     While amenable to use of ORS, practioners believe it does not face
the major question of stopping the diarrhoea and therefore do not
use it frequently.
3.          Additional research is being conducted which will provide specific
information pertinent to the ORS/social marketing programme. Such research
includes a nation-wide study to assess the demand of ORS and the profile of
supply sources; a study on diarrhoea management in urban areas, a cost/benefit
analysis of ORS vs IV fluids; and a study to obtain specific information on
mothers' knowledge of diarrhoea management.
4.          In 1985, as part of a total assault on the diarrhoeal problem, a
National Diarrhoea Management Plan was developed. The plan called for, among
other things, the marshalling of the resources of the public health
infrastructure and employ of a systematic Information, Education and
Communications (IE&C) to control and manage diarrhoeal disease. The active
involvement of the private sector in working with government in developing a
national oral rehydratlon salts/ social marketing programme was particularly
highlighted under the plan.
Aims
5.          The major aims of the GOI ORS/Social marketing programme are tos
(a) institute a national standard for ORS; (b) investigate various
alternatives to promoting the use of ORS through non-governmental
organisations; and (c) ensure sustainability of the programme after five
years.



- 106 -
ANNEX 14
Page 3
Product standardisation
6.          In India, currently over 75 manufacturers produce ORS of varying
composition and doses with prices ranging from R9.2 to Rs.8.5 per packet of
one litre solution. Almost 70 to 80 percent of the products available on the
market do not conform to the WHOJUNICEF approved composition, in addition to
being sold at relatively high costs. The wide range of ORS packets, available
in different doses, also varies in terms of packet size and packaging
materials. The majority of currently sold products consist of a package which
can provide solution equivalent to one litre.
7.          In order to promote oral rehydration therapy (ORT), it has been
seen as necessary to have a national standard for ORS packets.  Therefore, as
a policy measure, the MOHFW seeks to establish a national s-andard of CRS
packets.  These standards include:
(a)   WHO/UNICEF recommended formulation;
(b)   standard one litre dose;
(c)   standard size, packaging and packaging material;
(d)   standard instructions for use in two languages:  English and
a regional language;
(e)   standard graphic instructions for the illiterate consumer;
and
(f)   standard logo and name.
8.          Manufacturers, both in the public and private sector, are being
involved in the national effort to promote ORT as a solution to controlling
deaths due to dehydration from diarrhoea. A meeting was organised in February
1991 by the Indian Medical Association (IMA) with the drug manufacturers.
MOHFW, along with experts from international agencies such as WHO/UNICEF also
participated. A result of the meeting was consensus among medical
practitioners on the formulation of ORS and endorsement of the WHO/UNICEF
formulation as the mainstay for combating dehydration. IMA also emphasised
the need to have a uniform composition, a logo, and a pictorial mixing message
for correct use of ORS. The Indian Academy of Paediatricians also endorses
the WHO ORS formula as the ideal solution based on physiological findings.
The meeting ended with the recommendation that drug manufacturers should alter
current production to meet a standard product following the WHO recommended
formulation.
NGO marketing of ORS
9.          The MOHFW is considering various alternatives to promoting the use
of ORS through the involvement of NGOs. Several proposals have been received
and are being examined. The MOHFW's approach will be to implement various ORS
social marketing alternatives under an umbrella operational research
programme. The major proposals are described below:

Programme Sustainability
12.         There is a significant difference in the financial strategy
between the social marketing of contracep-ives and social marketing of ORS.
The social marketing of contraceptives is being supported by GOI on the basis
of the volume of contraceptives marketed and sold. The social marketing of
ORS has been formulated on the basis of 'resource gap", with a view to make
the projects self-sustainable in a period of five to six years. This means
that these social marketing projects would become self-sufficient because a
"built-in" profit would be sustained by the agencies on their own.
Management
13.         The ORS social marketing programme will be managed by the Project
Implementation Agency in the MOHFW's Department of Family Welfare. The Deputy
Secretary, MCH and the Assistant Commissioner for ORT will have joint
responsibility for programme management and implementation.
Budget
14.         The budget for the five-year programme is Ra 150 million rupees.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE HOTHERHOMD PROJECT
Acute ResPiratory Infections In Children in India
Introduction
1.        ARI contribute to about 202 of mortality in children under 5 years
of age. It is the second most common cause of death in this age. ICMR
multicentric study shows ARI accounting for 15 to 302 of childhood deaths,
with mortality ranging from 3.2 to 13.8 per 1,000 children. Case fatality
rate in hospitals is around 10Z. While country-wide figures are not
available, it is estimated that ARI claim some 600,000 to 800,000 deaths among
children each year.
2.         Id addition to the high mortality, morbidity from ARI is
considerable.! Children suffer 3 to 5 episodes each year, lasting for 7-14
days. Although the vast majority of ARI are self-limiting, these cause
considerable discomfort as well as adverse nutritional impact. Huge expenses
are incurred by the community with expenditures on a wide variety of
medications als well as placing a burden on limited hospital facilities. ARI
accounts for about 30-602 of all outpatient visits and 20-402 of paediatric
admissions.
3.         Pneumonia incidence, although difficult to determine accurately in
India, is estimated to be several times higher than in developed countries. 20
to 30 per cent of all children suffer from pneumonia each year. This is in
contrast to 3.6Z found in a study from North America. Additionally, bacterial
etiology pneumonia is predominant in India in sharp contrast to the
preponderance of viral causes of pneumonias in Europe and North America.
Bacteria accounted for only 102 of all pneumonia in one major study from USA.
Up to 602 of pneumonias are caused by bacteria, mainly streptococcus
pneumoniae, Hemophilus influenzae, and to a smaller degree Staphylococcus
aureus. Fortunately, cheap and effective antimicrobial drugs are available to
control these bacterial infections, and if given timely, selectively and with
appropriate dosage schedules, can substantially reduce mortality.
Technical Basis for ARI Control
4.          A resolution of the World Health Assembly in 1982 recognized the
importance of ARI control as a distinct health program and recommz,nded its
inclusion along with prevention and control of other communicable diseases,
e.g. CDD, EPI. WHO has prepared protocols and recommended standard case
management of ARI for children. These recommendations form the basis for the
policy of GOI.
5.          Ten intervention studies have been conducted throughout the world,
using the WHO's standard case management protocols in order to evaluate the
feasibility and efficacy of this approach in different settings of developing
countries. These studies have demonstrated that Health Workers at the
community level can recognize pneumonia and provide antimicrobials in
appropriate doses, resulting in substantial reduction in pneumonia mortality.



-110 -                       ANNEX 15
Page 2
In India, mortality reductions have been convincingly demonstrated by
intervention studies in the community, as well as in clinical settings.
6.          Because pneumonia is by far the major cause of death, and other
symptoms of acute respiratory illness such as wheezing, ear infection, sore
throat are not important causes of mortality, the Indian policy focuses
predominantly on the recognition, treatment/timely referral of acute
respiratory infections i.e. pneumonia, bronchiolitis and other chest
infections (all called herein 'pneumonia'). Following the standard WHO
protocols, it has been shown that respiratory rate and the recognition of
chest indrawing are the most critical and sensitive indicators of pneumonia.
7.        For children under 2 months of age with pneumonia indicated by
fast breathing only, and for children aged 2 months - 5 years with fast
breathing, chest indrawing or other signs of severe pneumonia/very severe
disease, referral to a clinical facility where diagnosis and treatment can be
overseen by a trained imedical officer, is considered essential. These cases
will be admitted and receive pt .nteral antibiotics and, where necessary,
oxygen and intravenous therapy.
8.         Treatment of uncomplicated pneumonia will be based on the use of
the broad spectrum antibiotic, cotrimoxazole, which has been shown to be
effective against common bacterial pathogens present in Indian children.
Twice daily regimen for five days is recommended for treatment of pneumonia.
9.          Standard training material for each level of the health care
system i.e. hospitals with admission facilities, primary health centers,
sub-centers and the community will be developed to guide and standardize
diagnosis, treatment and criteria for referral.
Objectives and Strategies,
10.         The main objective of the ARI control program is to reduce deaths
due to pneumonia in children under five years of age. Applying the standards
for case management is the main strategy for reducing mortality.
11.         In India, most bacterial pneumonia in children is susceptible to
the broad spectrum antibiotic, cotrimoxazole. An important element in the
strategy is the selective use of antibiotics so that limited bacterial
resistance occurs from the treatment. Limiting the use of antimicrobial drugs
to only those children who need them will have numerous health and economic
benefits. At this time, ear infections, streptococcal sore throat and
sinusitis are not included in the ARI strategy for village-level health
workers, and future strategies will deal with them.
12.         ARI control programs are to be integrated within the Primary
Health Care system and close linkages will be emphasized with other health
programs such as EPI, CDD, Nutrition and MCH.
Diagnosis and Treatment of ARI Children
13.         Mothers and families.  Communication materials will assist mothers
and families to identify a rapid breathing rate, chest indrawing and inability



- lii -
ANNEX 15
Page 3
to feed as signs of respiratory illness. Imuediate referral to the nearest
health care provider is indicated.
14.         Health Care Providers (all levels).  The basic criterion for a
diagnosis of pneumonia is the respiratory rate, carefully counted in a resting
child over a full minute. The respiratory rates for a pneumonia diagnosis are
60 or more per minute for infants from 0-2 months, and immediate referral by
paramedical workers; 50 or more per minute for children 2-12 months of age,
with referral if there are signs of chest indrawing, and if no signs of
respiratory distress, treatment with cotrimoxazole, 2 tablets twice daily for
S days; and 40 or more per minute for children 1-5 years of age, and if no
chest indrawing, treatment wtith cotrimoxazole, 3 tablets twice daily for 5
days. In addition fever control and advice on fluids, feeding and home cough
remedies will be given. The new policy discourages the routine use of
antimicrobials, cough syrups containing ephedrine, codeine, atropine, or
alcohol.
15.         Medical Doctors.  Doctors are to be trained to assess rapid
respiratory rate, chest indrawing and inability to drink as more practical and
reliable to diagnose pneumonia than the traditional practices of listening
with a stethoscope, blood or sputum count, or X-ray. Doctors will also judge
the severity of the illness and appropriate treatment by evaluating other
signs such as whether the child stopped eating; child too sleepy to wake;
sharp and loud breathing noises (stridor) even when the child is calm;
wheezing; convulsions; severe undernutrition and the like. Infants and
children with these signs generally will be referred for hospital admission.
In addition to antibiotics, treatment may include round the clock nursing,
intravenous drugs, laboratory and radiological investigations.
Program Implementation Strategy
16.         A medical college is assigned to work with district health
authorities to plan. train, monitor, and evaluate the programs. The district
plans include training of all doctors and female health workers, provision of
instructional materials and the supply of necessary antibiotics (pediatric
cotrimoxazole). Trai.ing will be provided at me,iical schools, district or
taluka hospitals, and selected CHCs/PHCs by members of the District Training
Teams. Other implementation strategies include:
(a)   trained MCH staff will conduct orientation sessions at the sub-
center level to train health guides, community leaders, etc;
(b)   community-based workers will meet mothers in the village through
anganwadi workers and mahila mandals to explain practices and
indications for referral;
(c)   ARI control efforts will be linked closely with immunization
outreach activities, promotion of breast feeding, growth
monitoring and ORT for diarrhoea/management;
(d)   an integrated structure reaching from village workers to district
level officers in.:luding hospital staff and private practitioners;
and



- 112 -
ANNEX 15
Page 4
(e)   the utilization of existing staff and facilities.
17.         The strategy is expecGed to result in successful early detection
and treatment of pneumonia at the peripheral level, reduction of unnecessary use
of many antimicrobials and useless or harmful commercial co1.sgh medicines, and
savings from fewer hospital admissions.

Social Marketing Of Contraceptives
A. Social Marketing Of Condoms (Nirodh)
1.        In order to promote the use of contraceptives, a Social Marketing
Program was launched in 1968 with the participation of 12 Consumer Goods,
Pharmaceutical and Oil Companies both in the public and private sector. A
list of these companies is shown in Table 1. Specific geographic areas were
earmarked for each Company and the product was available through the over
300,000 outlets of these Companies in rural and urban areas. In addition, the
Government also took the following steps to increase the availability of
condoms:
(a) an advertising campaign to create demand was launched. This includ-
ed advertisements through newspapers, journals, TV, Radio, short
films, jingles, cinema slides, hoarding, wall painting and point of
sale materials;
(b) special sales promotion measures like supply of commercial Nirodh in
1/2 gross PWC jars which has the advantage of the open display of
the product at the retail counter and also serve as a bonus for the
retailers who retained the jar, were introduced;
(c) assistance was made available    the 12 Marketing Companies for
arranging display contests as w,1l1 as participation in important
melas and fairs;
(d) the services of Operation R6search Group, (ORG) Baroda was utilized
to carry out family planning sur -ys in 1970 and 1980 to assess the
success of the program as well a.. "o provide monthly data on retail
off-take of condoms of all brands, including Nirodh;
(e) on the basis of findings of the st.rvy carried out by ORG in 1980, a
scheme to pay one paise per piece :oid for and to be used for demand
generation activities was sanctioned to the Marketing Companies in
December, 1983. This scheme was made available to the Companies who
had sold more than 10 million pieces;
(f) initially only dry condoms under the generic name Nirodh were
introduced in the program. However, based on the monthly feed back
received from ORG in regard to choice, lubricated condoms under the
brand name Deluxe Nirodh were introduced in the programme n July,
1984 and were priced at Rs. 11- for a pack of 5 pcs.; and
(g) in the initial stages the price to the consumer was fixed at 15
paise for a pack of 3 pcs. However, this was revised to 25 paise
for three pieces in 1974.



- 115 -
ANNEX 16
Page 2
Sales
2.        Sales under the Social Marketing Program increased from 15.74
million pieces in 1968-69 to 162 milli.jn pieces in 1982-83. However, it
remained stagnant around 200 million pieces during 1983-84 and 1984-85 iaspite
of introducing Deluxe Nirodh as well as assistance to the Marketing Companies
for demand generation. In order to overcome this bottleneck a series of
meetings were held with the result that
(a) the Companies involved in Social Marketing agreed to sell Nirodh as
one of their own products;
(b) the Nirodh publicity campaign which was mainly being carried out
through a Government agency would be carried out by the Companies in
their area of operation. The Government agency would provide
umbrella coverage through T.V., Radio and Cinema. The companies
would be provided assistance e 3 paise per piece sold with effect
from 1.8.1985 (instead of one paise per piece sold which was in
effect from 1.12.1983) for promotional activities. The Companies
were to contribute one paise per piece sold as their contribution to
the national endeavour; and
(c) the State Governments were asked to constitute Coordination
Committees with the representative of the Marketing Companies, State
Governments Officials and representatives of Ministry of Health and
Family Welfare to coordinate and review the progress of Social
Marketing Program in each States and remove the bottlenecks if any.
3.        As a result of this initiative the sales under the program picked up
considerably and increased over to 245 million pcs. in 1985-86. However, it
has again stagnated around 250 million pcs. In 1987, the participation of one
Company (Union Carbide) which covered about 40Z of the country and contributed
about 47Z of the total sale was withdrawn and involvement of additional
Companies/Voluntary Organizations was considered. Parivar Seva Sanstha (Marie
Stopes) a voluntary organization in the field of Family Welfare joined the
Government in this effort and took up a project of selling Condoms under the
brand name 'SAWAN' and 'BLISS' in Haryana with assistance from Ford
Foundation. Similarly, Population Services Interiatcional (India), a non-
profit Society registered under the Societies Act and funded by its associates
PSI (Washington) took up selling of Nirodh in the States of Punjab, Haryana,
Himachal Pradesh, Jammu and Kashmir and Union Territory of Chandigarh under
the brand name 'MASTI'in 25 districts of Uttar Pradesh. These two
organirations have been allowed to extend the sale of branded Condoms in
Punjab and Haryana by Parivar Seva Sanstha and in remaining districts of Uttar
Pradesh ind the Union Territory of Delhi by Population Services International.
4.        During 1987, a third product which is thinner, lubricated as well as
colored and produced by Hindustan Latex Ltd. at its two new plants were
introduced in the program under the brand name 'Super Deluxe Nirodh'. This
was priced at Rs. 2/- for a pack of 4 pcs.

B. Social Marketing of Oral Pills
9.        The program of Social Marketing of Oral Pills was launched in
November, 1987. The ICMR had approved two types of contraceptive pills for
use under the National Family Planning Program. They are:
(a) Norothisterone Acetate           -      1.00 mg.
Ethinyl Estradiol                -      0.03 mg.
(b) DL-Norgestrel                    -      0.50 mg.
Ethinyl Estradiol                -      0.03 mg.
Both formulations of Oral Contraceptive Pills are being procured under the
brand name 'Mala'. To identify the different composition, it was decided to
put 'N' as a suffice for formulation with NEA and 'D' as suffix for
formulation with DL-Norgestrel. Mala-'N' is being provided under the Free
Distribution Program and Hala 'D' is being provided under the Social
Marketing. The package of the product consists of 28 tablets (21 of Oral Pill
and 7 Placebo).

ZBEHIND THE TECHNICAL TERMS
General Notes on Main Direct Causes of Maternal Mortality
(References 'Preventing Maternal Deaths' WHO. Geneva 1989)
Obstructed Labor and Ruptured Uterus
1.          In most cases of obstructed labor, the problem arises because the
space in the bony birth canal of the mother is either too small or too
distorted to permit easy passage of the head of the baby during labor. If
well fed, most women attain their genetically determined maximum stature at
about 18 years with growth of bony pelvis ceasing 3 years later. With poor
nutrition, environmental hygiene, and diseases like malaria, diarrhoea and
measles, growth in stature will be slow, often causing stunting. There is
also a bone disease (osteomalacia) affecting pelvic size (once common among
the poor in large industrial European cities).
2.          Without early treatment, obstructed labor can last for days and
may result in maternal death from infection, exhaustion and death of the fetus
through infection, birth injury, and lack of oxygen. Antibiotics are needed
within 12 hours of membranes rupturing, and after 12 hours for all women
remaining undelivered. Infection may also be the result of traditional local
treatment for prolonged labor, such as putting herbs or other substances into
the vagina. Ruptured uterus is rare among women giving birth for the first
time, but common among women who have borne several children. Once the uterus
ruptures, the woman experiences severe abdomiral pain and tenderness, heavy
bleeding from torn vessels of the uterus and death follows within 24 hours
from hemorrhage and shock. Emergency caesarian section, assisted vaginal
vacuum extraction or forceps delivery are required to treat obstructed labor
and ruptured uterus.
Hemorrhage
3.          There are two main types of hemorrhage: Bleeding which occurs
vaginally before the baby is born (ante-partum hemorrhage) and bleeding which
begins shortly after the birth of the baby (post partum hemorrhage).
4.          Bleeding during pregnancy is always abnormal.  When it occurs
before the 28th week of pregnancy, it is abnormal and is likely to be
associated with abortion. Bleeding after the 28th week of pregnancy may be
due to premature separation of the placenta which is attached to the inside of
uterus and provides the growing fetus with blood and nutrients. Women over 35
years, and with four or more previous births, are commonly affected. The
primary objective of the treatment is to improve woman's general health status
by giving blood transfusion, pain-relieving drugs and supportive measures.
Without treatment the woman will die of blood loss and shock, along with the
baby. Another cause of antepartum hemorrhage is 'placenta previa.' This
means that the placenta lies in an abnormal position innide the uterus, i.e.
blocking the birth canal opening. Bleeding may be slight and painless. The
aim of the treatment is to prolong pregnancy until a mature haby can be born.
Blood transfusion may be necessary and delivery of baby around the 38th week
of pregnancy, not the usual 40th week. Ectopic pregnancy outside the uterus
e.g. in Fallopian tube also causes hemorrhage before the 10th week. If



- 131 -
ANNEX 17
Page 10
untreated, this condition can be fatal in a matter of hours as the tube
ruptures producing pain, shock and bleeding into the abdomen.
5.          Postpartum hemorrhage occurs when excessive bleeding occurs
through the birth canal after the birth of the baby. Normally the placenta is
expelled within 30 minute after birth of baby, and uterine contractions
continue so that bleeding stops. If sections of placenta remain in the uterus
they cause bleeding. An unduly prolonged labor, injuries sustained during
childbirth, rupture of uterus and tears in birth canal can also cause
hemorrhage. The risk of dying from hemorrhage depends on the amount and rate
of blood loss and state of health of the woman. A woman with antepartum
hemorrhage is estimated to have around 12 hours to live unless she receives
treatment, and a woman with postpartum hemorrhage only 2 hours. Hemorrhage is
the biggest single cause of maternal deaths in many developing countries.
Hypertensive Disease of Pregnancy
6.          During the second half of pregnancy, especially during a first
pregnancy, a woman may develop a hypertensive disease. In pre-eclampsia the
blood pressure rises, protein appears in the urine and there is swelling of
tissues, especially of the legs. In some cases this disease remains mild
throughout pregnancy. In others it becomes severe with high blood pressure,
headache, vomiting, disturbance of vision and abdominal pain. In the last
stages (eclampsia) convulsions occur, and if untreated the woman dies of
heart, liver and kidney failure or brain hemorrhage. In tropical Africa the
disease can progress rapidly from first signs to eclampsia within 24 hours,
although average time may be 2 days. Pre-eclampsia is rare in second and
subsequent pregnancies, except with very overweight women, diabetics, a
particular type of blood pressure condition, or in cases of multiple births.
Very young women and women over 35 with first pregnancies are vulnerable. The
cause of pre-eclampsia is unknown. Treatment is symptomatic. Bed rest and
drugs that lower blood pressure are prescribed. Labor and delivery may be
induced around 37 weeks or a Caesarian delivery performed. When eclampsia has
developed, prompt treatment is required. The disease causes a minimum 5?
death rate. Treatment aims at controlling convulsions, lowering blood
pressure, and rapid delivery, with Caesarian if not delivered within 6 hours,
if the mother is to have a chance of survival.  Treatment cortinues after
delivery. To reduce maternal mortality, effective prenatal care and early
detection of women with signs of toxaemia are necessary.
Infection
7.          Women are particularly prone to infection following delivery and
abortion. The placental site or retained placental fragments in the uterus
may become infected. An unhygienic environment, dirty hands, instruments of
birth attendants, or insertion of herbs, leaves, and oils into the vagina
during labor, can also cause infection.
8.          After a normal delivery, infection is rare, irrespective of
hygienic surroundings, if nothing is introduced into the vagina. However,
many women in the Third World do not have straightforward deliveries and are
subject to harmful interference during labor through untrained birth
attendants. As a result, infection (puerperal sepsis) is one of the three



- 132 -                         ANNEX 17
Page 11
main causes of death in developing countries, and particularly high where
there are high rates of illegal abortion.
9.          When it starts, infection is generally confined to the uterus,
with abdominal pain, tenderness, vaginal discharge and fever. If untreated,
infection can be fatal within 6 days as infection spreads to other sites, such
as the fallopian tubes, pelvis, and eventually into the blood stream, causing
abscesses in the brain, muscles and kidneys. Treatment is straightforward in
early cases (such as rest, balanced diet, pain-relieving drugs and
antibiotics). In advanced disease, it is more complicated. Germs that cause
infection are also becoming increasingly resistant to antibiotics, largely as
a result of indiscriminate use of antibiotics. Improvements in personal and
environmental hygiene from home to hospital are important in infection
control.



- 133 -                      ANNEX 18
Page 1
INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Work Routines for Field Workers
Introduction
1.          Multi-Purpose Health Workers (MPHWs) have 47 tasks in their job
descriptions. There is a consensus that they cannot perform all these tasks
for all of the clients needing a service of some kind in a population of 5,000
people. Therefore, under the proposed project, an effort will be made to
systematize the work of field workers so that (a) they concentrate on those
tasks which will have most impact on reducing mortality; (b) tasks are divided
between village level workers so as to make them most productive; and (c)
workers concentrate most of their time on those clients who are most in need
of services. The Universal Immunization Program Plus/Safe Motherhood (UIP
Plus/SM) package to be emphasized under the project already consists of those
interventions which will have most impact on the death rate. This annex sets
out the principles to be applied under the project with regard to items (b)
and (c) above, i.e., task assignments between different village level workers
and prioritization of clients.
Task Assignment
2.          Because of her 18 month training, and because of her special
access to women as a woman, the MPHW Female (MPHWF) is the best qualified
worker at the village level to deliver MCH/FP services. But to increase her
outreach, she must make maximum use of the four other health workers availabile
at the village level in many parts of the country, i.e., the MPHW Male
(MPHWM), the TBA, the VHG and the anganwadi worker (AWW).   The table below
shows how, under the proposed project, tasks would be distributed among the
para-medical workers at the village level so as to make maximum use of the
comparative advantage of each.
Tasks at Village Level for UIP Plus
and Safe Motherhood Proaramme

3.          Of the tasks assigned to trained TBAs, two are of particular
importance. First, the TBA will have a key role in the early identification
of pregnancies, because, as a local woman, she will know about these before
the MPHWF. She is therefore the key to the early provision of ante-natal
care, which is critical to the 'cycle of care' approach to be adopted under
the project (Para 6). Second, she will have a key role in the identification
through weighing of low birth weight babies and in new born care, because in
the cultural context of much of India she will have access to the mother in
the first days after birth where the MPHWF, as a relative outsider, may not.
4.          AWWs are now stationed in almost half of all Indian villages with
over 1,000 population. Because they are women, because they are from the
local area, and because they are at a ratio of 1:1,000 people, they are a
tremendous potential resource for MCH care. In ICDS blocks, it is anticipated
that, in addition to sharing many other responsibilities, AWW will take over
the tasks of IFA and Vitamin A distribution at immunization/MCH sessions, thus
freeing MPHWs to give more time to tasks that orly they have been trained to
do.
Prioritization of Clients
5.          Under the project, it is proposed that workers will concentrate
their attention on pregnant and lactating women, and children with particular
health problems. Beginning regular preventive care during pregnancy is
important because good ante-natal care is (a) the key preventive health
measure for obstetrical complications and (b) of key importance for the
subsequent health of the child in view of the links between maternal health
and nutrition education/maternal weight gain during pregnancy and babies'
birth weights/adequacy of child care in the home. Continuing intensive care
through at least the first six months of lactation is important, because most
infant deaths are either neo-natal or linked with early malnutrition and/or
infection around the time of weaning; this is also a key period for education
on birth spacing.
6.          The intention is therefore to institute a 'cycle of care'
approach, in which the health worker intervenes early in the reproductive



- 136 -
ANNEX 18
Page 4
cycle with necessary information on MCH/FP and identification of women with
actual or potential obstetric complications, rather than later in the cycle at
the time of a difficult birth, or once an LBW infant's health has
deteriorated. Ideally, the cycle of care should begin with education of the
adolescent girl, but it is not yet clear how this can be managed given the
large numbers involved. Therefore, under the project, operations research
will be carried out on how to incorporate' the adolescent girl into the cycle
of care; this will be initiated in ICDS blocks, because of the availability of
additional field workers there. (Annex 30)
7.          Under the proposed project, States will be asked to ensure that
village level workers spend a specified amount of their time each month on (a)
immunization/MCH sessions; (b) home visits focused on pregnant and lactating
women (PLWs), low birth weight infants and children with special health
problems: and (c) ante-natal care/MCH clinics at Sub-Centers. It will be up
to each State or district to decide how this work is scheduled, providing that
(a) schedules allow for priority clients to be visited by MPHWF or AW once a
month; (b) schedules are regular and hence predictable; and (c) schedules are
made known well in advance to both clients and supervisors. As noted below,
these priority MCH activities will absorb only part of the worker's time each
month; States and districts will decide how the remainder of workers' time
will be used, according to local priorities.
8.          Immunization/MCH sessions.   Advantage will be taken of the access
to mothers and children at the monthly immunization sessions already held
throughout the country to also deliver other UIP Plus services, such as IFA
and Vitamin A, and to provide education on the whole range of UIP Plus/SM home
care and services. Immunization sessions will therefore become village
-mmunization/MCH sessions. For a typical MPHW, this activity may take about
five days a month, assuming a monthly session in each of four villages of over
a thousand population, and quarterly sessions in each of three subsidiary
villages. These sessions will be carried out on a specified day of the week,
so that this becomes known to the public.
9.          Home Visits.  MCH sessions will need to be complemented by a
regular program of home visits to priority clients, for two reasons. First,
some program interventions are too lengthy or too intimate to be carried out
at public clinics; examples are initial ANC examinations, or family planning
education. Second, some priority clients will not come to the MCH sessions;
examples are mothers with LBW/malnourished but apparently healthy babies, and
very poor mothers who cannot take time from work, even though their families
may be at high health risk.
10.          It is proposed that health workers should make the provision of
full coverage ante- and post-natal care for pregnant and lactating women and
children a particular priority for their home visit time. The time
implications of the above approach are manageable. On average, there will be
about 150 PLWs in an MPHW's area. Of these, 10-12 will be newly identified
pregnancies in a given month, who need a lengthy ANC exam/education session.
These cases, plus visits to children with particular health problems, might
together take three days a month. The remaining PLWs should need no more than
a 'look-in' visit each month; this might take an additional six days a month



- 137 -
ANNEX 18
Page 5
(140 women x 10 minutes = 23 hours, and assuming that four hours work can be
done on a home visit day, the rest going on travel).   As the program
progresses, and mothers increasingly come to HSC ANC/MCH clinics (see below),
the home visit time spent on ante-natal care by ANM8 will decline.
11.         The feasibility of concentrating home visit time on newly pregnant
women and children under three with particular health problems, depends on
other village health workers--TBAs, VHGs and AWWs--identifying these women and
children before the MPHWF's visit, and being present to brief her on her
arrival. MPHWFs cannot take the time to go house to house looking for child
illness cases.
12.         ANC/MCH Clinics.  MPHWFs cannot complete ANC examinations of newly
pregnant women in the field, since these require a urine test. Therefore,
MPHWFs will hold an ANC clinic for two to three hours once a week at the HSC
to deal with these cases. As with immunization sessions, the opportunity will
be taken to offer the full range of MCH/FP services and, like immunization/MCH
sessions, ANC/MCH clinics will be held on a particular day of the week.
13.         Other priorities.  The time taken by the routines outlined above
will be about 16 days a month, counting immunization/MCH sessions, home visits
and ANC/MCH clinics. Assuming that workers have 25 working days a month of
which five are required for attending meetings, record maintenance, taking FP
cases to the PHC etc., this leaves approximately four days a month for other
direct service provision, IEC or community mobilization activities.
Priorities for the use of this time may be set by States and districts, but it
will be preferable if they could be set by local workers and their
communities, on the basis of perceived local needs. Likely priority uses for
remaining worker time will be HSC clinics for curative care cases; visits to
other high risk families (e.g. poor mothers not coming for services); group
IEC meetings for clients and influencers; and individual IEC sessions for
resistant clients. A sample monthly work plan for a typical MPHWF is given
below.

14.         Changing the cpaign approach to family planning.  Currently,
family planning work is mainly undertaken on a seasonal, campaign basis
between January and March each year. The pressure on workers to meet their
family planning targets by the end of March--the end of the performance year--
is a major factor behind this seasonal drive. While it makes sense for
sterilizations to take place during the cooler months when the risks of
infection are least, motivational and birth spacing work should take place
throughout the year, integrated with MCH service provision. Preoccupation
with family planning work during the winter months should not be allowed to
interfere with the proposed routine home visit round to pregnant and lactating
women. It will therefore be essential for States and districts participating
in the proposed project to refrain from pressuring workers to give priority to
family planning work at the expense of MCH work in the last quarter of the
year. Substantial field experience in India shows that integrated provision
of MCH/FP care does not threaten the achievement of FP targets; on the
contrary, mothers' confidence in health workers built up through the provision
of good MCH care actually facilitates the achievement C FP targets,
especially for the temporary methods.
Work Routines For Field Supervision
15.         The quality of service delivery and IEC work under the proposed
project will depend critically on the support and on the job training that
workers receive from supervisors. The quality of this support and training in
turn depends on supervisors' familiarity with what is happening in the field,
and the regularity of their contacts with field workers. States and districts
will develop their own work routines for field supervision. But States will
be asked to ensure that these meet the following minimum stav ards without
which quality control over the UIP+/SM program cannot be ensured. First, HAs
Male and Female should spend a minimum of eight working days a month in the
field, of which half should be working with an MPW at an immunization/MCH
session or ANC/MCH clinic, and half should be accompanying an MPHW on a home
visit day. Assuming six MPHWs per HA, this means each MPHW will receive
support and on the job training from a supervisor twice in six weeks--once at
a clinic, once on a home visit day.
16.         Second, each PHC and CHC MO and each district level officer should
spend a minimum of four days a month in the field working on the UIP Plus/SM
program. The number of required supervision visits is less than for HAs, to
take account of the realities of MOs' curative care work at the PHC/CHC, the
pressure of administrative work at the district level, and the
responsibilities of managers at both levels for health programs other than UIP
Plus/SM. But because the required number of visits is small, compliance can
be legitimately enforced. These routines mean that the average MPW should
receive a field visit from an MO at least once a month (assuming two MOs per
new PHC). Each CHC would receive a visit at least once in two months
(assuming 20 CHCs per district and three medically qualified supervisory
personnel available for field supervision at the district level). District
staff visiting a CHC should set aside a whole day per visit, so that they can
also visit one PHC and one HSC (a different one each time) in that CHC's area.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Information, Education and Comunication Strategy
A. Introduction
1.          The Government of India's (GOI) Seventh and draft Eighth Five-Year
Plans calls tor lE&C fectivities to contribute to: (a) improved child survival
through promotion of immunization, oral rehydration therapy (ORT) and other
child development services; (b) improvements in women's health by increased
acceptance of antenatal services, deliveries through trained personnel and
telescoping of fertility as far as possible ir women's twenties; and (c)
promoting the status of women with a view to empowering them as full partners
and decision makers in the family and the community.
2.          Promotion of women's health status within the family and
community, increased utilization of MCH services, particularly at the primary
level and, enhanced home care health management, are the cornerstones of the
GOI's MCH program strategy. The GOI has acknowledged, however, that serious
obstacles and barriers exist that constrain the realization of its national
MCH goals and program objectives. Lack of client knowledge of available
services, poor motivation to use services and misconceptions and
misinformation about the need for and use of services, remain obstacles to
generating and sustaining client demand. Negative perceptions about the
ability of available services to provide the level of care required, i.e.
range of MCH services, drugs, etc. remain constraints to the utilization of
government services and health compliance.
3.          On the service delivery or supply side, problems relate to a lack
of quality services, particularly at the village level, and poor health
functionary knowledge of maternal mortality health technology and ineffective
communication skills.
4.          The Family Welfare Department of the MOHFW is responsible for
family planning and maternal and child health services. Within this
Department, the Mass Education and Media, (MEM) division has primary
responsibility for formulating and implementing MOHFW IE&C programs. In
specific program areas, such as UIP and UIP Plus, the concerned section is the
UIP section which has an officer responsible for conceptualizing and
implementing UIP communication programs.
5.          IE&C is viewed by the MOHFW as an important tool in bringing about
the proposed transformation of the national MCH profile. However, the program
effectiveness of the MEM division has been weakened seriously, particularly at
the State and district levels, by workers' inability to plan and implement
IE&C programs that address and meet localized health issues and client needs.
Other issues, such as poor program integration and rapport among the various
levels, limited worker coordination and resource utilization between the
various mateL.aal and child health welfare, and family welfare programs also
constrain program impact. As a result, IE&C activities conducted by the MEM
division are failing to meet program impact expectations. This status-quo



- 140 -
ANNEX 19
Page 2
changes significantly however, within area projects developed with donor
support.
B. Profile of Donor Supported MCH/IE&C Interventions
6.          UNICEF is a major donor supporting GOI's MCH programs.  Following
successful implementation of a UIP communication program in collaboration with
the UIP section of the MOHFW, UNICEF is planning to make a significant
investment in IE&C for Safe Motherhood and Child Survival. Key elements of
its UIP/IE&C program have been: (a) strong linkage between the health service
and the IE&C program; (b) use of teams comprising Angandwadi workers, DWCRA
group leaders, ANM8, and Gramsavaks to conduct village drives; (c) use of
formative research to guide strategy design and message development
activities; (d) employment of the private sector to develop creative messages
and materials; and (e) use of action oriented messages to trigger desired
responses. In its 1991-1995 program, UNICEF proposes to assist the GOI in
developing an umbrella national communication strategy for all related
component areas under the Child Survival and Safe Motherhood project. A
specific communication strategy will be developed for birth spacing.
Communication activities would focus on: (a) creating and increasing awareness
that a significant portion of health problems can be prevented by proper home
management by people themselves; and (b) care available through service
delivery units. Existing institutions/organizations would be supported to
function as a network of wprimary health care resource centers' which would be
developed to disseminate and provide information on health care for people.
The UNICEF strategy also aims at supporting advocacy efforts to (a) make safe
motherhood a societal priority; (b) enhance the welfare and well being of the
child; anG (c) sustain, mobilize and expand governmental and non-governmental
organization efforts for the "package" of MCH services.
7.          USAID is in the final year of financing the IE&C Training Project
which began in 1987 in the States of Uttar Pradesh, Rajasthan, Madhya Pradesh
and Bihar. This project has four major components: (a) preparation of visit
schedules for health personnel at various levels; (b) continuous training of
district, PHC, sector and subcenter level staff; (c) qualitative improvement
in supervision; and 'd) introduction of systems for monitoring and evaluation.
Mid-project evaluation findings are that innovations and positive results have
been achieved in some pockets of the project districts. For example, fixed
health worker visit schedules have introduced some predictability about visits
of health functionaries. However, visit schedules have been affected by a
variety of manpower and other issues. In some districts, villages have become
more aware of the available services and this, in turn, has generated greater
demand. This project is being absorbed into other donor area projects and the
MOHFW plans over time to institute it on a national basis.
8.          UNFPA.  The Health and Family Welfare area Project being
implemented by UNFPA in the state of Rajasthan has a major IE&C component.
This project ends in 1994. The IE&C component supports the reorganization and
integration of the existing MEM Wing, State Health Education Bureau and other
Publicity/Health Education Cells of the Directorate of Medical Health and
Family Welfare into a single unit called the State Health Extension, IE&C
Bureau.



- 141 -
ANNEX 19
Page 3
9.          IDA. The Second Tamil Nadu Integrated Nutrition II and ICDS I
project are among those IDA sul'ported projects making valuable contributions
in the MCH/IE&C area. Both projects have adopted the social marketing process
to program design and implementation. Hallmarks of the social marketing
process are; (a) its emphasis on approaching the problem from the client's
perspective; (b) use of formative research and field testing; (c) employment
of professional agencies to assist in program implementationg and (d) use of a
mix of mass and interpersonal media. As part of GOI support to these
initiatives, the Department of Women and Child Development has recently added
a communication expert to the ICDS Directorate at the center. These projects
are operational in Tamil Nadu, Orissa, and Andhra Pradesh.
C. Aims and Oblectives
10.         The UIP section of the MOHFW vhich has been spearheading the UIP
IE&C program will also undertake the project's IE&C program in collaboration
with other MOHFW departments. The IE&C component of the project will directly
support three major MCH program goals. These are to:
-     Increase utilization of available MCH services;
*     Increase home care health management; and
*     Promote the safe motherhood in.ttiative.
General IE&C program objectives will be to:
Upgrade and increase key health functionary, knowledge of maternal and
child health technology and communication skills;
*     Generate and sustain client demand for specific health care including
ORT, attended deliveries, antenatal and postpartum services;
*     Develop integrated program supporting communication strategies and
activities for community outreach and demand generation;
*     Enhance community elucation by health functionaries through the use of a
mix of media approaches targeted at specific audience segments;
*     Advocate at national and -tate levels for the state motherhood
initiative; and
*     Increase program efficiency through the use of operations research and
ongoing monitoring and tracking exercises.
11.         The scope of component activities will be national, however,
specific program activities will be implemented in a phased manner in specific
districts within states. The emphasis will be on States in the Hindu belt.
(Refer to Annex 8: Phasing of Districts for VIP Plus and Safe Motherhood).
D. StrateRY
12.         The GOI views the Child Survival and Safe Motherhood project as a
functional program to operationalize key aspects of the MCH program. As such,
the underlying strategy will be to move away from standard approaches in
program design and implementation and use new processes wherever applicable.



- 142 -
ANNEX 19
Page 4
Thus, for program planners, the planning and implementation process that will
be used for the component is as important as the outcome of the activities.
13.         The HOHFW's strategy for the IE&C component is to build on the
IE&C experiences of the ICDS, TNINP I and UIP programs to put in place a new
approach to IE&C activities using existing infrastructure. In this approach,
the Center and States would have distinct program roles. For the Center, key
roles would be to develop an umbrella comnunication effort for UIP Plus and
Safe Motherhood activities, coordinate planning, manage the program and its
resources, and produce prototype materials for adaptation and use by the
states. The Center plans to use a systematic process to program planning
involving the private sector, key IE&C experts and institutions. The
operational model that would be followed is the UIP communication model.
14.         The States' role within the project would be to adopt key elements
of the national umbrella strategy and implement structured but localized IE&C
programs and activities in accordance with program plans worked out at the
central and state levels. Emphasis would be placed on giving states the
resources to develop flexible and innovative program activities. At the
district level, program implementors would be encouraged to shift their
program focus, within the framework of given campaigns and program activities,
away from awareness creation to action stimulation.
15.         The MEM views the role of IE&C within the project as a vital one.
The MEM's has introduced at the state level some new programs aimed at
decentralizing planning and materials development activities and shifting
program focus from attainment of numerical targets as a indicator of program
success and impact to, use of client-related behavioral change and compliance
targets. Key MEM staff within the projects also point to the need for
emphasis on providing information and education on services, as well as
training grassroots level functionaries and media personnel to impart accurate
messages. The MEM staff has proposed the use of women's groups, Mahila
Swasthya Sang, for village outreach; employ of private producers for
television and radio productions and the development of film spots using
famous film stars as key influentials.
E. Component Activities
16.         Key activities to be conducted under the project are: at the
Central level:
-     IE&C strategy development and program planning workshops
-     design of media campaigns
-     development of prototype materials for service givers and clients
at the State level:
-     formative and process research (community analyses and service level
inventories)
-     IE&C strategy development and program planning workshops
-     adaptation of prototype materials
-     multi-media, ongoing campaigns
-     select small-scale operations research activities
-     fnnovative community outreach and education activities

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Functional Guidelines for Field Level Recordkeepinp
1.          Over the almost three decades of the family welfare program, there
has been a series of efforts to develop management information (and
evaluation) systems. (MIS/MIES) in the different states. While the
requirements of the Government of India have served to standardize to a large
extent the data that are collected and transmitted, the means and formats for
recording data at field level and their use for monitoring purposes vary
widely from state to state.
2.          Given these circumstances, any effort to institute a uniform
recordkeeping system at subcenter level at this point in time would be
counterproductive and futile. Instead, the project will aim to bring about
modification in subcenter recordkeeping where necessary in order to bring it
into conformity with the following functional guidelines:
(a)   the objective of recordkeeping at field level is to produce data
that will be of use to field workers, supervisors and program
managers for purposes of monitoring, feedback and planning.
(b)   the system of recordkeeping should be designed so as to put the
least possible burden on the field worker who does the actual
recording. This means;
Ci)   consolidation of records and rigorous elimination of
duplication;
(ii)  consideration of reporting of forms (e.g., there should be
only one consolidated monthly subcenter report sent to the
PHC); and
(iii) provision of printed registers, cards, reporting forms, etc.
(c)   the system of recordkeeping should be designed so as to facilitate
assessment of both;
(i)   the current status of individual members of the target
population;
(ii)  the activities carried out by the worker in the reporting
period.
(d)   the system of recordk2eping should be designed so as to facilitate
the calculation of coveztee rates for various services from
numerators and denominators derived from the system itself.
Specifically, this means that complete records of alls (i) married
woman ages 15-44; (ii) p2egnant woman; (iii) births; and (iv)
under-fives; must be maintained if family planning and MCH
coverage rates are to be calculated.
(e)   records should be kept up to date on an ongoing basis; annual
updating of records should not be substituted for keeping records
current.



- 154 -                         ANNEX 21
Page 1
INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Ensuring The Qglity Of Drugs In India 1/
Introduction
1.          The quality of drugs in India is regulated under the Drugs and
Cosmetics Act of 1940 and the Drugs and Cosmetics Rules of 1945. Under these
laws, the role of the Central Government (GOI) and State Governments has been
well defined. State Governments are responsible for the licensing, sale,
distribution and manufacture of drugs in the States. The GOI is concerned
with the approval of new drugs, the quality of imported drugs, updating
standards of drugs, amendment of legislation and coordination of activL:ies
with the Stato Drug Controllers for uniform enforcement.
2           Laboratory Quality Control including Standard and Reference
Substances. There are two Central Drug Testing Laboratories in India: the
Central Drugs Laboratory, Calcutta, and the Central Indian Pharmacopoeia
Laboratory, Ghaziabad. The Central Drugs Laboratory, Calcutta, maintains
reference standards of drugs included in Pharmacopoeias and provide the
reference standards to drug manufacturers and approved laboratories. For sera
and vaccines, the Central Research Institute, Kasauli, is the statutory
reference laboratory. The Central Drugs Laboratory is a WHO collaborating
Center.
3.          Laboratory quality control is also provided through a network of
Central Laboratories and through the Laboratories in the States to meet the
increased pressure of work with the expansion of formulation manufacturing
units. Private laboratories are also permitted to test and are specifically
approved for the purpose. Under the present requirements of the Drugs Rules,
each manufacturing unit is expected to have its own laboratory testing
facility for both bulk drugs and formulations made thereof. The present
licensing procedure has been made very stringent by making it obligatory of
all units to have this facility whether they are new or applying for the
renewal of a license.
4.          Good Manufacturing Practices.  A new schedule 'M' was added to the
Drugs Rules in 1988 and has been implemented. India is also participating in
the WHO Certification Scheme for the export of drugs whereby manufacturing
units are required to follow WHO Good Manufacturing Practices. At present,
the inspection of units which apply for a certificate under the WHO scheme is
carried out generally by a team comprising the Central Drugs Standard Control
Organization (CDSCO) and State Drug Control Authorities. The final
certificate is issued by the State depending on the results of the inspection.
1/  Prepared by WHO and UNICEF, New Delhi.



- 155 -
ANNEX 21
Page 2
S.          The progress made in the short period airter the notification of
Schedule 'X' is satisfactory and is expected to improve further with proposed
strengthening of both testing facilities as well as the inspecting staff.
6.          Regulatory Control.  In  addition to the self-regulation exercised
by the manufacturers, the Drugs Control Organization also exercises through
its team of Drug Inspectors, a regulatory control by drawing samples of
formulations which are available in the market for sale to the general public
or are supplied to the public/private hospitals and institutions. Stringent
measures are taken if eny sample fails the quality control tests.
7.          Vaccines.  WHO has been collaborating with countries of the South
East Asia Region (SEAR) since 1977 in the development of vaccine programs.
The vaccines produced are in accordance with WHO specifications and are tested
in the CRI, Kasauli, which is recognized by WHO.
8.          With technical and financial inputs, WHO and UNICEF have now
strengthened facilities for production of bacterial and some of the viral
vaccines in India. India has also developed capabilities for the production
of DPT vaccine, rabies vaccine and polio vaccine. Several Indians have been
trained and new technology (fermentation) was introduced by WHO. WHO has also
organized an inter-regional seminar at the Pasteur Institute, Conor. to
establish tisiue culture technology for the production of rabies vaccine.
9.          Quality Control of Vaccines.  WHO's major thrust in the South-East
Asia Region has been to establish expertise in quality control of bacterial
and viral vaccines. Nationals from several countries of the region were
trained in quality control procedures and techniques. Intercountry and
national seminars were organized for training of nationals in quality control
of DPT, polio and measles vaccines. A network of WHO Collaborating Centers
has been established in the region: in India, Indonesia and Thailand, where
facilities are available for quality control of DPT vaccine and training of
nationals in quality control procedures. International consultants
recommended by WHO, and working on the request of GOI, UNICEF and WHO, have
reviewed vaccine production and quality under the Universal Immunization
Program (UIP) and found them both satisfactory.
10.         Future Trends in Vaccinology.  In the future, WHO will concentrate
on the transfer of technology for the development of new vaccines,
particularly the viral vaccines for hepatitis, rabies, and new bacterial
vaccines, and will develop training programs for vaccine production and
quality control.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Basis of Prolect Cost Estimates
Introduction
1.          The estimates are based on MOHFW projections of expenditures for
the years 1991/92 through 1994/95 with some retroactivity to October 1990.
Investment Costs
2.          Civil works costs are based on the cost of minor extensions and
repairs about 6,300 existing PHCs and CHCs and construction of 120 stores and
about 1,700 subcenters. Estimates for improvements or extensions to the first
level referral facilities such as CHCs for the Safe Motherhood program are
based on the preliminary results of the Facility Survey.
3.          Professional fees for design and supervision of construction are
estimated on the basis of private practice in India which total about 15Z of
the cost of civil works.
4,          Cold-chain equipment is based on the costs of detailed equipment
lists developed by MOHFW and UNICEF. These lists are included in the Project
File.
5.          Supplementary equipment for Sub-centers, PHC, and CHCs in the
Districts under the Safe Motherhood Initiative, are based on the costs of
lists of equipment developed by MOHFW and UNICEF on the norm of 400
Subcenters, 50 PHCs and 10 CHCs per district at unit cost of RS 6,500, 20,000
and 100,000 respectively.
6.          Educational Materials and MCH Supplies.  Materials for training
and medical and MCH supplies, postage, stationery, and office material are
estimated on the costs of annual requirements determined by MOHFW and reviewed
by the appraisal mission. Syringes and needles costs are based on estimates of
annual requirements of about 6.0 million syringes and 4.0 million needles at
the annual cost of Rs. 68.42 million.
7.          Vehicles are based on the provision of two jeeps/vans per district
for all 466 districts and 20 tr--wheelers per district for the 180 districts
under the Safe Motherhood Initiative.
8.          Training is based on norms agreed among MOHFW, UNICEF, and the
appraisal mission for each type of medical, managerial, paramedical and other
staff to be trained in a typical d-trict under the UIP "Plus" and the Safe
Motherhood programs respectively. Costs include the travel and daily
allowance of the trainers and trainees, and the cost of development and
distribution of training materials. More precisely, the estimates cover the
cost of training of (a) the National Core Training Team (100); (b) the cost of
the Leadership Team at the Center and in the States (134); (c) the cost of
