INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Proiect Additionalitvy and Sustainability
The Health Sector
1.    Achievements in health and population over the past 20 vears in India
have been impressive. With per capita income of about US$ 300, India's life
expectancy at birth of 57 represents a gain of 30 percent in two decades,
exceeding the level reached in Japan just 45 years when per capita income was
considerably higher than in today's India. Despite impressive achievements,
w'men and children continue to face serious health challenges.  Unlike other
Asian countries where women on average live 2 to 7 years longer than men, life
expectancy for Indian women lag behind men, and infant mortality, while
declining, is still high in most rural India. Launching the family welfare
program, with special emphasis on safe motherhood and child health, the
central government intends to correct misallocation of resources at home away
from women and children, and the state governments tendency to favor more
curative services at the tertiary hospital level.
2.    India spends about four percent of its GNP on health.  This share
compares favorably with the Asian average of 3.1 but is about half the 8
percent spent on health in industrialized countries. Private out of pocket
expenditures account for about two thirds of total spending. Per capita
government expenditures amounts to USS 4.00, of which the center spends USS
1.00 and the States about USS 3.00. India remains almost exclusively
dependent on tax revenues to support all government activities in health.
User fees and health insurance finance a negligible share of government
expenditures. The range of public per capita state expenditures is very
large.  Himachal P.adesh spends 5 times more per person (150 Rs.) than Uttar
Pradesh (28 Rs.) or Bihar (29 Rs.). Central Government expenditures on family
welfare and HCH programs are more equitably distributed across states. Indeed
the MCH program is designed to target the poorer states and to offset state
government expenditures which favor hospitals and curative services.
Family Welfare Program (FWP)
3.    Resource allocations over the last two decades reflect increasing
government commitment to the program. Financial outlays under the Seventh
Plan (32560 million Rs.) were six hundred times greater than those allocated
under the Third Plan. The share of FWP to total health expenditures increased
from 10 percent during the Third Plan to almost 50 percent during the Seventh
Plan and compared to total public sector expenditures from 0.3 to 1.8 percent.
MCH is a key and growing component of FWP, accounting for about one-fourth of
expenditures in 1990, up from 16 percent five years earlier. Operational
expenses account for three fourths the expenditures, drugs and vaccines for
over 10 percent, and staff training absorbed 3 percent of total capital and
recurrent expenditures, a significant share implying emphasis on quality
improvements in service delivery.
4.    As public sector budgets increased, FWP and MCH activities competed
favorably for more than proportionate increases of resource allocations.



- 168 -
ANNEX 26
Page 2
During tight budgetary squeeze, however, they were at a disadvantage. The
recent difficult budgetary conditions, further aggravated by the Gulf crisis,
resulted in reduced allocations for FWP and HCH programs. The 1990/91 FWP
allocations of 6750 million Rs. were 142 less than the 1989/90 expenditures
(7810 million Rs.). For HCH activities, allocations were reduced by 3 percent
from 1970 to 1900 million Rs. The decline was the first in the history of
these programs and implies vulnerability during periods of stagnating overall
government budgets.
5.    The MCH project will reverse the sluggish start of the first year of the
new planning period (1991-95) as GOI adjusts its resource allocation criteria
in favor of externally assisted projects during periods of budget
difficulties. The proposed 1991/92 MCH allocations of 574 million Rs.
represents the highest annual increase since the inception of the program
(200X) and amounts to 384 million Rs. ($ 192 million) over 1990t91
allocations. The proposed allocations for the remaining years of the Eighth
Plan 1991-95 would increase at 112 p.a. over the 1991/92 allocations. By
1995, MCH will account for one-third of total PWP expenditures, an increase
from one-fourth in 1990.
Project Additionality
6.    Resource allocation for the MCH program, with and without the MCH
project (supported by UNICEF and IDA), are presented in Table 6. Six
scenarios depicting probable allocations without the project have been
identified in Tables 7-14. The first assumes a continuation of the decline at
the 1989/90-1990/91 level of 3.3?. The second postulates that the decline
will be arrested in 1991/92 and will remain stable for the consecutive three
years. The third scenario applies the same annual rates of growth experienced
during the previous five years of the Seventh Plan - a very optimistic
scenario. The fourth, fifth and sixth scenarios assume 10, 20 and 30 percent
annual increases during the 1991-95 period. The proposed resource allocations
"with the project" are given annually in Table 3.
7.    The basic conclusion is that external assistaLce to the MCH program,
through the project, would be mostly, if not totally additional resources over
what would be predicted had external assistance not been provided. By the end
of 1992, "additional, resource allocations would range from US$ 147 million
under scenario 3 to US$ 195 million under scenario 1. By the end of 1993,
cumulative additional resource allocations, again mostly on account of the
project, would range from US$ 315 to US$ 431 million.
Sustainabi ity
8.    Under the present financial circumstances in India, it would be
difficult to present a quantifiable set of indicators ensuring availability cf
financial resources to sustain FWP/MCH activities at meaningful levels beyond
the project's life cycle. However, in addition to government assurances,
several qualitative factors provide positive indications of program
sustainability.
(a)   When the project is completed in FY9!, recurrent expenditures on
the project would represent a very small share - 3.4? of total GOI
expenditures on health and family welfare programs.



-169 -                         ANNEX 26
Page 3
(b)   The Central Government finances one hundred percent of capital and
recurrent expenditures of the FWPIMCH program. A unique features
of this program is that during the successive five-year plans, no
liability of the previous plans has either been transferred to the
non-plan budget of the states or the central government. All
liabilities will continue to be paid by the central government
from the "Plan* budget. Inasmuch as central government revenues
are more stable than those of state governments, the FWP/MCH
activities are less likely to experience major fluctuations in
resource allocations.
(c)   FWP/MCH program is one among six special schemes comprising the
'Technology Mission' program for which the central government has
made a commitment to allocate financial resources on a priority
basis; a moral shield if not a guarantee against major budgetary
reductions.
(d)   The Central Government launched FWP program to redress the growing
inequitable distribution of health expenditures as state
government expenditures favored second and tertiary level services
(mainly hospitals) at the expense of primary, preventive care
services.  FWPIMCH activities disproportionately benefit the
poorer segment of the population as FWP expenditares represent a
larger share of total health expenditures in sates with lower per
capita income compared with the more endowed states. In addition,
the need to sustain the MCH program will especially be true in
times of fiscal constraints to assist in mitigating the
consequences of macroeconomic stabilization.
Ce)   Successful implementation of an integrated package of MCH
activities under the project would set in place an effective
infrastructure across the country, supported by institutional
mechanisms, information, monitoring and evaluation systems and
trained staff that would not require duplication in future years,
thus easing the pressure for continued increases in financial
outlays. The cost of maintaining these efforts would increase but
at much lower levels than initial program set-up.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE HOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Coordination Between Ministry of Health and
Family Welfare and Women and Child Department
Introduction
1.          Cooperation between family welfare and ICDS workers in the field
has improved in recent years. One way in which this has been fostered is by
holding immunization sessions at Anganwadi Centers, with worker from both
programs assisting. Under the proposed project, efforts will be made to build
on this beginning to achieve similar levels of cooperation in implementing the
other key UIP Plus/Safe Motherhood interventions. The gains from improved
cooperation are potentially very significant since ICDS now covers about 40Z
of India, including most of the most backward and hard-to-reach areas. ICDS'
288.000 Anganwadi Works (AWs) offer an enormous potential for increasing
health service outreach in these areas. Both programs stand to gain from
improved cooperation since their aims are so similar. For example, the
following interventions under the proposed project are already recognized
elements of the ICDS service package:
(a)   Anaemia prophylaxis for pregnant and lactating women;
(b)   Immunization;
5C)   Ante- and post-natal care including high risk referrals;
(d)   Diarrheal disease c ntrol;
(e)   Health check/referral for under-fives;
(f)   Vitamin A prophylaxis for under-fivesg and
(g)   Identification and referral of ARIs.
2.          This Annex sumnarizes several Government Orders issued in early
1991 with the aim of improving coordination. These orders are important,
because they help to clarify roles for workers, and demonstrate to field staff
that improving cooperation is seen as a serious issue. But GOs alone may not
be sufficient to change time-worn practices in the field. Therefore, measures
will be taken under the proposed project in five additional areas to reinforce
and complement the existing instructions:
(a)   joint planning;
(b)   clarification of task assignments and work routines;
(c)   joint supervision;
(d)   informal joint training; and
(e)   harmonization of formal training curricula.



- 179 -                          ANNEX 27
Page 2
Recent Government Orders
3.          In February, 1991. the following orders were issued by both Family
Welfare and Women and Child Development Departments:
(a)   Multi-Purpose Health Workers Female (MPHWFs) to visit each
Anganwadi Center (AWC) in their area at least once a fortnight, on
a predetermined day;
(b)   Delivery kits to be routed through AWs to TBAs and pregnant
women;
(c)   Vitamin A to be distributed by AWs;
(d)   Health staff to attend monthly meetings held by ICDS staff;
(e)   Referral of cases from ICDS to health services to be strengthened
by:
*i)   issuing quick 'priority' referral slips for all ICDS cases;
(ii)  creating an exclusive counter at PHCs/CHCs/district
hospitals for attention to ICDS referral cases; and
(iii) number of referral cases to be a special agenda item for
monitoring and discussion at monthly meetings.
Joint Planning
4.          ICI)S staff should be fully involved in the annual planning process
at the district level, since ICDS is an intrinsic and important part of
district health services. ICDS staff have in particular an important
substantive contributi.n to make to target-setting at the district level,
since special targets say need to be set for ICDS blocks. With the greater
outreach available in lCDS areas, it may be realistic to plan for project
coverage targets to be achieved faster in these Blocks than over the district
as a whole. As well as the substantive contribution, the process of joint
planning will have important benefits. Officials who have worked together to
produce a District plan will have greater commitment to jointly achieving its
goals.
Task Assignments anJ Work Routines
5.          The proposed distribution of tasks between AWWs, and MPHWs, TBAs
aind VHGs is shown in Annex 18. The two project interventions for which AWWs
would be primarily vesponsible at MCH sessions would be the distribution of
Vitamin A and of IFA. With regard to the other project interventions, MPHWs
and AWs would work to cooperate, and ensure that these are incorporated into
field workers" work routines via the supervision and training system.
6.          AWWs would assist the MPHW in the following main wayst
(a)   alert mothers to the timing of upcoming immunization/MCH sessions
to be held in their village, encourage mothers to come, attend and



- 180 --
ANNEX 27
Page 3
assist at the sessions, be available to mothers in subsequent days
for follow-up;
(b)   meet with the MPW on her arrival in the village on home visit
days, to brief her on the priority clients to visit. These would
in particular include newly pregnant women; children with health
problems such as severe ARls; severely malnourished children, or
malnourished children with infection; and clients, especially poor
clients, not making use of program services;
(c)   accompany the MPW on selected home visits in the afternoon (the
AWW, who is a part-tine, low-paid worker, and who has a full
program of work at the AWC in the mornings, cannot be expected to
escort the HPHW throughout all of her rounds).  AWs and MPHWFs
should especially aim to jointly visit rwothers not making use of
services; and LBW/malnourished children who may need referral, or
special follow-up care from the AW; and
(d)   help in the achievement of MCh targets, including birth-spacing,
by advising the MPHWF of clients needing particular services, and
by educating and encouraging mothers.
7.          In order for AWs to have an incentive to assist MPWs in the above
way, it is important that their cooperation be reciprocated by help from the
MPW, in the following main ways:
(a)   advApe on appropriate dare/referral for children who have either
persistent malnutrition or malnutrition complicated by infection.
These are cases likely to be beyond the AW's skills to treat;
(b)   help in reducing malnutrition by using the opportunity of her
regular ante-natal care visits to stress to mothers the importance
of eating more during pregnancy; taking advantage of supplementary
feeding at the AWC; and of rigular growth monitoring for the
child;
(c)   use her greater education/IEC skillslsocial standing to assist the
AWW to put across key messages at group meetings; to encourage the
participation of resistant clients; and to develop the support of
local influential persons for both FW and ICDS programs; and
(d)   help the AWW with the completion of her records.  This will be
particularly important in tribal areas where AWWs are often not
fully literate.
Joint Supervision
8.          Greatir cooperation can also be fostered by joint supervision of
field workers. This has both substantive benefits, in temss of greater
understanding of each others' program; and proceas benefits, in that if field
workers are visited by their supervisor from both programs simultaneously,
this sends a clear message about the importance of working together at the
village level. It is proposed that ICDS and health staff at the district
level should carry out at least one joint supervisory field visit a month;



- 181 -
ANNEX 27
Page 4
BMOs/CDPOs at least four per month; and MOs/Mukyha Sevikas at the PHC level at
least four per month.
Joint Informal Training
9.          Revised task assignments and work routines, along with the new
special emphases of the family welfare program on the UIP Plus/Safe Motherhood
interventions, can best be communicated by training workers from both programs
together in the groups in which they will cooperate. At the higher levels of
the two services, it is proposed that this should be done through a series of
two-day annual workshops, for both program managers and trainers. For
managers, separate workshops will be held at the State and district level, to
orient them to the UIP Plus/Safe Motherhood program, prior to the holding of
the first district planning exercise.
10.         For trainers, three types of workshops will be held.  One, at the
regional level, will bring together trainers from Middle-Level ICDS Training
Centers with principal trainers from Health and Family Welfare Training
Centers, plus the National Core Training Team to be formed under the proposed
project. The second, at the State level, will bring together the Medical
College consiltants and otber trainers used by the ICDS Central Technical
Committee to conduct the Integrated Training Scheme for ICDS, the trainers of
State Anganwadi Training Centers, and the State Core Training Team. The
third, at the district level, will bring together the District Training Team
to be created under the proposed project, with the medical consultants
attached to the ICDS blocks in the district. In each case, the purpose would
be orient the participants to the priorities to be adopted by the family
welfare program under the Eighth Plan, and to ensure that a common approach to
training workers of both services is taken. In each case also, state or
district managers from both programs would be asked to participate, to promote
the exchange of experience between the field and classroom.
11.         At the field level, MOs and CDPOs would have received joint
orientation at the district-level workshops. Thereafter, a series of
workshops would be held at the block-ltvel to bring together all ICDS and
family welfare field staff in a given sector- i.e. a group of up to three
supervisors (two HAs and an MS), ten MPWs (male and female) and 20-25 AWWs.
The trainers would be the BMO and the CDPO, assisted by a member of the
District Training Team. The purpose would be to orient the group as a team to
the proposed family welfare prioritiros; work out plans for the sector area in
the context of the district and block level plans already developed; and to
address problems in service delivery, IEC or inter-departmental cooperation in
the local area. Such workshops would be carried out on annual basis to review
progress and problems, and to draw up plans for the succeeding year.
Harmonizing Basic Training Curricula and Operational Strategies
12.         The orientation training summarized above would be informal, based
on curricula to be developed jointly by MOHFW and WCD. In addition, there is
a need to ensure that on-going formal training course curricula in both family
welfare and ICDS, as well as ICDS operational strategies, reflect the
priorities of the proposed Eighth Plan family welfare program. Issues which
must be addressed include, for example, the need to reflect the special
emphases of the UIP Plus program; to incorporate the new strategies for IFA

Page 5
and Vitamin A; to develop a new module for Safe Motherhood, including a
consistent set of guidelines for identifying obstetric complications and for
referrals; and to revise ICDS training curricula to follow the new policy
guidelines on diarrhoeal disease control developed by MOHFW. In these and
other areas, changes will need to be made to the basic and in-service train3ng
curricula for both MPWs and AWWs, and therefore also in supervisor training

systematically identify needed changes, which will then be pursued with
relevant authorities such as NIPCCD and the Nursing Council.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
District Plann=._  Guidelines
Introduction
1.          The Family We-lfare jrogram is a centrally sponsored scheme,
implemented by the States within the framework of fairly rigid guidelines or
norms developed by the Center. During the Seventh Five-Year Plan period,
there was growing concern that the centralized, standardized nature of the
scheme gave local program managers insufficient flexibility to meet varying
local needs. There was also increasing awareness that variation within
States in terms of language, social and religious groupings, distances between
settlements, access to services, and health and nutrition status, is as
significant as variation between states, given the size and diversity of
India. There is now a consensus that the family welfare program should as far
as possible be tailored to meet the needs of individual districts, and
therefore that the district should be seen as the basic 'unit of management'
for the proposed project.
2.          A second, related concern was that the target-setting process for
the program, especially for family planning acceptors, was too top-down in
nature. Performance targets imposed from above, and not sufficiently related
to local capacities and constraints, had three main adverse effects: first,
staff motivation suffered where targets were unrealistically difficult or too
easy to achieve, rather than 'challenging but attainable'; second, in many
areas, 'target-chasing' became more important than providing high quality
care; and third, in some areas, performance figures were rigged. Under UIP, a
more participative approach to target-setting was developed, and there is a
growing consensus that a greater involvement of local managers in setting
realistic targets for all interventions is likely to improve not only worker
motivation and program quality, but also program coverage.
3.          Therefore, under the proposed project, MOHFW intends to adopt a
district-based planning system, which will allow local managers more
flexibility in designing operational strategies to meet local needs, and
participation in setting their own performance goals for each of the key
interventions   This annex outlines the process which will be followed to
develop distr t plans, and the basic principles or 'rules of the game' which
States and d: -ricts will follow.
The Planning Procesb
4.          Guidelines for district planning will be developed by MOHFW by
September, 1991 on the basis of trial planning efforts in one or two States.
The process being experimented with begins with a State-level workshop in
which State and District managers are briefed on the content of the proposed
UIP Plus/Safe Motherhood program, and in which a first draft of a series of
district plans is drawn up. A subsequent workshop is designed to refine these
plans, and to synthesize the lessons of this initial planning process in a set
of planning guidelines, to which would be attached a sample district plan to
illustrate the required format (but not to serve as a standard model for



- 184 -
ANNEX 28
Page 2
content). Once finalized, these guidelines will be circulated to all
districts participating in the proposed project. Preparation of a
satisfactory district plan will be a precondition for any district to have
access to project financing.
PlanninR Principles
5.          While the deta,led guidelines for the planning process are under
preparation, it is understood that district managers will plan according to the
following basic principles.
6.          Continuous process.  The planning process will be an annual one,
rather than a single exercise undertaken at the beginning of the project. Each
year, districts will review progress made the previous year, d2velop specific
plans to improve performance during the subsequent year, and develop targets for
the major project interventions based on what district managers believe to be
possible.  Draft plans will be discussed with State authorities, modified as
necessary, and adopted as agreed. The planning process should therefore take
place in September and October of each year. This will be late enough in the year
for performance figures for the previous financial year -o be available, and yet
early enough for the financial implications of the proposed plans to be fed into
the annual central planning/budgeting process, which is normally completed in
December of each year.
7.          Vertical and Horizontal Cooperation.  The planning process will be
initiated by district-level staff, working with the health staff representatives
of each block in the district. The district plan will be built up from estimates
by each block of what they can achieve, based on their staff and other resource
availabilities, and the particular advantages and constraints of their areas.
The planning process will also be a joint effort with other agencies in the
district whose work affects public health. From the beginning, the Women and
Child Development department (or its local equivalent in the particular State),
will be involved in the process, because of the importance of the ICDS scheme to
achieving program goals (see Annex 27). Other departments/agencies would be
associated later in the process, such as health units of railways, defence and
public sector undertakings and private sector %.orporations; private voluntary
agencies; associations of private practitioners; and representatives of
traditional medical systems.
8.          Intra-District Variations.  Just as districts vary substantially
from each other, so also may PHC and Sub-Center areas within districts,
depending on their geographic or population characteristics, or local resource
availabilities. Plans will therefore be expected to allow for this variation,
by differentiating strategies and targets by block or groups of blocks.
Appropriate responses may to be to concentrate resources in particular areas
of the district, for example, to concentrate certain IEC activities in
resistant areas, or supervision resources on poor performing blocks; or to
develop distinct service delivery strategies, for example for referral in
hard-to-reach parts of a district.
9.          Special Strategies.  To respond to particularly difficult local
conditions, in districts as a whole or in parts of districts, district
managers will be free to propose special strategies or programs. These may
take the form of a reallocation of resources under their control, in which



- 185 -
ANNEX 28
Page 3
case they can be implemented without outside sanction. But under the proposed
district planning process, managers will mlso be free to propose new
strategies which require additional resources or changes in established
procedures, and hence sanction from the State or Center. Such strategies
might include, for example, proposals for additional transport or supervisory
posts for hard to reach areas; or special demand creation activities, such as
development of special IEC materials in local languages. Such strategies
should be justified in terms of special local constraints, and in terms of the
likely benefits, quantified if possible, of the proposed additional
expenditures.
10.         Such proposals would be sanctioned, or turned down at the time of
the annual review of district plans, by the State or center as appropriate.
States will be given the discretion to sanction additional amounts for
districts up to a specific limit per district, after which central approval
would be required. MOHFW will decide on the size of these ceilings, and
develop illustrative guidelines for eligibility of proposals by December 1991.
11.         Target-setting.  Each district will agree to tentative performance
targets for each PHC based on the planning discussions described above, and
will amalgamate these targets into proposed performance targets for the
district as a whole. Where these targets differ significantly, either
positively or negatively, from 'norm' performance targets established by the
Center per average unit of population, districts should be prepared to explain
the differences. The final targets agreed for the district should be the
product of discussion between State and Center, based on a realistic appraisal
of assets and constraints in the area, rather than a negotiated agreement.

Page 1

Key Indicators
1.          Direct assessment of program effectiveness in achieving program
goals of reducing maternal and child morbidity and mortality will be
difficult. Particularly in rural India, vital events are not reliably
recorded. State-level estimates of infant and young child mortality rates
will continue to be available from the Sample Registration System (SRS). With
this exception, mortality and morbidity estimates will be dependent on
expanded roverage evaluation surveys and on special studies skich as the
excellent maternal mortality study carried out by the Indian Institute of
Management Bangalore in Anantapur district of Andhra Pradesh in 1984.
2.          Program progress can also be monitored through a number of more
accessible process indicators, as indicated below. Many of these indicators
will take two forms: (a) quantity of services delivered; and (b) coverage
level achieved. Quantity of services delivered is or will be available from
service statistics, including reports from PHCs/CHCs and reports from both
public and private sector hospitals on child survival and safe motherhood
services provided.
3.          Coverage estimates will be available from coverage evaluation
studies, contraceptive prevalence surveys and prevalence calculations based on
service statistics, special studies, and to the extent available aggregated
coverage statistics based on field-level recording. In the case of those
indicators referring to the treatment of diseases (ARI, diarrhoeal disease),
coverage will be calculated using the number of cases treated from service
statistics as the numerator and either the number of under-ones from the child
register or a demographic estimate multiplied by a standard estimate of
incidence as the denominator.
Process Indicators of Project
A.    Child Survival
(a)   low birth-weight births identified;
(b)   immunization coverage by type and dose;
(c)   ARI cases treated;
(d)   diarrhoeal disease cases treated; and
(e)   Vitamin A prophylaxis.

identified);

(f)   contraceptive prevalence by method.

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Operational Research and Studies
Section lt Operational Research and Studies Undertaken by GOIJUNICEF under
the Universal Immunization Program (UIP)
Introduction
1.          The term operational research (also called operations research or
action research) is used to cover a variety of kinds of research activities.
For example, OR projects can incorporate research designs with careful
controls built in or they can be carried out on a much less formal basis. The
scale can also vary greatly. What they have in common is an action component,
that is, they are used to introduce, test and/or modify strategies,
structures, and activities, as a survey would do. OR projects are also
frequently used to evaluate alternative changes or options in existing
programs.
2.          The immunization program in India is one of the largest in the
world in terms of pregnant women and infants immunized annually; quantities of
vaccines utilized; number of immunization sessions organized over
geographically widespread and socio-economically diverse areas. Climatic
conditions range from sub-tropical to temperate variations in temperatures.
Such conditions throw up a variety of problems for which solutions must be
found leading to needs-based operational research. The vast experience and
data collected can be used to reach relevant conclusions on program-related
issues and by program managers on the field utility of some of the equipment
currently being marketed for the immunization program. Vaccine-efficacy and
low _; 4s of vaccine-associated adverse events can be documented.
3.          Since operational research is generally carried out in the field
by personnel directly involved in the program and is need-based, the results
are more readily applied for improving management of the program. The
Immunization Program in India has given priority to such activities. Medical
Colleges and national-level institutes are involved into the various
activities of the immunization program, including operational research.
Current Status of Research Activities
4.          Vaccination Coverage Evaluation Surveys are an important
management tool in assessing program achievement, determining reasons for
failure to immunize and a better understanding of the sources of immunization
in the community. The surveys also help to assess the accuracy of data
through service statistics. Information on important indicators of antenatal
and perinatal care are also obtained. 870 surveys have been conducted.
5.          Disease Surveys.  Large scale sample surveys for lameness and
neonatal deaths were conducted in 1981 and 1982 in urban and rural areas of
all major States to collect baselire data on poliomyelitis and neonatal
tetanus. Immunization coverage levels during this period were negligible.



- 189 -
ANNEX 30
Page 2
Surveys on the same scale have not been repeated.  However, lame-sss and
neonatal tetanus surveys are being combined with vaccination coverage surveys
in selected districts.
6.          Surveillance and Documentation of Disease Trends.  Additional
funds have been provided to selected Me4ical Colleges for epidemiological
analysis of data on vaccine preventable 2iaeases of cases treated at the
medical college hospitals. Use of the surveillance data from the medical
colleges and other large hospitals in the country has been utilized to
document declining trends of poliomyelitis and neonatal tetanus.
7.          Line listing of cases of poliomyelitis was started in 1989 to
check for duplication and spot-mapping of cases for identification of high-
risk areas. Line listing of neonatal tetanus cases was started from 1991. In
addition to making available data for epidemiological analysis, line lists are
useful to study the degree of duplication and identification of high riok
pockets. Follow-up of 'low-risk' patients and a typical case study has
started to study the reliability of clinical diagnosis in busy OPD's anu by
general physicians with little experience of poliomyelitis.
8.          Active surveillance has also been started in selected districts
with low incidence so that new or no cases are missed. The observations would
also be useful in determining the degree of under-reporting through passive
surveillance in a given area. However, additional support in terms of better
medical care and physiotherapy facilities would be required to make active
surveillance sustainable.
9.          Poliovirus Isolation.  The isolation of polioviruses is essential
to reach the goal-of eradication of poliomyelitis. Although, India is still
experiencing endemic poliomylitis, it is necessary that the facilities be
developed at this stage because of the large size of the country, the
logistics of collection, storage and transportation of faecal samples is
complex and would require time for its establishment.
10.         The country has at least 6 laboratories which have the capacity
and technical expertise to conduct these tests. Guidelines for the collection
and transportation of samples have been issued. A meeting of the concerned
laboratories was organized in 1990 to ensure uniformity in methodological and
interpretation of results. Samples have started flowing in to all the
laboratories but the numbers are limited. Although the laboratories in the
network have adequate experience, a limiting factor so far, has been the
recurrent shortage of supplies. As the samples are contaminated material it
is also essential that equipment is not shared with other "sterile, laboratory
work. As the workload increases additional equipment will need to be provided
to the laboratories for the establishment of a separate poliovirus isolation
unit. Additional funding will also be required for developing the logistics
of collection, storage and transportation of samples from the treatment
facilities to the laboratories.
11.         Samples from clinically diagnosed cases in Bombay have been tested
by ERC, Bombay over the last decade. There has been a change in the pattern
of isolates; the proportion of Type I isolate is declining and an increase in
the rate of non-polio enteroviruses has been observed over the last three



- 190 -
ANNEX 30
Page 3
years. This period corresponds with declining trend of poliomyelitii in
Bombay.
12.         Vaccine Efficacy is an important measure of the quality of an
immunization program. Moreover, as immunization coverage levels increase, the
proportion of cases with history of immunization also increases. This can
often cause alarm among pediatricians and others responsible for the care of
the child. The situation is aggravated if there is an alternative, more
expensive vaccine available. Program managers have therefore been encouraged
to assess their data for vaccine efficacy as a management tool, especially if
there are an: doubts or complaints have been received. A paper on 'A Simple
Screening Method for Field Evaluation of Vaccine Efficacy, has been widely
circulated.
13.         A study on the efficacy of oral polio vaccine (OPV) in Bombay has
been completed and it concluded that (a) the efficacy of OPV was high and (b)
the relatively high incidence of poliomyelitis was due to a failure to fully
immunize all eligible children. Another study using the same methodology
showed similar results ia Delhi.
14.         Use of OPV for Polio Control.  OPV is the vaccine of choice in
India for the control of poliomyelitis. The vaccine is known to be effective
in many countries of the world which have reached high levels of immunization
coverage. The obvious advantages of OPV are its ease of administration, herd
immunity and low cost. The vaccine is socially acceptable and there is a high
demand. India is self-sufficient in the production of DPT. The indigenous DPT
vaccine is not compatible with IPV.
15.         OPV vaccine was introduced in the national immunization program at
the end of 1979. Up to 1986, the overall reported immunization coverage with
OPV3 was less that 50? of the estimated infants in the country. By 1989, the
estimated coverage was around 75Z with a wide range of performance in the
States and the districts. Despite high priority on surveillance, increased
awareness in the community and better accessibility to health care facilities,
there has been a declining trend of poliomyelitis since 1989. The decline is
commensurate with immunization coverage levels. The reported incidence of
poliomyelitis has declined from more than 32,000 cases in 1981 to 13,866 cases
in 1989 and 8,014 in 1990. During the first quarter of 1991, the number of
reported cases are less than half the numbers in the corresponding period in
1990. The decline has been steeper and over a longer perlod in many States
and a large number of districts. The surveillance data from ten States and
Union Territories with a combined population of more than 250 million is
monitored separately as these States have a better health infrastructure,
higher immunization coverage levels and fairly reliable surveillance systems.
16.         During the initial years of the program, attention was largely
focused on strengthening health infrastructure, improving logistics, and the
development of training material to ensure quality and sustainability.
Coverage levels in the first half of the 1980's were low to moderate. Since
1985, with selective focus on individual districts and heavy inputs in the
Program, increases in immunization coverage levels has been more rapid. Any
impact in the early years of the Program was negated by improvements in
surveillance activities.



- 191 -
ANNEX 30
Page 4
17.         However, due to doubts raised by some scientists and ceotroversies
on the use of oral and killed polio vaccines, a project for the field use of
IPV was taken up in 2 health unit districts with an approximate population of
2.5 million. Since problems in field use and high costs is a major concern is
large and developing countries. the study was primarily focussed on
operational aspects related to field use of IPV and additional investments
that would be required in terms of manpower, training, health education of
community, supervision and supplies. The results of the study are likely to
be available by 1991. The study is being conducted by the (ICMR) Indian
Council of Hedical Research.
18.         Monitoring of the Quality Control for Vaccines. Samples of OPV are
lifted regularly from different levels of the coid chain system and tested for
potency as one of the measures to monitor the effectiveness of the cold chain.
These tests were started in 1984. 932 of the 8100 samples tested in 1990 had
more than the minimum acceptable titre at the time of testing. If any sample
is found to be unsatisfactory, the reasons for the deterioration in the
quality of the vaccine are quickly identified and specific follow-up measures
are taken to avoid recurrance in the future.
19.         Adverse Events following Immunization (AEFI'S).  The use of
several hundred million doses of various vaccines over the last decade, had
documented the safety of the vaccines used under the Immunization Program.
However, since no vaccine is 10OZ effective, none is entirely without risk.
As immunization coverage levels increase, the incidence of vaccine preventable
diseases and their complications decline and surveillance systems and
communication networks improve, the perceived relative importance of adverse
events can increase and influence public 8'- Atance of immunization services.
The monitoring of severe adverse events fo- jwing immunization was started in
India in 1985.  The main objectives were to identify programmatic errors, if
any, so that preventive measures are taken to avoid recurrence in future, to
allay public fear and to assure the community and medical professionals of the
low risk of inherent vaccine-associated severe adverse events.
20.         State and regional expert teams, with an epidemiologist, a
pediatrician and a microbiologist have been constituted for the investigation
of severe adverse events. Medical college professors have been associated
with most of the teams. Formats for the investigation of the adverse events
have been circulated through government circulars and printed documents.
Reports and technical papers on the subject have been published for
dissemination of information.
21.         Life time Cost of Refrigeration and Cold Rooms.  Heavy investments
have been made in the cold chain. A comprehensive review of program
operations in the districts was developed for mid-term evaluation. The
objective of the review was to document achievements as well as to identify
weaknesses and problems that might require corrective action or additional
support. The review wcre conducted by the Ministry of Health & Family Welfare
in 1987 & 1988 in 7 of the 30 districts taken up in the first year of UIP. A
national level review in 48 districts commissioned by the Ministry of Health &
Family Welfare in 1989. The experience gained had enabled some of the States
to take the initiative of conducting similar studies in selected districts
themselves.

Section 2: Indicative List of Operational Research Topics
22.         The project will first of all make use of existing, related
operational research under the Bank-assisted Population and Nutritlon (TNINP
and ICDS) Projects in the areas of improved implementation of the family
welfare program with emphasis on a shift to temporary contraceptive methods
and MCH services, and health and nutrition education for adolescent girls,
respectively. Under the project this research will be expanded to States not
covered under the Population and ICDS projects. In addition, operational
research projects will be developed in the following project areas:
(a)   Program management.  Testing of composition and functions of
proposed district resource teams.
(b)   District planing.  Options for developing successful district
planning mechanisms.
(c)   Persistent diarrhoea.  Alternatives for identification and
treatment of persistent diarrhoea.
(d)   Acute respiratory infections (ARI).  Testing of an alternative for
home delivery of cotrimoxazole and referral of severe cases.
Se)   Low birth-weight babies CLEBW.  TestinS of current assumptions
concerning measures to improve survival of LBW babies and those
with birth injuries, and to prevent asphyxia;
(f)   Anaemia prophylaxis.  Evaluation of a series of related issues
including reasons underlying and ways to overcome compliance
problems assuming compliance, effectiveness of varying levels of
dosage; and importance of deparatization;
(g)   Safe Motherhood referral.  Testing the effectiveness of
alternatives for first: referral for emergency cases; and ways to
improve community-based care.
(h)   Field-level interpersonal communication strategies.  Evaluation of
strategies for identifying local demand constraints to acceptance
of MCH services and training field workers to deal with them.
(i)   Social Marketing of ORS.  Evaluation of (i)a mix of commercial
advertising and marketing with health functioning outreach (PSS
approach) vs (ii) predominance use of commercial advertising and
marketing strategies (PSI approach).
Section 3: Special Safe Motherhood Evaluation Studies
23.         Particularly in the initial phases of the introduction of the Safe
Motherhood program, special evaluation s udies will be necessary to gain
insights into the functioning and effectit;eness of the program, identify
bottlenecks and constraints, and assess cost-effectiveness implications of the
program's various components. Research institutions with the capacity to
carry out such studies will have to be identified.



- 193 -
ANNEX 30
Papg 6
24.         In order to provide a baseline, such studies would have to be
carried out imaediatel, in selected districts where the SM program is to be
introduced. When progcam inputs, e.g. training, equipping and staffing of
CHCs, provision of emergency transportation, etc., have been in place and
functioning for a reasonable time, about a year, a follow-up study can be
carried out.
25.         Since the family welfare program is implemented by the States and
circumstances vary from State to State. it would be desirable for at least one
study to be carried out in every State. Within districts, blocks and
subcenters can be sampled. (The exact construction of the sample would be
determined by technical considerations. Even in rural India a maternal death
is a relatively rare event, which has implications for the size of the sample
needed.)
26.         There are two keys to carrying out these studies successfully.
The first is the identification of all pregnant women within the sample
population and the recording of the outcome of the pregnancy. Female field
workers (ANHs) are supposed to register all prenatal cases. However, it
cannot be assumed that even in the best of circumstances, an ANK will be able
to record all pregnancies, particularly because so many deliveries in India
take place at the home of a women's parents rather than at her own (husband's)
home. It will therefore be necessary for the researchers to identify
pregnancies not seen by the ANH as well. All pregnancies coald then be
classified as without (maternal) mortality or morbidity, with morbidity, and
with mortality. The resulting percentages would provide weights for the
population of pregnant women, and once such weights were established the
morbidity and mortality catefiries could be oversampled for intensive
investigation.
27.         At the village level, female workers (ANHs) must be able to list
antenatal cases and record standard background, history and service data
including results of tests, subsequent antenatal checkups, identification of
risk, referral recommendations, place and outcome of the delivery, by whom
delivered, postnatal care provided, etc. Natal registers for this purpose are
already in use in some States. If it is not possible to provide workers
covered by the studies with printed registers, at a minumum they need to be
given a copy of the format so that they can record the necessary data in blank
registers. It is essential that service data be recording on an on-going
basis in such a fashion that it can be recovered subsequently by researchers,
in order to know which pregnant women are reached by the ANM and what services
she is providing to those whom she does register.
28.         The second key is to follow the experience of individual cases
across levels. Once a woman leaves her village to obtain referral care,
detailed information concerning her case will be available only from those
institutions where she subsequently receives treatment. In cases where a
women in a study population does go outside the village for treatment, the
place, kind of treatment, outcome and transportation utilized would have to be
followed up and recorded. Similarly, the handling of all emergency cases
would have to be followed up.
29.         A set of parallel studies before and after the introduction of
Safe Motherhood inputs in the initial SM districts would provide process data



- 194 -
ANNEX 30
Page 7
on the provision of basic maternal care and the working of the referral system
as well as impact data in terms of reduction of maternal mortality. It would
also provide a basis for assessing the relative importance of various inputs
(training, high-risk identification, availability of staff and/or equipment,
availability of emergency transportation, etc.) in preventing maternal deaths.
(Obtaining useful data on morbidity reduction is more problematic.) In
addition, by estimating costs for the inputs, a cost-effectiveness analysis
can also be carried out.
30.         The desired impacts of the Safe Motherhood program can be measured
in terms of reductions in case fatalities and complications.   An illustrative
list of the interventions necessary to achieve these impacts and a set of
process indicators that could be used to evaluate the achievement of these
process indicators is given below. These indicators can also be analysed in
terms of prevention of pregnancies (no. 1); reduction of pregnancy
complications (nss. 2, 3, 5, 6, 7); and improvement in the treatment of
pregnancy complications (nos. 4, 8, 9).

INDIA
CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAFE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Rationale for Recommended Methods of Procurement
1.          Generally the nor-'al Bank methods and threshoids required for
projects in India will be applicable to this project. Some exceptions are
warranted, however, because of the special nature of vaccine, medicine and
vitamin manufacture and supply.
2.          Vaccinera.  There is an economic reason why it is unlikely that
foreign manufacturers would be interested in entering the Indian market for
these vaccines. There is no "natural" demand for these items. In contrast to
other pharmaceuticals where there is some demand in the private sector, the
vaccines needed for the project, like all preventive medicines, do not combat
illnesses that are considered an immediate threat to life and they are
included only in government programs aimed at improving the general level of
health in the population. Governmentb typically target the poorest level of
society for these programs with the result that, even in the developed world,
prices tend to be controlled and profit ma-gins very slim. Therefore, there
is usually little idle manufacturing capacity available because manufacturers
are not inclined to invest in plant to produce these vaccines when other items
can be more profitable. For this reason and those relating to logistics, '4H0
recommends that governments establish either a local or a regional
manufacturing capability for all vaccines that are on the WHO "essent54al"
list.
3.          Logistics are a key factor in deciding what procurement approach
is appropriate. Use of local sources to the maximum extent is justified by
the need to reduce the risk of loss during transrirt alone. The vaccines
covered by the project must be shipped in a refrigerated state and even a
brief expcsure to heat can either drastically reduce its shelf life or destroy
it completely. (See Table 1 which outlines the stability of various project
vaccines ak different temperatures).  In addition, the Government controls
which are imposed, combined with monitoring by WHO, ensure that the required
quality standards are kept and that the prices charged are reasonably related
to actual cost.
4.          The market situation affecting each vaccine differs, however, and
each has been considered separately by the appraisal mission.
(a)   Oral Polio:  There are three existing local suppliers of oral
polio vaccine and they cannot as yet meet all of MOHFW's needs.
These three firms consist of (i) a Central Government enterprise
(Babcock) which has a collaboration and imports the raw material
it needs in bulk from the Soviet Union; (ii) a S-ate Government
enterprise (Haffkine) which procures its raw material from various
foreign sources selected through competitive bidding; and (iii) a
private sector company (Radicula) which has a foreign



198 -                          ANNEX 32
Page 2
collaboration with and obtains its raw materials from an Italian
firm. The shortfall of less than 10 is covered by imports
purchased by UNICEF's Copenhagen office using their global
competitive bidding procedures and offered to the Ministry on a
grant basis. MOHFW complains of delivery losses with some of
these imports because of failures in maintenance of the "cold
chain." Recomendation: Accept use of the ezisting LCB procedure
regardless of estimated contract value because of the importance
of minimizing the distance between supplier and end-user, with the
exception of any vaccines purchased from Babcock because the raw
material they use comes from an ineligible source.
(b)   DPT and TT:  There are five or six WHO-approved local suppliers
respectively for these vaccines. All have proven that they can
deliver acceptable quality vaccines with a minimum of loss due to
refrigeration problems during delivery. Recommendation: Accept
use of LCB procedures regardless of the estimated contract value
for the same reasons as for the polio vaccine.
tc)   BCG:  There is only one WHO-approved source of BCG supply in
India: BCG Laboratories India, Guindi, and only nire others in the
world. In a recent case where MOHFW asked UNICEF to procure some
BCG on the world market to make up for a drop in production at
Guindi, they found that there is a current shortage of supply
worldwide. Reconendation: Allow procurement of BCG by direct
contracting from BCG Laboratories India because of delivery
problems. In any event, given the world supply situation, ICB
would be unlikely to result in lower costs. Any shortfall in
supply should be made up by international shopping.
(d)   Measles:  There are two strains of measles vaccine in the world,
E-Z and Schwartz. WHO recommends use of E-Z in the third world
because it can be administered to children younger than 9 months
and MOHFW have accepted their recommendation. There are only two
manufacturers of the EZ strain in the world: a Yugoslavian firm
and the Indian firm preferred by MOHFW which has a collaboration
with this Yugoslavian firm. Recomuendations To avoid the same
delivery problems anticipated for oral polio, DPT, TT and BCG,
agree to procurement by direct contracting from the any Indian
manufacturers.
5.          Vitamin A.  IOHFW has opted for ease of administration to young
children and to achieve better overall compliance, to disperse the Vitamin A
covered by the project during clinic visits in liquid form. The most common
alternative, soft gelatin capsules which are generally used in the West are
prone to degradation under the extreme temperatures prevailing during much of
the year in India with consequent loss of patency. Vitamin A in liquid
formulation is not available elsewhere in the world. Recomendation: Agree to
the use of LCB for procurement of Vitamin A from all approved local
manufa.turers.



- 199 -
ANNEX 32
Page 3
6.          Pharmaceuticals.  The only pharm%ceutical of significant value
needed for the project is ORS (Oral Rehydration Salts). It is justified to
procure ORS as close to the end-user as possible to minimize contamination and
loss. It is normally supplied in inexpensive packets that are easily damaged
during shipment or storage. It is readily available from over 50 qualified
local suppliers and customarily procured by the State Governments themselves,
in small quantities, not by the Ministry. Rocoondations  Permit the State
Governments to continue procurement of ORS using their existing LCB
procedures.

ATTACHMENT 1s CONCLUSIONS
1.          In this paper an effort has been made to assess the loss of
potency of various vaccines kept at elevated temperatures. A summary of
available informa?-ion on EPI vaccines is shown in Table 1, where data for
various storage .emperatures are presented and, in Table 2, where the
estimated rat-'t-   degradation of EPI vaccines at 370C are listed.
2.          t1  otability of EPI vaccines varies considerably.  They can be
ranked by ths.  rn,I--ance to storage at 370C from stable toxoids to unstable
reconstituted med1ieL vaccine or oral polio vaccine (Table 16). The data
presented show th-at some vaccines can withstand a long period of exposure
without a significant loss of potency. The high resistance of tetanus toxoid
to heat, for example may warrant studies on using this vaccine without
refrigeration. Tetanus toxoid could retain sufficient potency for short term
exposure to heat when used in the outreach system of delivering immunization
against tetanus to women of childbearing age in areas where cold chain cannot
be maintained.
3.          However, each exposure to elevated temperature results in some
degradation of a vaccine, even if the remaining potency is still above a level
which is considered as the minimal immunizing potency. Furthermore, each
exposure to ambient temperature has a cumulative impact in reducing vaccine
potency: vaccines at peripheral health units will not be able to withstand
the temperatures mentioned in this review if their potency has already been
compromised by previous breaks in the cold chain. Data presented in this
review may provide some guidelines to programme managers and storekeepers at
central and provincial levels in making proper decisions about a vaccine
exposed to elevated temperatures.
4.          All vaccines should be routinely stored at the temperatures
recommended by manufacturers and by national programmes. The cold chain for
vaccines still remains a highly vulnerable point for immunization programmes
in developing countries with tropical climates.   Developed countries with
temperate climates can also have similar problems. In all countries, systems
of refrigeration, temperature monitoring and record keeping are required to
assure that each vial of vaccine is maintained under appropriate conditions
until it has been used, and that it is used before the expiry date shown on
the label.
5.          The area of greatest need for further research is the heat
stability of oral polio vaccine. This is the most sensitive vaccine of the
EPI family and it therefore sets the standard for the cold chain. Any
improvement in this vaccine would have an influence on the cost and
effectiveness of immunization programmes in tropical climates.

The study was initiated and coordinated by Dr. M. Pesez, a member of the WHO
Expert Advisory Panel on The International Pharmacopoeia and Pharmaceutical
Preparations. The World Health Organization gratefully acknowledges the
contribution of Dr. M. Pesez and of Messrs Roussel-Uclaf SA, Romainville,
France which generously offered facilities within their analytical
laboratories to effect the work.
Acknowledgements are also due to rrofessor A.W.M. Indemans,
Director of the Laboratory of the Dutch Pharmaceutical Society, The Hague,
Netherlands, who kindly performed stability studies on some substances subject
to narcotic regulations, and to Mr. B. Ohrner, Director of the WHO's
Collaborating Centre for Chemical Reference Substances, Solna, Sweden who has
corroborated many of the results obtained.1

list of essential drugs.2  Altogether 110 of 296 substances tested were
degraded under the condition employed.

CHILD SURVIVAL AND SAEE MOTHERHOOD PROJECT
Suaervision Plan
I.     Bank Supervision Ingut into Key Activities
Details of the supervision needs for this project are given in the
table below.
In the earlier years, the project will require about 18 staff
weeks a year of supervision, later dropping to 12. Each mission will comprise
about 4.5 staff weeks in the early stages and 3 - 4 staff weeks towards later
stages of the project. The number of missions undertaken per year will be 3
in the early years and 2 later.
The focal points of supervision will be:
(a)   review of the Semi-Annual Progress Reports that will be prepared
by MOHFW. The principal objective of these reports is the
adequate monitoring of project activities.
(b)   review of the Operational Research Program that has been
instituted and related studies that have been undertaken, in order
to determine whether program objectives are being
achieved as planned.
(c)   utilization of key indicators in the assessment of program
effectiveness. Many of these indicators will take two forms:
(1) quantity of services delivered; and (2) coverage
level achieved.
(d)   annual review of plans for the UIP Program, UIP "Plus" Program and
the Safe Motherhood Program. The annual plan for training and
civil works would also be reviewed.
IDA and UNICEF will work closely together in the monitoring and
evaluation process, both at Delhi and at the State level. UNICEF has a
representative in each state to who will assist in project supervision.
National level supervision will clearly not be possible on each
mission. Each mission, however, will visit a sample of states to examine
progress at the field level.

There will be one more mission in 1993 and two each in 1994 and
1995.  While the earlier m;s-lons will concentrate on initiating implementation,
the latter part will concentrate on checking results against key indicators and
dealing with key constrainttr to implementation.
II.   Borrower's Contribution £, Supervision
General
(a)   Overall project monitoring and evaluation, i.e., use of credit
proceeds, achievement of objectives, etc., would be undertaken by
MOHFW with the assistance of other specialized institutions attached
to the MOHFW.
(b)   Progress Reports are to be submittel as f^1lows:
(i) in the form agreed upon;
(ii)  semi-annually; and
(iii) by the Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW), which
will coordinate report preparation by the various project
agencies.
(c)   As part of supervision, GOI has agreed to undertake operational
research to determine whether program objectives are being achieved.



- 213 -
ANNEX 33
Page 5
(d)   GOI will also utilize the key performance indicators satisfactory to
IDA for evaluating the progress of the project and furnish to IDA
annual monitoring reports and end-of-project summative evaluation
reports focussing on the impact of the project.
Particular Areas of Concern
(a)   National and Regional Maternity Mortality Review Committees, with
their specialized power, will assist MOHFW in monitoring and
evaluating the Safe Motherhood Program.
(b)   For the monitoring and evaluation of the civil works portion of the
project, PWDs in the States will be responsible.
(c)   Procurement details and procedures will be monitored by MOHFW
along with UNICEF in the early years and later by MOHFW alone.
